201 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Al-Bihani v. Obama


No. 09-5051


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


590 F.3d 866; 389 U.S. App. D.C. 26; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 102


October 2, 2009, Argued 
January 5, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Ghaleb Nassar Al-Bihani v. Obama, 619 F.3d 1, 393 U.S. App. D.C. 57, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 18169 (2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Al-Bihani v. Obama, 131 S. Ct. 1814, 179 L. Ed. 2d 794, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 2620 (U.S., 2011)
Related proceeding at Khan v. Obama, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 18471 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 6, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:05-cv-01312-RJL).
Parhat v. Gates, 532 F.3d 834, 382 U.S. App. D.C. 233, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 13721 (2008)
Al Bihani v. Obama, 594 F. Supp. 2d 35, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 5713 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner detainee, a Yemeni citizen, had been held at the U.S. naval base detention facility in Guantanamo Bay, Cuba since 2002. He had traveled through Pakistan to Afghanistan eager to defend the Taliban's Islamic state against the Northern Alliance. He was handed over to United States Coalition forces in early 2002. He appealed the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's denial of his petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: The detainee was lawfully detained whether the definition of a detainable person was, as the district court articulated it, an individual who was part of or supporting Taliban or al Qaeda forces, or associated forces that were engaged in hostilities against the United States or its coalition partners, or the modified definition offered by the government that required that an individual substantially support enemy forces. Any person subject to a military commission trial was also subject to detention, and that category of persons included those who are part of forces associated with Al Qaeda or the Taliban or those who purposefully and materially supported such forces in hostilities against U.S. Coalition partners. The facts showed the detainee was part of and supported a group--prior to and after September 11--that was affiliated with Al Qaeda and Taliban forces and engaged in hostilities against a U.S. Coalition partner. He, therefore, fells squarely within the scope of the President's statutory detention powers. In the absence of a determination by the political branches that hostilities in Afghanistan have ceased, the detainee's continued detention was justified.


OUTCOME: The order denying the detainee's petition for a writ of habeas corpus was affirmed.

Act Now to Stop War v. District of Columbia


No. 08-7098


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


589 F.3d 433; 389 U.S. App. D.C. 1; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 27326


November 10, 2009, Argued 
December 15, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part, Claim dismissed by Act Now To Stop War & End Racism Coalition v. District of Columbia, 798 F. Supp. 2d 134, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 79106 (D.D.C., 2011)
Motion granted by Act Now to Stop War and End Racism Coalition v. District of Columbia, 286 F.R.D. 117, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 143568 (D.D.C., 2012)
Stay denied by Act Now To Stop War & Racism Coalition v. District of Columbia, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 151854 (D.D.C., Oct. 23, 2012)
Costs and fees proceeding at, Motion granted by Act Now to Stop War & End Racism Coalition v. D.C., 286 F.R.D. 145, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 165078 (D.D.C., 2012)
Summary judgment granted, in part, summary judgment denied, in part by, Summary judgment denied by Act Now to Stop War & End Racism Coalition v. District of Columbia, 905 F. Supp. 2d 317, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 169202 (D.D.C., 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:07-cv-01495).
Act Now to Stop War & End Racism Coalition v. District of Columbia, 570 F. Supp. 2d 72, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 61233 (D.D.C., 2008)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant antiwar group and religious foundation sued appellee District of Columbia, alleging that regulations governing the placement of posters violated the First Amendment and due process. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed the suit, finding that the foundation lacked standing and that Younger abstention was appropriate as to the group's claims. The foundation and group appealed the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The district court erred in finding that the foundation lacked standing where its executive director's affidavit plainly indicated an intent to engage in conduct violating the 60-day limit for removing posters related to a specific event, the foundation was challenging a regulation that was claimed to burden expressive freedom, and the District had brought an enforcement action against the group for violations of the postering rules, thereby providing an expectation that the government would enforce the law. As for the group's claims, it could have raised constitutional challenges in the instant action that were completely independent of and severable from the violations that it was facing in the District's administrative proceedings. Since it was unclear whether the group had been charged with violating regulations other than the adhesive rule, the group had suggested in oral argument that it was not foreswearing severability, and the case was being already being remanded, it was appropriate to reverse and remand the judgment so that the parties could supplement the record to lay an accurate basis for resolving the Younger abstention issue.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. The case was remanded.

 

203 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Motley


No. 08-3079


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


587 F.3d 1153; 388 U.S. App. D.C. 395; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 26625


October 16, 2009, Submitted 
December 8, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:06-cr-00144-ESH-1).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia, which sentenced him to 120 months of imprisonment for possessing with intent to distribute 50 grams or more of cocaine base, a violation of 21 U.S.C.S. § 841(a)(1) and (b)(1)(A)(iii).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to the drug offense pursuant to a plea agreement that required him to cooperate with law enforcement authorities and required the government to seek a departure pursuant to U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 5K1.1. The government sought the § 5K1.1 departure but did not file a motion pursuant to 18 U.S.C.S. § 3553(e) for a sentence below the mandatory statutory minimum. On appeal, the court held the district court did not have authority to sentence defendant below the statutory minimum absent a motion by the Government under § 3553(e). The Government did not violate due process in failing to file such a motion because the plea agreement did not require it to do so, and its decision was rationally related to legitimate government ends. Specifically, its assessment that defendant's cooperation was sufficient to warrant a significant departure from the Guidelines sentence calculation but not a departure from the mandatory minimum sentence, in light of his drug selling history, was rational. The decision served the legitimate ends of seeking more cooperation and ensuring that the sentence reflected the seriousness of the offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

204 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Ord v. District of Columbia


No. 08-7094


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


587 F.3d 1136; 388 U.S. App. D.C. 378; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 26380


September 18, 2009, Argued 
December 4, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Harris v. District of Columbia, 696 F. Supp. 2d 123, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 26704 (D.D.C., 2010)
Injunction denied by Ord v. District of Columbia, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 144991 (D.D.C., May 10, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-00704).
Ord v. District of Columbia, 573 F. Supp. 2d 88, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 66365 (D.D.C., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Virginia Special Conservator of the Peace (SCOP) authorized to carry weapons within Virginia, sued appellee District of Columbia (D.C.) alleging lack of probable cause for an arrest warrant for allegedly violating D.C. firearms laws. Because he was never arrested, the United States District Court for the District of Columbia found no genuine and imminent risk of prosecution and dismissed for lack of standing. The SCOP appealed.


OVERVIEW: The warrant, the arrests of his company's employees, and allegations that police were determined to drive his company from D.C., all showed a special priority on enforcing the law against him. The SCOP's injury stemmed from his inability to travel to D.C. and carry on his business there while armed without fear of prosecution. That injury was imminent because the District of Columbia has made clear its specific intention to prosecute him. He had U.S. Const. art. III standing. The warrant prevented him from entering D.C. and he had to abandon contracts. The question bore directly on the existence of probable cause: whether the responsible officer, knowing that the SCOP was exempt from D.C. Code § 7-2502.01(a) due to 18 U.S.C.S. § 926B, nonetheless filed an affidavit in bad faith. Even if the allegations did not state a Fourth Amendment claim (a question for the district court), that was no basis to find the claim was so insubstantial as to deprive the district court of jurisdiction. The SCOP may not have been seized in the traditional sense, but the warrant effectively exiled him from D.C., thus restricting his ability to travel and causing him substantial injury.


OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was reversed and the claims were remanded to the district court for further proceedings.

 

205 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Skinner v. United States DOJ


No. 05-5284


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


584 F.3d 1093; 388 U.S. App. D.C. 261; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 23611


September 14, 2009, Argued 
October 27, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Skinner v. DOJ, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 7686 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cv01376).
Skinner v. DOJ, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 12218 (D.D.C., June 17, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff prisoner sued defendant Bureau of Prisons (BOP) under the Privacy Act of 1974, 5 U.S.C.S. § 552a, for damages and amendment of his prison records after he was found guilty of possessing drug paraphernalia and sanctions were imposed that included the loss of 40 days of good-time credits. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed, finding that inmate records were exempt under the Act. The prisoner appealed.


OVERVIEW: Under § 552a(j), the BOP, in 28 C.F.R. § 16.97(a) (1976), exempted its inmate records from § 552a(d)'s amendment provision. Thus, dismissal of the amendment claim was proper. While § 16.97(a) did not exempt the records from § 552a(e)(5)'s duty to accurately maintain records used in any determination, and § 552a(g)'s damages provision provided a remedy for § 552a(e)(5) violations, it was unnecessary to decide if a later regulation exempting the records from § 552a(e)(5) applied, since the damages claim was barred for lack of a successful habeas challenge to the disciplinary hearing on which it was based. The prisoner was challenging the duration of his imprisonment, thus, his sole remedy was a writ of habeas corpus. While under § 552a(g)(4) he alleged willfulness in relying upon inaccurate records to discipline him unfairly for allegedly possessing cocaine, a restoration of good time credits would accelerate release. Thus, he first had to proceed in habeas even though his second claim only sought damages since all of his punishments resulted from the same "Incorrect Information" in his file and from the same finding of guilt at the same hearing on the basis of that information.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of the claim for amendment of the prisoner's records was affirmed. The dismissal of the damages claim was affirmed on the alternative ground that such a claim was not cognizable unless the prisoner first secured relief through a writ of habeas corpus.

 

206 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Emily's List v. FEC


No. 08-5422


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


581 F.3d 1; 388 U.S. App. D.C. 150; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 20768


May 4, 2009, Argued 
September 18, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:05-cv-00049-CKK).
EMILY'S List v. FEC, 569 F. Supp. 2d 18, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 58046 (D.D.C., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff nonprofit entity appealed the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia's judgment rejecting its First Amendment challenge to regulations promulgated by defendant Federal Election Commission (FEC) restricting how non-profits were permitted to spend and raise money to advance their preferred policy positions and candidates. Plaintiff also challenged the regulations under the Federal Election Campaign Act (FECA), 2 U.S.C.S. § 431.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was a hybrid nonprofit that made expenditures for advertisements, get-out-the-vote efforts, and voter registration and also directly contributed to candidates and parties. By passing 11 C.F.R. §§ 106.6(c), (f), 100.57, the FEC did not ban nonprofits from receiving and spending large political donations but dictated that they pay for most election-related activities out of their hard-money accounts. On appeal, the court decided whether the FEC could regulate nonprofit entities that were unconnected to a candidate, party, or for-profit corporation--in other words, whether independent nonprofits were to be treated like individual citizens (who, under Buckley, had the right to spend unlimited money to support their preferred candidates) or like political parties (which, under McConnell, did not have the right to raise and spend unlimited soft money). The court refused to apply the McConnell standard to nonprofits and held that the First Amendment permitted them, like individuals but unlike political parties, to spend unlimited amounts out of their soft-money accounts for election-related activities. Three of the regulations also exceeded the FEC's authority under FECA.


OUTCOME: The court that the FEC regulations contravened the First Amendment and FECA. The court reversed the judgment of the district court and directed it to enter judgment for the nonprofit corporation and to vacate the challenged FEC regulations.

 

207 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Nat'l Ass'n of Mfrs. v. Taylor


No. 08-5085


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


582 F.3d 1; 388 U.S. App. D.C. 190; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 20237; 37 Media L. Rep. 2249


September 12, 2008, Argued 
September 8, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-00208-CKK).
Nat'l Ass'n of Mfrs. v. Taylor, 549 F. Supp. 2d 68, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 31762 (D.D.C., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an industrial trade association, sought judicial review of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's denial of its motion for a judgment on the pleadings in its challenge to the constitutionality of § 207 of the Honest Leadership and Open Government Act of 2007 (HLOGA). The suit contended that 2 U.S.C.S. § 1603(b)(3), as amended by HLOGA § 207, violated the First Amendment and was vague.


OVERVIEW: 2 U.S.C.S. § 1603(b)(3), as amended, satisfied the requirements of strict scrutiny. The statutory language was quite clear. The purpose of the lobbying disclosure statute was to provide greater public information about who was lobbying Congress and what they were lobbying about. The government had a compelling interest in providing the public and its elected representatives with information regarding who was being hired, who was putting up the money, and how much they were spending to influence public officials. Amended § 1603(b)(3) effectively advanced the interest the government proffered in support of it, and it was not fatally underinclusive. Finally, it was narrowly tailored. The association's facial and as-applied First Amendment challenges failed. The risks that the association claimed its members would suffer if their participation in controversial lobbying were revealed were no different from those suffered by any organization that employed or hired lobbyists itself. Amended § 1603(b)(3) was not unconstitutionally vague. Neither the term actively participates nor the term lobbying activities rendered amended § 1603(b)(3)(B) impermissibly vague.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

Comcast Corp. v. FCC


No. 08-1114


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


579 F.3d 1; 388 U.S. App. D.C. 102; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 19483; 37 Media L. Rep. 2281; 48 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 605


April 24, 2009, Argued 
August 28, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of an Order of the Federal Communications Commission.
In re Commission's Cable Horizontal & Vertical Ownership Limits, 23 F.C.C.R. 2134, 2008 FCC LEXIS 1254 (F.C.C., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner cable company and several intervenors involved in the cable television industry petitioned for review of a rule in which the Federal Communications Commission (FCC), exercising its authority pursuant to 47 U.S.C.S. § 533(f)(1), capped at 30% of all subscribers the market share any single cable television operator could serve. Other entities in the broadcast industry intervened in support of the FCC.


OVERVIEW: As an initial matter, the court found that petitioner cable operator had standing based on its statement that it could have seriously pursued a specific but unidentified transaction if the rule had not been in place. Because intervenors supporting the FCC raised the same issues and arguments as the FCC, their standing was of no consequence. As for promulgation of the new rule, in view of the overwhelming evidence concerning the dynamic nature of the communications marketplace and the entry of new competitors at both the programming and the distribution levels, it was arbitrary and capricious for the FCC to conclude that a cable operator serving more than 30% of the market posed a threat either to competition or diversity in programming. The FCC failed to identify a sufficient basis for imposing upon cable operators the special obligations, represented by the 30% subscriber limit. The FCC failed adequately to take account of the substantial competition cable operators faced from non-cable video programming distributors. The FCC failed to heed the court's direction and the same objections to the rationale for the cap were again before the court; thus, vacatur was indicated.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition for review and vacated the subscriber limit.

Cobell v. Salazar


No. 08-5500 Consolidated with 08-5506


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


573 F.3d 808; 387 U.S. App. D.C. 339; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 16666; 39 ELR 20163


May 11, 2009, Argued 
July 24, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari dismissed by Cobell v. Salazar, 130 S. Ct. 3497, 177 L. Ed. 2d 1085, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 5260 (U.S., 2010)
Motion denied by Cobell v. Salazar, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 155041 (D.D.C., June 17, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:96-cv-01285-JR).
In re Cobell, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 23807 (D.C. Cir., Nov. 18, 2008)
Cobell v. Kempthorne, 569 F. Supp. 2d 223, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 59952 (D.D.C., 2008)
Cobell v. Kempthorne, 532 F. Supp. 2d 37, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 6658 (D.D.C., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an interlocutory appeal, appellant Native Americans and cross-appellant, the Secretary of the Interior and federal officials, sought judicial review of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's order's holding the Department of the Interior to be in continuing breach of its duty to account for Native American trust funds, that accounting for the funds was impossible, and awarding monetary relief to the Native Americans.


OVERVIEW: Since passage of the American Indian Trust Fund Management Reform Act of 1994 (1994 Act), Pub. L. No. 103-412, 108 Stat. 4239 (codified as amended at 25 U.S.C.S. § 162a et seq., and 25 U.S.C.S. §§ 4001-4061) and the filing of the present lawsuit, the Department of the Interior (Interior) had mixed success in its efforts to account for Native American trust funds. The district court was incorrect insofar as it assumed that the scope of the accounting obligation could not be adjusted in equity. The Native Americans were entitled to an accounting under 25 U.S.C.S. § 4011(a). The district court's holding of impossibility contradicted the requirement of an equitable accounting--one that made the most efficient use of limited government resources. The overarching aim of the district court should be for Interior to provide the trust beneficiaries the best accounting possible, in a reasonable time, with the money that Congress is willing to appropriate. Interior could use statistical sampling when verifying transactions under $ 500. The court was given guidance on how to handle land escheatments and administrative fees.


OUTCOME: The orders of the district court were vacated, and the case was remanded for further proceedings consistent with the opinion.

 

210 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Mills v. District of Columbia


No. 08-7127


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


571 F.3d 1304; 387 U.S. App. D.C. 221; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 15324


May 8, 2009, Argued 
July 10, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Class certification denied by Mills v. District of Columbia, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 31488 (D.D.C., Mar. 31, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-01061).
Mills v. District of Columbia, 584 F. Supp. 2d 47, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 87825 (D.D.C., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, four motorists, filed a motion for a preliminary injunction to enjoin further implementation of a police checkpoint program in appellee, the District of Columbia. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia denied the motion. This appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: The motorists were District of Columbia citizens who were denied entry at a neighborhood checkpoint on account of their refusal to provide certain information to the police. They asserted that the checkpoints constituted unconstitutional seizures in violation of the Fourth Amendment. The district court held that the motorists did not establish irreparable harm or a likelihood of success on the merits and thus denied their motion for a preliminary injunction. The appellate court disagreed. The reason for stopping each motorist was the possibility, without individualized suspicion, that he or she might have been the potential perpetrator of an undetected, perhaps uncommitted, crime. Since the primary purpose of the checkpoint program was a general interest in crime control, the checkpoints violated the Fourth Amendment. Thus, the motorists' likelihood of success on the merits was strong. They also sufficiently demonstrated irreparable injury, particularly in light of their strong likelihood of success on the merits: the motorists had a right to drive upon the public streets of the District of Columbia or any other city absent a constitutionally sound reason for limiting their access.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was reversed, and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

 

211 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Zivotofsky v. Sec'y of State


No. 07-5347


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


571 F.3d 1227; 387 U.S. App. D.C. 144; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 15282


October 17, 2008, Argued 
July 10, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Zivotofsky v. Sec'y of State, 610 F.3d 84, 391 U.S. App. D.C. 273, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 13225 (2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Brown v. Wilson, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 3528 (E.D. Ky., Jan. 13, 2011)
Vacated by, Remanded by Zivotofsky v. Clinton, 132 S. Ct. 1421, 182 L. Ed. 2d 423, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 2536 (2012)
Vacated by Zivotofsky v. Sec'y of State, 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 11057 (D.C. Cir., May 15, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 03cv01921).
Zivotofsky v. Sec'y of State, 511 F. Supp. 2d 97, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 68849 (D.D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff minor, by his parents, appealed the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's dismissal, under the political question doctrine, of his action seeking to order defendant Secretary of State, under § 214(d) of the Foreign Relations Authorization Act, Fiscal Year 2003, Pub. L. No. 107-228, 116 Stat. 1350 (2002) (codified at 22 U.S.C.S. § 2651 note) to record "Jerusalem, Israel," or "Israel" as his birthplace.


OVERVIEW: While the Act stated that the State Department was to record "Jerusalem, Israel," on passports and Consular Reports of Birth, under its long-standing policy, the State Department identified only "Jerusalem" as the minor's place of birth. Under U.S. Const. art. II, § 3, the President had exclusive and unreviewable constitutional power to keep the United States out of the debate over the status of Jerusalem. Policy decisions made pursuant to that recognition power were nonjusticiable political questions. Every president since 1948 had, as a matter of official policy, purposefully avoided taking a position on the issue whether Israel's sovereignty extended to the city of Jerusalem. The State Department's refusal to record "Israel" in passports and Reports of Birth of U.S. citizens born in Jerusalem implemented that long-standing policy of the Executive. The court would not question the President's exercise of the recognition power. Resolving the claim either at the jurisdictional stage under the political question doctrine or on the merits by striking the Act implicated the recognition power. Only the Executive could define policy on Israel's sovereignty over Jerusalem.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

 

212 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Blalock


No. 08-3020


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


571 F.3d 1282; 387 U.S. App. D.C. 199; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 15320


January 26, 2009, Argued 
July 10, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:07-cr-00157-HHK-1).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pled guilty to unlawful possession of a firearm by a convicted felon under 18 U.S.C.S. § 922(g)(1). The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia found that appellant possessed the gun in connection with his possession with intent to distribute marijuana, applied a four-level enhancement to his sentence under U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 2K2.1(b)(6) (2007), and sentenced him to 57 months in prison. This appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant contended that the district court erred by increasing his offense level under § 2K2.1(b)(6). He objected to the district court's findings that he possessed marijuana with the intent to distribute it in violation of 21 U.S.C.S. § 841(a)(1) and (b)(1)(d), and that he possessed his weapon in connection with the drug offense. With respect to the claim that appellant lacked the specific intent to distribute the marijuana due to PCP intoxication, there was no clear error in the district court's determination that appellant had the requisite intent. Despite his intoxication, appellant was able to operate a motor vehicle, deliberately drive to the police station, retrieve his gun from the trunk, fire the weapon into the air several times, and then remove the magazine and throw down both the gun and magazine when ordered to do so. With respect to the claim that appellant did not use or possess his weapon in connection with the marijuana offense, there was no dispute that his handgun was found in close proximity to drugs, and the only way to reverse the district court's "in connection with" finding under § 2K2.1(b)(6) required disregarding § 2K2.1(b)(6), cmt., application n. 14.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

 

213 of 475 DOCUMENTS


SoundExchange, Inc. v. Librarian of Cong.


No. 08-1078


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


571 F.3d 1220; 387 U.S. App. D.C. 137; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 14954; Copy. L. Rep. (CCH) P29,796; 91 U.S.P.Q.2D (BNA) 1204; 48 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 170


March 24, 2009, Argued 
July 7, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Appeal of an Order of the Copyright Royalty Judges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An order of the Copyright Royalty Judges (CRJ), an agency comprised of members appointed by appellee Librarian of Congress, set the royalty rate that intervenor satellite radio service companies had to pay under 17 U.S.C.S. §§ 114(f)(1)(A), 801(b)(1), to copyright owners under a sound recordings license. Appellant, a royalty distributor, sought review, arguing it was arbitrary, capricious, and not supported by substantial evidence.


OVERVIEW: The CRJ chose an initial rate equal to 6.0 % of revenue, increasing to 8.0 % over the term of the license, but did not to set a separate rate for ephemeral copies, deeming the 17 U.S.C.S. § 112 rate for an ephemeral copy "embodied" in the rate set for the § 114 license. Even if the CRJ had held that 13 %, the rate sought by the distributor, was reasonable for transmitting musical works, the CRJ was under no obligation to choose a rate derived from a market-based approach. The claim that § 801 clearly required using market rates was wrong. The distributor pointed to no credible evidence in the record that any of the types of revenue the order excluded from gross revenues currently constituted or were projected to account for an amount of gross revenues that would significantly impact the calculation. But, the Librarian conceded it was error not to have set a separate rate for the § 112 license to make ephemeral copies (digital copies of a sound recording stored on a computer), and the lack of evidence identified by the CRJ counseled against the court setting any rate. Thus, remand for the CRJ to set an appropriate rate for ephemeral copies was warranted.


OUTCOME: The petition for review was denied with respect to the royalty rate for the use of sound recordings but was granted with respect to the CRJ's failure to set a royalty rate for "ephemeral copies" of sound recordings and the case was remanded for the CRJ to set the royalty rate for ephemeral copies in the first instance.

 

214 of 475 DOCUMENTS


In re Grand Jury Subpoenas


No. 08-3056


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


571 F.3d 1200; 387 U.S. App. D.C. 117; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 15283


January 23, 2009, Argued 
June 23, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:07mc00319).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Movant congressman appealed an order of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia denying the congressman's motion to quash grand jury subpoenas served upon the law firm and upon individual lawyers who represented the congressman before the Ethics Committee of the House of Representatives. The congressman contended that testimony and documents called for were protected by the Speech or Debate Clause.


OVERVIEW: The inquiry of Ethics Committee concerned whether a trip of the congressman was an exercise of his official powers or an abuse of those powers, i.e., a privately sponsored vacation. The government served the subpoenas after the congressman declined to be interviewed as to certain statements made in his responses to the Committee. The district court concluded that his responses to the Committee were made in his personal capacity as a witness and not in his legislative capacity. On review, the court reversed the denial of the motion to quash because the matters at issues were protected by the Speech or Debate Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 6. The congressman's statements were protected because they were directly spurred by the inquiry into whether he had abused his office to obtain a vacation. Therefore, the congressman could not be questioned "in any other place" concerning the matters at issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's order denying the motion to quash and directed the district court to enter an order consistent with the opinion.

216 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Atherton v. D.C. Office of the Mayor


No. 07-5195


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


567 F.3d 672; 386 U.S. App. D.C. 144; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 11734


March 6, 2009, Argued 
June 2, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Atherton v. D.C. Office of the Mayor, 130 S. Ct. 2064, 176 L. Ed. 2d 418, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 2776 (U.S., 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Zachem v. Atherton, 130 S. Ct. 3275, 176 L. Ed. 2d 1209, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 4106 (U.S., 2010)
Motion granted by, Claim dismissed by Atherton v. D.C. Office of the Mayor, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 109068 (D.D.C., Sept. 26, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cv00680).
Atherton v. D.C. Office of the Mayor, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 25394 (D.D.C., Apr. 5, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a District of Columbia resident, filed a pro se complaint against appellees, the District of Columbia, District officials, and federal officials, alleging due process and equal protection violations pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 1983, 1985(3), 1986, and Bivens. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia granted appellees' motions to dismiss the complaint for failure to state a claim. The resident appealed.


OVERVIEW: The resident contended that he was unlawfully removed from grand jury service because of his deliberative judgments and his Hispanic ethnicity. In dismissing the case, the district court concluded that two officials, the District's juror officer and the federal supervising Assistant U.S. Attorney (AUSA) were entitled to absolute immunity from liability on the due process claims raised by the resident. This was error. When the juror officer dismissed the resident from the grand jury, she was performing administrative/managerial functions. The District failed demonstrate that the acts the juror officer performed were quasi-judicial functions that were functionally comparable to those of a judge. Therefore, she was not entitled to absolute immunity. The supervising AUSA's alleged activities--improperly removing a grand juror on the basis of his ethnicity and/or for the content and quality of his deliberations--had nothing to do with a prosecutor's preparation for or participation in a criminal trial. Thus, the supervising AUSA was not entitled to absolute immunity because the activities for which he was being sued did not relate to his performance as an advocate for the Government.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of the due process claims against the juror officer and the supervising AUSA were reversed, and the case was remanded to allow the district court to assess whether those officials were protected by qualified immunity. The dismissal of the remaining claims was affirmed.

United States v. Philip Morris USA, Inc.


No. 06-5267 Consolidated with 06-5268, et al.


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


566 F.3d 1095; 386 U.S. App. D.C. 49; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 11008; 73 Fed. R. Serv. 3d (Callaghan) 896


October 14, 2008, Argued 
May 22, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Tobacco-Free Kids Action Fund v. Philip Morris USA Inc., 130 S. Ct. 3502, 177 L. Ed. 2d 1090, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 5427 (U.S., 2010)

US Supreme Court certiorari denied by R.J. Reynolds Tobacco Co. v. United States, 130 S. Ct. 3501, 177 L. Ed. 2d 1090, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 5455 (U.S., 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by British Am. Tobacco (Invs.) Ltd. v. United States, 130 S. Ct. 3502, 177 L. Ed. 2d 1090, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 5450 (U.S., 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by United States v. Philip Morris USA Inc., 130 S. Ct. 3501, 177 L. Ed. 2d 1090, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 5479 (U.S., 2010)
Later proceeding at United States v. Philip Morris USA Inc., 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 19156 (D.D.C., Feb. 23, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 99cv02496).
United States v. Philip Morris USA, Inc., 449 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 57759 (D.D.C., 2006)



DISPOSITION:  The court affirmed in part, finding the manufacturers liable for RICO infractions but remanding for dismissal of the trade organizations. The court partially affirmed the remedial order, including the denial of additional remedies sought on cross-appeal, but vacated and remanded the order with regard to four issues regarding the applicability and scope of injunctive relief as to one manufacturer, all subsidiaries, and third-party retailers.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant cigarette manufacturers and tobacco trade organizations appealed a judgment from the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia finding them liable under the Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (RICO), 18 U.S.C.S. § 1962(c), (d), for jointly engaging in a pattern of mail and wire fraud. Plaintiff government and intervenor public interest groups cross-appealed from the denial of additional injunctive remedies.


OVERVIEW: Nine cigarette manufacturers and two trade organizations were found liable for joining in a 50-year conspiracy to deceive the American public about the health effects and addictiveness of cigarettes in violation of RICO. On appeal, the court held that the definitions of "persons" and "enterprise" in 18 U.S.C.S. § 1961(3), (4), embraced a mixed group of individuals and corporations associated in fact. A "scheme to defraud" consisting of 108 acts of mail fraud and wire fraud under 18 U.S.C.S. §§ 1341, 1343, based on "a pattern of racketeering activity" on the part of each defendant under 18 U.S.C.S. § 1962(c), with the specific intent to defraud and material falsity, was proved. The manufacturers were properly enjoined from making further misleading advertising statement, but injunctive relief against the trade organizations, which had been dissolved under a previous master settlement agreement, was dismissed as moot. The injunctions were overly broad in several respects. The district court properly rejected the government's proposed remedies of disgorgement; counter-marketing, smoking cessation, and youth smoking reduction plans; and a quasi-judicial monitoring scheme.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, finding the manufacturers liable for RICO infractions but remanding for dismissal of the trade organizations. The court partially affirmed the remedial order, including the denial of additional remedies sought on cross-appeal, but vacated and remanded the order with regard to four issues regarding the applicability and scope of injunctive relief as to one manufacturer, all subsidiaries, and third-party retailers.

 

218 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Laurel Baye Healthcare of Lake Lanier, Inc. v. NLRB


No. 08-1162, Consolidated with 08-1214


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


564 F.3d 469; 385 U.S. App. D.C. 354; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 9419; 186 L.R.R.M. 2417; 157 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P11,233


December 4, 2008, Argued 
May 1, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Laurel Baye Heathcare of Lake Lanier, Inc. v. NLRB, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 16039 (D.C. Cir., July 1, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review and Cross-Application for Enforcement of an Order of the National Labor Relations Board.
Laurel Baye Healthcare of Lake Lanier, LLC, 2008 NLRB LEXIS 57 (Feb. 29, 2008)



DISPOSITION: The court denied the Board's cross-petition for enforcement and remanded the matter for further proceedings before the Board at such time as it might once again consist of sufficient members to constitute a quorum.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a petition for review of an order of respondent National Labor Relations Board finding that petitioner engaged in unlawful labor practices, and imposing a remedy. The Board cross-petitioned for enforcement of the order.


OVERVIEW: Unlike the typical petition for review of an Board order, petitioner did not advance allegations of error in the Board's findings, conclusions, or remedies, but rather challenged the authority of the Board to enter the order at all, as the Board had only two members and therefore did not meet the statutory Board quorum requirement of three members. The Board argued that because the Board itself had earlier delegated all of its authority to a three-member panel of which the two remaining Board members constituted a quorum, that quorum of the delegee panel had the authority to enter the order. The court concluded that the Board's purported order was beyond its lawful authority, so the purported order was without force. Congress provided unequivocally that a quorum of the Board was three members, and that this requirement had to be met at all times, 29 U.S.C.S. § 153(b). The delegee group quorum provision did not eliminate this requirement.


OUTCOME: The court denied the Board's cross-petition for enforcement and remanded the matter for further proceedings before the Board at such time as it might once again consist of sufficient members to constitute a quorum.

Rasul v. Myers


No. 06-5209 Consolidated with 06-5222


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


563 F.3d 527; 385 U.S. App. D.C. 318; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 8980


September 14, 2007, Argued 
April 24, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Rasul v. Myers, 130 S. Ct. 1013, 175 L. Ed. 2d 618, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 8982 (U.S., Dec. 14, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Remand from the United States Supreme Court.
Rasul v. Myers, 512 F.3d 644, 379 U.S. App. D.C. 210, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 509 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States Supreme Court vacated the present court's decision and remanded the case for further consideration in light of the Boumediene decision. In the present case, appellants, four British nationals, who were former detainees at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba sued defendants, 11 federal officials. The complaint contained seven counts.


OVERVIEW: Counts 1-3 invoked federal jurisdiction under the Alien Tort Statute, 28 U.S.C.S. § 1350, and alleged violations of international law. Count 4 alleged violations of unspecified provisions of the Geneva Convention. Counts 5 and 6 asserted Bivens claims for violations of the Fifth and Eighth Amendments to the Constitution, and count 7 alleged a violation of the Religious Freedom Restoration Act (RFRA), 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 2000bb et seq. The detainees did not attempt to show how the Boumediene decision bore on counts 1 to 4. In regards to the remaining three counts, the present court declined to decide if the Boumediene decision portended application of the Due Process Clause and the Cruel and Unusual Punishment Clause to Guantanamo Bay detainees. The officials were entitled to qualified immunity as to counts 5 and 6. The Insular Cases could not have clearly established that constitutional rights extend to aliens held at Guantanamo Bay. As to count 7, the Boumediene could not possibly have altered--retroactively--the meaning of RFRA. The court had previously held that, as a matter of statutory interpretation, the detainees were not protected persons within the meaning of the RFRA.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reinstated, but on a more limited basis. The district court's dismissal of counts 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 and 6 was affirmed, and the district court's denial of the officials' motion to dismiss count 7 was reversed.

Kiyemba v. Obama


No. 05-5487 Consolidated with 05-5489


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


561 F.3d 509; 385 U.S. App. D.C. 198; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 7739


September 25, 2008, Argued 
April 7, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Kiyemba v. Obama, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 29827 (D.C. Cir., July 27, 2009)
Rehearing denied by Kiyemba v. Obama, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 29828 (D.C. Cir., July 27, 2009)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Kiyemba v. Obama, 559 U.S. 1005, 130 S. Ct. 1880, 176 L. Ed. 2d 362, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 2642 (2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 05cv01509), (No. 05cv01602).
Kiyemba v. Obama, 555 F.3d 1022, 384 U.S. App. D.C. 375, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 3013 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated habeas corpus actions, respondent government appealed nine interim orders from the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia requiring the government to provide 30 days' notice to the district court and to counsel before transferring petitioners, nine detainees, from incarceration at a U.S. military base in Guantanamo Bay, Cuba.


OVERVIEW: After the government decided that they were no longer considered to be enemy combatants, the detainees sought to prevent their transfer to any country where they were likely to be subjected to further detention or to torture. On appeal, the court rejected the government's argument that § 7 of the Military Commissions Act (MCA), 28 U.S.C.S. § 2241(e)(1), barred the district court from exercising jurisdiction in the detainees' ongoing habeas cases over ancillary claims such as those related to the detainees' potential transfers. The U.S. Supreme Court's Boumediene rule protected all habeas corpus rights, not just so-called "core" habeas rights. Furthermore, 28 U.S.C.S. § 2241(e)(2) of the MCA did not bar the court from taking jurisdiction, as it did not apply to habeas claims. The Munaf rule precluded the court from issuing a habeas writ to prevent transfers on the grounds asserted by the detainees, i.e., torture, further detention, or prosecution by a recipient country. The district court lacked authority to bar the government from releasing a detainee into the custody of another sovereign merely because that sovereign might prosecute or detain the detainee under its own laws.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the district court's interim orders and reversed.

Hettinga v. United States


No. 07-5403


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


560 F.3d 498; 385 U.S. App. D.C. 168; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 6930; 39 ELR 20077


November 7, 2008, Argued 
April 3, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Dismissed by, On remand at Hettinga v. United States, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 26968 (D.D.C., Mar. 15, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 06cv01637).
Hettinga v. United States, 518 F. Supp. 2d 58, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 54990 (D.D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff farm and dairy owners appealed the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's dismissal of their claim against defendant United States, challenging the constitutionality of the Milk Regulatory Equity Act of 2005's amendments, in 7 U.S.C.S. § 608c, to the Agricultural Marketing Agreement Act (AMAA), 7 U.S.C.S. §§ 601-674. The owners argued exhaustion of administrative remedies was not required.


OVERVIEW: 7 U.S.C.S. § 608c(15)(A)'s mandatory administrative exhaustion requirement did not apply to constitutional challenges to the AMAA itself, as distinct from challenges to regulatory orders and attendant obligations. The owners were not challenging a marketing order for which exhaustion was required; the complaint alleged the MREA, as adopted, contained provisions that singled out and punished the owners, specifically, § 608c(5)(M), (N). The government's suggestion that the amendments did not subject formerly exempt producer-handlers to such obligations ignored the inexorable consequences of the amendments. It was not shown that to become effective, the MREA required a marketing order. The question of whether exhaustion was jurisdictional or mandatory did not have to be reached because it was clear the AMAA did not create a jurisdictional exhaustion requirement for challenges to the AMAA itself. Because the owners' objections did not involve an alleged defect in a marketing order and the Secretary of Agriculture lacked the power to provide a remedy, requiring exhaustion as a prudential matter would not protect agency authority or advance judicial efficiency.


OUTCOME: The district court's dismissal was reversed.

Khaled A.F. Al Odah v. United States


No. 05-5117 Consolidated with 05-5120, 05-5121,05-5123, 05-5124, 05-5125, 05-5126, 05-5127


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


559 F.3d 539; 385 U.S. App. D.C. 110; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 4538


November 7, 2008, Argued 
March 6, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 02cv00828).
In re Guantanamo Detainee Cases, 355 F. Supp. 2d 443, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 1236 (D.D.C., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated habeas case, appellant United States (government) sought judicial review of a 2005 order by the United States District Court for the District of Columbia compelling disclosure of certain classified information to counsel for appellees, certain detainees held at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba. The government appealed from the order granting defense counsel access to the redacted information.


OVERVIEW: Both the government and the detainees urged the court to rely, by analogy, on the procedures applicable to criminal proceedings and to appellate review under the Detainee Treatment Act of 2005, Pub. L. No. 109-148, 119 Stat. 2680 (2005). They disagreed as to how the court should apply those procedures. The district court's order simply stated that the classified information was relevant. Although the district court may well have considered materiality as an implicit part of the relevance analysis, the order did not indicate whether that was the case. Absent such an indication and an explanation of the district court's reasoning, the appellate court could not conclude that the redacted information was in fact material. The appellate court was unable to determine whether the district court found that the redacted classified information was material to the detainees' cases and necessary to facilitate meaningful habeas review. Nor could the appellate court determine whether the district court found that alternatives to disclosure were insufficient. The district court had to state its individualized determinations on the record in order to enable informed appellate review.


OUTCOME: The district court's order compelling disclosure of certain classified information to counsel for certain detainees held at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba was vacated, and the cases were remanded so that the district court could consider the findings required before a discovery order could issue.

Am. Farm Bureau Fedn v. EPA


No. 06-1410 Consolidated with 06-1411, 06-1415, 06-1416, 06-1417


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


559 F.3d 512; 385 U.S. App. D.C. 83; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 3562; 39 ELR 20042; 68 ERC (BNA) 1417


September 15, 2008, Argued 
February 24, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of an Order of the Environmental Protection Agency.
Am. Farm Bureau Fed'n v. EPA, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 18262 (D.C. Cir., July 31, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, petitioners, various state agencies, environmental groups, and industry groups, joined by several intervenors and amici, sought review of an order of respondent Environmental Protection Agency (EPA). The associations challenged the EPA's 2006 revisions to its national ambient air quality standards (NAAQS) for fine and coarse particulate matter (PM) promulgated under the Clean Air Act, 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 7401-7671p.


OVERVIEW: On review of the EPA's 2006 national clean air standard revisions, the court held that the EPA failed to adequately to explain why--in view of the risks of short-term exposure to fine PM and the morbidity resulting from long-term exposure to fine PM--the annual standard adequately protected public health under 42 U.S.C.S. § 7409(b)(1). The EPA failed to explain (1) its reason for not accepting the Clean Air Scientific Advisory Committee's recommendations that short-term air quality studies were relevant in setting new national standards; (2) why its daily standard shielded the public from risks associated with short-term exposure; and (3) how sub-populations of children and vulnerable adults were protected. Otherwise, the EPA reasonably analyzed the risks associated with fine PM. However, the EPA's decision to set secondary fine PM NAAQS identical to primary NAAQS was contrary to the requirements of 42 U.S.C.S. § 7409(b)(2). With regard to coarse PM, the EPA reasonably retained a daily standard for urban and nonurban areas. The daily NAAQS and indicators for coarse PM adequately protected public health. The EPA reasonably decided that an annual coarse PM standard was unnecessary.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petitions for review in part and denied them in part. The court remanded the national standards for fine PM to the EPA for further proceedings. The court denied the petitions for review with regard to the EPA's standards for coarse PM, finding that such standards were not arbitrary, capricious, or otherwise contrary to law.

Nat'l Cable & Telecomms. Ass'n v. FCC


No. 07-1312


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


555 F.3d 996; 384 U.S. App. D.C. 349; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 2828; 47 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 301


September 10, 2008, Argued 
February 13, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: , , 22 F.C.C.R. 6927, 2007 FCC LEXIS 2679


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a petition for review of an order of respondent Federal Communications Commission (FCC), arguing that a 2007 order violated the First Amendment or was arbitrary in violation of the Administrative Procedure Act (APA).


OVERVIEW: In its 2007 order the FCC changed, for the third time, its requirements for the form of customer approval necessary to satisfy 47 U.S.C.S. § 222. By conceding the constitutionality of § 222, petitioners necessarily conceded at least two factual predicates underlying both the statute and the FCC's Order--namely, that the government had a substantial interest in protecting the privacy of customer information and that requiring customer approval advanced that interest. Also, the privacy of customer information could not be preserved unless there were restrictions on the carrier's disclosure of it. The FCC reasonably concluded that an opt-in consent requirement directly and materially advanced the interests in protecting customer privacy and in ensuring customer control over the information. The evidence supported the FCC's decision to prefer opt-in consent. The evidence showed that customers were less willing to have their information shared with third parties as opposed to affiliated entities. The FCC reasonably concluded that customer information would be at a greater risk of disclosure once out of the control of the carriers and in the hands of entities not subject to § 222.


OUTCOME: The petition was denied.

 

226 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. McCants


No. 06-3161


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


554 F.3d 155; 384 U.S. App. D.C. 292; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 2399


October 21, 2008, Argued 
February 10, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 02cr00130-01).
United States v. McCants, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 78409 (D.D.C., Oct. 27, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the sentence imposed by the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, following his entry to a guilty plea to the possession of false document-making implements in violation of 18 U.S.C.S. § 1028(a)(5) on three specific dates. He asserted that the district court improperly calculated his sentencing range by misapplying U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 2B1.1.


OVERVIEW: The defendant produced false identifications and counterfeit documents that were used by others to bilk several banks out of more than $ 110,000. The plea agreement stipulated that U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 2B1.1 applied to the offense. Defendant conceded two two-level increases to his sentence because his offense involved more than ten but fewer than fifty victims, and because the offense involved the possession of device-making equipment. He opposed the government's assertion that his relevant conduct included the bank fraud conducted by others. If the bank fraud was conduct relevant for his admitted offense, the loss he caused would rise to between $ 200,000 and $ 400,000, and his offense level was increased by twelve levels. He also challenged a finding that his offense involved the use of sophisticated means, another two-level enhancement under U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 2B1.1(b)(8)(C). On appeal, the court affirmed as to the latter enhancement, but not the former. The bank fraud was not temporally tied by the prosecution to the defendant's admitted possession of the criminal tools.


OUTCOME: The sentence was vacated and remanded for resentencing, because the district court erred by including as relevant conduct a bank fraud the government failed to show had the requisite connection to defendant's admitted criminal acts. The two-level enhancement for use of sophisticated means was affirmed.

Southwest Airlines Co. v. Transp. Sec. Admin.


No. 07-1279 Consolidated with 07-1280, 07-1281, 07-1282, 07-1283, 07-1284, 07-1285, 07-1286, 07-1287, 07-1288, 07-1289, 07-1290, 07-1291, 07-1292, 07-1293, 07-1294, 07-1296, 07-1297, 07-1298, 07-1323, 07-1338, 07-1347


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


554 F.3d 1065; 384 U.S. App. D.C. 325; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 1884


November 6, 2008, Argued 
February 3, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified and rehearing denied by Southwest Airlines Co. v. Transp. Sec. Admin., 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 29759 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 10, 2009)
Decision reached on appeal by Southwest Airlines Co. v. Transp. Sec. Admin., 650 F.3d 752, 397 U.S. App. D.C. 68, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 13392 (2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of a Final Order of the Transportation Security Administration.
Southwest Airlines Co. v. Transp. Sec. Admin., 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 28409 (D.C. Cir., Dec. 5, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner airlines filed a petition for review of a final order of the Transportation Security Administration (TSA), arguing that TSA erroneously overcharged them for their statutory portion of security costs. The airlines challenged TSA's final decisions, claiming that they reflected substantive statutory violations, were arbitrary and capricious, and were flawed procedurally.


OVERVIEW: The court found merit in the attack on TSA's understanding of the Aviation and Transportation Security Act's (ATSA), Pub. L. No. 107-71, 115 Stat. 625 (2001), "overall" limit, but not in the other objections. As to the "overall limit," the airlines argued that TSA violated the plain language of 49 U.S.C.S. § 44940(a)(2)(B)(i) by basing its calculation of the fees on a Government Accountability Office estimate which had included the costs of screening non-passengers, such as "meeters-and-greeters" and sightseers. The court concluded that the phrase "screening passengers" had a widely understood meaning: it referred to the process of searching airline passengers at an airport security checkpoint, not to the entire set of activities undertaken to promote passenger safety. As to "per-carrier limit," however, although the airlines might have preferred that TSA rely on their Appendix A information, TSA was entitled to conclude that, in the absence of an audit, such data were not reliable enough. TSA was also free to select a reasonable alternative, such as the industry's average cost.


OUTCOME: TSA erred in its interpretation of the ATSA's overall limit and in its classification of the audit opinion, so the court remanded for certain modifications. The court otherwise affirmed TSA's decision.

 

228 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Bismullah v. Gates


No. 06-1197, No. 07-1508, No. 07-1523


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


551 F.3d 1068; 384 U.S. App. D.C. 145; 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 334


November 20, 2008, Argued 
January 9, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Rehearing.
Gates v. Bismullah, 128 S. Ct. 2960, 171 L. Ed. 2d 881, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 5081 (U.S., 2008)



DISPOSITION: Dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, military detainees, sought review pursuant to § 1005(e)(2) of the Detainee Treatment Act (DTA), 10 U.S.C.S. § 801 note, of a determination by a Combatant Status Review Tribunal that the detainees were enemy combatants. The court reinstated its decision establishing DTA review procedures. On a petition for rehearing, respondent, the Secretary of Defense, contended that the court lacked subject matter jurisdiction over the petitions.


OVERVIEW: The rehearing was granted to determine whether the court retained jurisdiction after the U.S. Supreme Court had found unconstitutional § 7 of the Military Commission Act of 2006 (formerly DTA § 1005(e)(1)), codified at 28 U.S.C.S. § 2241(e), which abolished habeas jurisdiction for Guantanamo detainees. The Secretary contended that because that provision was unconstitutional, DTA § 1005(e)(2) also had to fall. The court dismissed the petitions for lack of jurisdiction because the availability of judicial review pursuant to DTA § 1005(e)(2) as a supplement to habeas relief was not consistent with the basic objective of Congress when it enacted the DTA judicial review provision. Congress had understood that review under § 1005(e)(2) was to be a substitute for and not a supplement to habeas corpus. The court was confident that Congress would not have enacted § 1005(e)(2) in the absence of the provision banning courts from exercising jurisdiction over a detainee's habeas petition. Therefore, because the latter provision was held unconstitutional and the two provisions were not severable, the judicial review provision of § 1005(e)(2) also failed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the detainees' petitions.

 

229 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Kaemmerling v. Lappin


No. 07-5065


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


553 F.3d 669; 384 U.S. App. D.C. 240; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 26507


September 8, 2008, Argued 
December 30, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 06cv01389).
Kaemmerling v. Lappin, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 30184 (D.C. Cir., Dec. 28, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant federal inmate sought judicial review of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's dismissal of his case alleging that the DNA Analysis Backlog Elimination Act of 2000 (DNA Act), 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 14135-14135e, violated his rights under the Religious Freedom Restoration Act (RFRA), 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 2000bb-2000bb-4, and the First, Fourth, and Fifth Amendments of the U.S. Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The district court incorrectly dismissed the case for failure to exhaust administrative remedies under the Prison Litigation Reform Act (PLRA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 1997e because there was no administrative process to exhaust as the U.S. Bureau of Prisons lacked authority to provide the inmate any relief or to take any action whatsoever in response to his complaint challenging enforcement of the DNA Act. The inmate's Free Exercise Clause claim failed. He was not relieved of the obligation to comply with a valid and neutral law of general applicability on the ground that the law proscribed or prescribed conduct that his religion prescribed or proscribed. His RFRA claim failed. He did not allege any religious observance that the DNA Act impeded, or acts in violation of his religious beliefs that it pressured him to perform. His status as a first-time offender of a non-violent crime did not lessen the government's interest in collecting his DNA. The DNA Act did not violate the equal protection component of the Due Process Clause of the Fifth Amendment, the right against self-incrimination, or the Fourth Amendment right to be free from unreasonable searches and seizures.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

 

230 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Adams v. FAA


No. 07-1180 Consolidated with 07-1194, 07-1226, 07-1326, 07-1366, 07-1390, 07-1447, 07-1507, 07-1524, 08-1023, 08-1360


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


550 F.3d 1174; 384 U.S. App. D.C. 68; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 25575; 91 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P43,421; 28 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 904


November 10, 2008, Argued 
December 19, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Adams v. Faa, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 7009 (U.S., Oct. 5, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner pilots sought review, pursuant to 49 U.S.C.S. § 46110(a), of the orders of the Federal Aviation Administration (FAA), which denied their requests for an exemption from 14 C.F.R. § 121.383(c) (the Age 60 Rule), barring them from flying commercial aircraft after they turned 60. Respondents, the FAA and United States Department of Transportation, sought dismissal of the petitions as moot.


OVERVIEW: Commercial airline pilots who reached 60 before December 13, 2007, petitioned for review of the FAA's denial of an exemption from the Age 60 Rule. On December 13, 2007, the President signed into law the Fair Treatment for Experienced Pilots Act (FTEPA), 49 U.S.C.S. § 44729. The court dismissed the petitions for review. The court held that the FTEPA, which expressly abrogated the Age 60 Rule, mooted the petitions for review of the orders denying exemption from the Age 60 Rule. The court held that, if the pilots wished to challenge the constitutionality of the statute on its face, they were, assuming they could show Article III standing, to file a complaint in the district court because seeking initial review of the statute before the court extended beyond the jurisdictional grant of 49 U.S.C.S. § 46110(a). The court did not have jurisdiction to consider constitutional questions unrelated to the FAA's order, including their claim that FTEPA's treatment of pilots who reached age 60 before its enactment was unconstitutional. The facial challenge to the Act was to be brought in the district court, which had original jurisdiction over federal question claims under 28 U.S.C.S. § 1331.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petitions for review.

Basardh v. Gates


No. 07-1192


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


545 F.3d 1068; 383 U.S. App. D.C. 257; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 23397



November 4, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Ameziane v. Bush, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 108518 (D.D.C., Dec. 2, 2008)
Writ of habeas corpus granted, Request denied by Basardh v. Obama, 612 F. Supp. 2d 30, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 32983 (D.D.C., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Motion to Hold Case in Abeyance or, in the alternative, Dismiss Without Prejudice.
Basardh v. Gates, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 6257 (D.C. Cir., Mar. 13, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a detainee at the United States Naval Base at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba, petitioned for direct judicial review under the Detainee Treatment Act of 2005, 10 U.S.C.S. § 801 note, of the determination of his status as an enemy combatant. The government filed a motion to hold the petition in abeyance.


OVERVIEW: The detainee also had a habeas petition pending in the district court. The petition for direct review in the appellate court raised common issues regarding the legality of the detainee's detention. Resolution of the habeas action in his favor could entirely, or partially, moot his direct review petition. Habeas was the preferred course for the Guantanamo Bay detainees. In light of the United States Supreme Court case of Boumediene, the appellate court had lost jurisdiction over the direct review petition and every all other such petitions filed under the Detainee Treatment Act. There was a high probability that a consequence of Boumediene's striking down the legislative bar against habeas jurisdiction was that the direct judicial review provision of the Detainee Treatment Act fell as well. Congress had rejected proposed amendments to the Military Commissions Act that would have afforded detainees access to dual remedies. Since Boumediene had restored habeas jurisdiction, the original reasons for direct review had vanished. Since the direct review provision could no longer function in a manner consistent with the intent of Congress, it too fell. Abeyance was warranted.


OUTCOME: The government's motion to hold the case in abeyance pending the conclusion of the detainee's habeas proceedings in the district court was granted.

 

232 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Emergency Coalition to Defend Educ. Travel v. United States Dep't of the Treasury


No. 07-5317


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


545 F.3d 4; 383 U.S. App. D.C. 223; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 23307


September 16, 2008, Argued 
November 4, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 06cv01215).
Emergency Coalition to Defend Educational Travel v. United States Dep't of the Treasury, 498 F. Supp. 2d 150, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 54619 (D.D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, an association of academics, two college professors, and three undergraduate students, appealed a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia granting defendant Secretary of the Treasury's motion to dismiss a complaint alleging that 2004 amendments to regulations governing Cuba-based study abroad programs violated the First and Fifth Amendments as well as the Administrative Procedures Act (APA).


OVERVIEW: Travel to Cuba was generally restricted under the Trading with the Enemy Act, 50 U.S.C.S. app. § 5(b), as implemented by the Secretary's Cuban Assets Control Regulations, 31 C.F.R. pt. 515, with furthered the objective of isolating the Cuban government by depriving the island's economy of the benefit of U.S. dollars. The academics objected to 2004 amendments that tightened the licensure requirements for student travel-abroad programs by restricting licenses to full semester courses taught by university professors in order to prevent the programs from degenerating into purely tourist activities. Applying an intermediate level of scrutiny, the appeals court agreed that the regulations did not unconstitutionally restrict academic freedom rights under the First Amendment because they were content-neutral and furthered an important government interest. The academics' Fifth Amendment challenge was unfounded because the government could restrict international travel for foreign policy reasons. The academics' APA claim failed because the factual premises underlying a commission report that prompted the regulatory revisions were intertwined with unreviewable foreign policy judgments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

Free Enter. Fund v. Public Co. Accounting Oversight Bd.


No. 07-5127


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


537 F.3d 667; 383 U.S. App. D.C. 119; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 18029; Fed. Sec. L. Rep. (CCH) P94,812


April 15, 2008, Argued 
August 22, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended June 9, 2009.
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Free Enter. Fund v. Public Co. Accounting Oversight Bd., 129 S. Ct. 2378, 173 L. Ed. 2d 1291, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 3580 (U.S., 2009)
Affirmed in part and reversed in part by, Remanded by Free Enter. Fund v. Pub. Co. Accounting Oversight Bd., 130 S. Ct. 3138, 177 L. Ed. 2d 706, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 5524 (U.S., 2010)
Vacated by, Remanded by Free Enter. Fund v. Public Co. Accounting Oversight Bd., 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 20200 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 27, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 06cv00217).
Free Enter. Fund v. Public Co. Accounting Oversight Bd., 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 24310 (D.D.C., Mar. 21, 2007)



DISPOSITION:  The grant of summary judgment to the Board and the United States was affirmed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a public interest group and one of its registered accounting firm members, appealed the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's grant of summary judgment to appellees, the Public Company Accounting Oversight Board and intervenor United States, on claims that Title I of the Sarbanes-Oxley Act of 2002, 15 U.S.C.S. §§ 7211-19, violated the Appointments Clause and separation of powers.


OVERVIEW: The Act did not encroach upon the Appointments Clause power because, in view of the Securities and Exchange Commission's comprehensive control of the Board, the Board's members were inferior officers not required to be appointed by the United States President. The Board's work was necessarily directed and supervised by the Commission. The for-cause limitations under 15 U.S.C.S. §§ 7211(e)(6), 7217(d)(3) on the Commission's power to remove Board members and the President's power to remove Commissioners did not strip the President of sufficient power to influence the Board and thus did not contravene separation of powers, as that principle embraced independent agencies like the Commission and their exercise of broad authority over their subordinates. Not only was the Board subject to statutorily-defined jurisdictional boundaries, but also the Commission was empowered to further limit the Board's functions as stated in § 7217(d)(2). Given the constitutionality of independent agencies and the Commission's comprehensive control over the Board, it could not be shown that the statutory scheme so restricted the President's control over the Board as to violate separation of powers.


OUTCOME: The grant of summary judgment to the Board and the United States was affirmed.

Wilson v. Libby


No. 07-5257


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


535 F.3d 697; 383 U.S. App. D.C. 82; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 17119


May 9, 2008, Argued 
August 12, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Wilson v. Libby, 557 U.S. 919, 129 S. Ct. 2825, 174 L. Ed. 2d 552, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 4694 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 06cv01258).
Wilson v. Libby, 498 F. Supp. 2d 74, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 51978 (D.D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of a judgment of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, alleging a Bivens claim and tort claims against defendants.


OVERVIEW: Appellants argued that the district court erred in holding that special factors precluded implication of a Bivens claim and that the government's Federal Employees Liability Reform and Tort Compensation Act of 1988 (Westfall Act), 28 U.S.C.S. § 2679, certification was proper. As to the Bivens argument, each claim in the complaint was based on this disclosure of Privacy Act protected information. The instant court could not create a Bivens remedy because the comprehensive Privacy Act and the sensitive intelligence information concerns affiliated with this case precluded it from doing so. The intentional omission of the Presidential and Vice Presidential offices from the comprehensive coverage of the Privacy Act required the court to deny the additional remedies to appellants which they sought. Because Congress created a comprehensive Privacy Act scheme that did not inadvertently exclude a remedy for the claims brought against these defendants, the court would not supplement the scheme with Bivens remedies. As to the Westfall Act, appellants also failed to exhaust their administrative remedies as required to pursue a tort claim against the United States.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.
