Pirelli Armstrong Tire Corp. Retiree Med. Benefits Trust v. Raines


No. 07-7108


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


534 F.3d 779; 383 U.S. App. D.C. 52; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 16730


April 23, 2008, Argued 
August 8, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Pirelli Armstrong Tire Corp. Retiree Med. Benefits Trust v. Raines, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 27943 (D.C. Cir., Dec. 24, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cv01783).
In re Fannie Mae Sec., 503 F. Supp. 2d 9, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 39348 (D.D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, shareholders on behalf of the Federal National Mortgage Corporation (Fannie Mae), appealed a decision of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia dismissing the shareholders' derivative action brought under Fed. R. Civ. P. 23.1 against defendants, Fannie Mae directors, alleging that the directors failed to prevent accounting irregularities and challenging their approval of severance plans for two officers.


OVERVIEW: The district court found that the complaint failed to meet the test for demand futility under Rule 23.1. The court found that there was federal subject matter jurisdiction over the derivative complaint on behalf of Fannie Mae pursuant to 12 U.S.C.S. § 1723a(a) because the statute's sue and be sued provision expressly referred to federal courts, despite the presence of an "of competent jurisdiction" phrase. On the merits, the court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint. The complaint failed to establish a likelihood of personal liability for the outside directors on the accounting related claims because under Del. Code Ann. tit. 8, § 141(e), the allegations did not create a reasonable doubt of the disinterestedness of the directors to consider a demand. The shareholders also failed to create a reasonable doubt as to whether the directors exercised a valid business judgment in order to rebut the presumption under the business judgment doctrine of § 141(e). Finally, the shareholders failed to create a reasonable doubt about the directors' independence. Therefore, the shareholders failed to meet their burden to show demand futility under Rule 23.1 and Delaware law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint.

Chaplaincy of Full Gospel Churches v. United States Navy (In re Navy Chaplaincy)


No. 07-5359


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


534 F.3d 756; 383 U.S. App. D.C. 29; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 16256


April 24, 2008, Argued 
August 1, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Chaplaincy of Full Gospel Churches v. Dep't of the Navy, 556 U.S. 1167, 129 S. Ct. 1918, 173 L. Ed. 2d 1060, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 2674 (2009)
Injunction denied by In re Navy Chaplaincy, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 10778 (D.D.C., Jan. 30, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 07ms00269).
In re Navy Chaplaincy, 516 F. Supp. 2d 119, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 76084 (D.D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, non-liturgical Protestant Naval chaplains both current and retired, appealed a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia holding that they lacked standing to sue defendant, the U.S. Navy, claiming that the Navy's retirement system discriminated in favor of Catholic chaplains in violation of the Establishment Clause of the First Amendment to the U.S. Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The non-liturgical Protestant chaplains did not allege that the Navy actually discriminated against any of them but argued that the Navy's operation of its retirement system discriminated in favor of Catholic chaplains in violation of the Establishment Clause. The district court found that the non-liturgical Protestant chaplains lacked standing to sue under U.S. Const. art. III. On appeal, the court agreed. Assuming arguendo that the Navy's system violated the Establishment Clause, the Protestant chaplains had not suffered an injury-in-fact because, as they conceded, they themselves did not suffer employment discrimination on account of their religion; rather, they suggested that other chaplaincy groups recognized by the Navy, such as liturgical Protestant and Special Worship chaplains, suffered discrimination. The non-liturgical Protestant chaplains lacked standing as taxpayers because their Establishment Clause claim did not arise out of the Taxing and Spending Clause in U.S. Const. art. I, § 8. They lacked standing based on their exposure to the Navy's message of religious preference because mere personal offense to government action did not give rise to standing to sue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

237 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Vann v. Kempthorne


No. 07-5024


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


534 F.3d 741; 383 U.S. App. D.C. 14; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 16561


May 6, 2008, Argued 
July 29, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Cherokee Nation v. Nash, 724 F. Supp. 2d 1159, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 66215 (N.D. Okla., 2010)
Motion denied by Vann v. Salazar, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 113313 (D.D.C., Sept. 30, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 03cv01711).
Vann v. Kempthorne, 467 F. Supp. 2d 56, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 91090 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the descendents of freed slaves (Freedmen), sued defendant Secretary of the Interior, alleging under 5 U.S.C.S. § 706(2)(A) that he arbitrarily recognized an election held by intervenor Cherokee Nation that disenfranchised the Freedmen. The Freedmen sought to enjoin such recognition. The Cherokee Nation appealed the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's denial of its motion to dismiss based on sovereign immunity.


OVERVIEW: The Freedmen argued that the Secretary's ratification of the Cherokee Nation's 2003 election disenfranchised them in violation of federal constitutional and statutory law. After determining that the tribe was a necessary party under Fed. R. Civ. P. 19(a), the district court concluded that the tribe and its officers could be joined because the tribe did not enjoy sovereign immunity against the Freedmen's suit. The issue raised by the Cherokee Nation on appeal was the extent to which sovereign immunity protected a federally-recognized tribe and its officers against suit. The court held that the district court erred in joining the tribe as a necessary party to the suit because nothing in U.S. Const. amend. XIII or a 1866 Treaty amounted to an express and unequivocal congressional abrogation of tribal sovereign immunity; therefore, the Cherokee Nation could not be joined in the Freedmen's federal court suit without the tribe's consent. However, tribal officers did not enjoy the same immunity from suit as did the tribe itself. Under Ex Parte Young, tribal officers could be enjoined from acting unconstitutionally.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court held that the Cherokee Nation was not amenable to suit based on its sovereign immunity and could not be joined as a required party under Fed. R. of Civ. P. 19(a). The court affirmed the district court's decision with regard to joining tribal officers as required parties.

Bryant v. Gates


No. 07-5121 Consolidated with 07-5180


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


532 F.3d 888; 382 U.S. App. D.C. 287; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 14909; 37 Media L. Rep. 1814


May 13, 2008, Argued 
July 15, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 05cv00064).
Bryant v. Secretary of the Army, 862 F. Supp. 574, 1994 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 13872 (D.D.C., 1994)
Bryant v. Gates, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 6256 (D.C. Cir., Mar. 12, 2008)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant advertiser sought judicial review of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's entry of summary judgment in favor of appellee United States on all of his claims. The advertiser asserted that the refusal by the Department of Defense to allow his advertisements to be published in its civilian enterprise newspapers (CEN) violated his rights under the First Amendment to the U.S. Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The suit arose out of the advertiser having submitted seven self-styled whistleblower solicitation advertisements to two dozen CENs. He claimed that § 4.11 of Department of Defense Instruction 5120.4 was unconstitutional on its face and as applied to his paid advertisements, by violating his rights to free expression and to freedom of the press under the First Amendment. Section 4.11 was not unconstitutionally vague. The bar in § 4.11 was well-defined. The advertiser argued unsuccessfully that § 4.11 was not applied in a consistent manner. He also argued unsuccessfully that § 4.11, as written and applied to his advertisements, was not narrowly tailored to serve a compelling governmental interest. Since the advertising section of a CEN was a nonpublic forum, the restriction upon speech in § 4.11 needed only be reasonable in light of the purpose of the forum and viewpoint-neutral. It was clearly both. His claim of facial viewpoint discrimination was patently unfounded. Section 4.11 by its terms did not distinguish between political viewpoints.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

 

239 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Cassell


No. 06-3146


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


530 F.3d 1009; 382 U.S. App. D.C. 96; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 14913


May 2, 2008, Argued 
July 15, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Cassell v. United States, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 901 (U.S., Jan. 26, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 00cr00270-01).
Cassell v. United States, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 53600 (D.D.C., July 19, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury convicted him of several drug and gun offenses, including possession of a firearm in furtherance of a drug trafficking offense in violation of 18 U.S.C.S. § 924(c)(1) petitioner inmate appealed a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, denying his collateral attack under 28 U.S.C.S. § 2255 on his 288-month sentence. Respondent was the United States government.


OVERVIEW: While executing a search warrant at a house owned by the inmate's uncle, police seized large quantities of drugs and cash, finding loaded semiautomatic weapons inside the inmate's bedroom. The appeals court addressed only one of the issues raised in the district court, the inmate's claim that trial counsel was ineffective for failing to insist that the judge instruct the jury that possession of a semiautomatic assault weapon was an element of a separate offense under 18 U.S.C.S. § 924(c)(1) that the jury was required to find beyond a reasonable doubt. The court noted that the inmate did not object to jury instructions that defined "firearm" but did not mention assault weapons. After conviction, the judge, without objection, determined that the firearm that the inmate possessed was a Colt AR-15 semiautomatic rifle. The appeals court held that the type of firearm possessed by the inmate was not an element of the crime but a sentencing factor, which the district court properly found without a jury under a preponderance-of-the-evidence standard. Therefore, counsel was not ineffective, under either the first or the second prong of the Strickland test, for failing to raise the issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

Owens v. Republic of the Sudan


No. 05-5173 Consolidated with 06-5079


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


531 F.3d 884; 382 U.S. App. D.C. 155; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 14716


February 7, 2008, Argued 
July 11, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Findings of fact/conclusions of law at Owens v. Republic of Sudan, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 135961 (D.D.C., Nov. 28, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia (No. 0 1 cv02244).
Owens v. Republic of Sudan, 412 F. Supp. 2d 99, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 2547 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the Republic of Sudan and its Interior Ministry, filed interlocutory appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's order denying their motion to dismiss a third amended complaint filed by plaintiffs, U.S. nationals injured in terrorist bombings of U.S. embassies in Africa and their family members, under the Foreign Sovereign Immunities Act (FSIA), 28 U.S.C.S. § 1605(a)(7).


OVERVIEW: The U.S. nationals and their families alleged that the Republic materially supported the terrorist groups that carried out three U.S. embassy bombings in Africa and asserted that the court had jurisdiction over the Republic and its Interior Ministry under FSIA's state sponsor of terrorism exception, 28 U.S.C.S. § 1605(a)(7). On appeal, the court held that Congress had been granted the power to define the jurisdiction of the lower federal courts. Thus, Congress' delegation to the Executive Branch of factfinding to declare whether a foreign state was to be designated a state sponsor of terrorism for FSIA purposes did not violate the separation of powers doctrine. The court also held that the third amended complaint alleged sufficient facts to meet the jurisdictional causation requirement of § 1605(a)(7). The allegations demonstrated a reasonable connection between the Republic's sheltering and financial support of two prominent international terrorist groups and the terrorist bombings of the African embassies, which was sufficient specificity to meet the pleading requirements of Fed. R. Civ. P. 8(a)(1) and the jurisdictional requirements of FSIA.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the Republic's motion to dismiss and remanded for further proceedings.

 

241 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Venetian Casino Resort, L.L.C. v. EEOC


No. 06-5361


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


530 F.3d 925; 382 U.S. App. D.C. 12; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 13534; 103 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1025; 91 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P43,250


November 20, 2007, Argued 
June 27, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 00cv02980).
Venetian Casino Resort v. EEOC, 453 F. Supp. 2d 157, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 70972 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Citing to the Trade Secrets Act (TSA), Freedom of Information Act (FOIA), and the Administrative Procedures Act, appellant employer sought to enjoin appellee federal agency from disclosing confidential information without notice. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed the case as unripe. The instant court reversed. On remand, the district court granted the agency's summary judgment motion. The employer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The agency, charged with investigating employment law violations, issued a subpoena for documents the employer deemed and identified as confidential. The employer claimed that the agency's policy, which permitted its employees to disclose confidential information to potential employment discrimination plaintiffs without first notifying the employer that its information would be disclosed, violated the Administrative Procedure Act (APA), the Freedom of Information Act (FOIA), and the Trade Secrets Act (TSA). Inter alia, the appellate court held that it was clear the agency had a policy of disclosing confidential information without notice. Although the purpose of the TSA might well be furthered if the agency gave submitters notice before disclosing their confidential information, the agency was not required to take explicit account of public policies that derive from federal statutes other than the agency's enabling act. However, maintaining two irreconcilable policies, one of which apparently enabled the agency or any person asking for information, to circumvent the agency's FOIA regulations, which required pre-release notification, was arbitrary and capricious agency action.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed, and remanded the case for the district court to enter an injunction prohibiting the agency from disclosing the employer's confidential information pursuant to its current disclosure policy.

Johnson v. District of Columbia


No. 06-7136 CONSOLIDATED WITH 06-7180


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


528 F.3d 969; 381 U.S. App. D.C. 351; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 13289


April 8, 2008, Argued 
June 20, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 02cv01452).
Johnson v. District of Columbia, 445 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 55442 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, police officer one, sought review of a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, which granted summary judgment to appellees, police officer two and the District of Columbia, in officer one's suit asserting claims of police brutality, assault and battery, and intentional infliction of emotional distress against the District and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 individual capacity claims against officer two.


OVERVIEW: Officer one, who was off duty, attempted to help a stranger who said that he was being robbed. Unbeknownst to officer one, the stranger was a drug dealer who was being chased by police. When officer one realized what was happening, he placed himself in a prone position on the floor, with his arms and legs spread to indicate surrender. Officer two allegedly repeatedly kicked and stomped on officer one's groin and buttocks until officer two saw a police badge on officer one's neck. On appeal, the court first held that the district court erred in granting summary judgment to officer two on the basis of qualified immunity because, under the facts as presented by officer one, officer two violated officer one's U.S. Const. amend. IV rights through the use of excessive force, and a reasonable officer would have known that kicking officer one in the groin and stomping on him after he surrendered were not lawful means of effecting a seizure. However, the common law claims against the District were properly dismissed because officer one's exclusive remedy against the District was to obtain compensation under the Police and Firefighters Retirement and Disability Act, D.C. Code § 5-701 et seq.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's judgment in favor of officer two and remanded for trial of officer one's 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim against officer two. The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the District as to officer one's common law claims.

United States v. Askew


No. 04-3092


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


529 F.3d 1119; 381 U.S. App. D.C. 415; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 13315


October 11, 2007, Argued 
June 20, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cr00010-01).
United States v. Askew, 313 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2004 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 6775 (D.D.C., 2004)
United States v. Askew, 375 U.S. App. D.C. 440, 482 F.3d 532, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 7969 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, challenging the dismissal of his motion to suppress.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the unzipping of his jacket was unlawful, because it was not undertaken for protective purposes, but rather amounted to an impermissible evidentiary search unsupported by probable cause or a warrant. The instant court concluded that the police officers violated defendant's Fourth Amendment rights by unzipping his jacket without his permission and without probable cause or a warrant. The frisk of defendant did not produce any evidence of a weapon. Nevertheless, the police undertook a further search aimed at determining whether certain physical evidence would identify defendant as the perpetrator of the crime in question. The police manipulated defendant's jacket, partially unzipping and opening it, so that a complainant could see the sweatshirt underneath defendant's jacket. Among other things, the government's argument, that the unzipping could be justified as an objectively reasonable response to defendant's alleged resistance during the pat down, was without support in the district court's factual findings. The district court declined to find either that the pat down was incomplete or that defendant in any way resisted the pat down.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded.

United States v. Brodie


No. 05-3131


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


524 F.3d 259; 390 U.S. App. D.C. 66; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 9690


November 13, 2007, Argued 
May 6, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by United States v. Brodie, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 28407 (D.C. Cir., June 26, 2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Brodie v. United States, 555 U.S. 1204, 129 S. Ct. 1396, 173 L. Ed. 2d 645, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 1157 (2009)
Post-conviction relief denied at Brodie v. United States, 626 F. Supp. 2d 120, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 53923 (D.D.C., 2009)
Writ of mandamus denied In re Brodie, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 677 (D.C. Cir., Jan. 11, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 02cr00190-01).
United States v. Brodie, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 7833 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 3, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a jury verdict from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia finding him guilty of conspiracy to make false statements to financial institutions to obtain mortgage loans under 18 U.S.C.S. § 371 and wire fraud under 18 U.S.C.S. § 1343. He also appealed imposition of a four-level sentence enhancement under U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 3B1.1(a) and alleged that his counsel was ineffective at sentencing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant coordinated a mortgage flipping scheme by using his shell company to purchase dilapidated real estate properties, obtaining inflated appraisals, then reselling the properties to himself for a much inflated value, often on the same day. He obtained mortgages based on the inflated appraisals, using the money to pay off the purchase prices and keeping the remaining funds for himself. After trial, the prosecutor informed defense counsel of the existence of undisclosed Brady evidence, i.e., new information that a key State's witness, a co-conspirator, had been accused of a similar fraud scheme, a fact that defendant could have used to impeach her. On appeal, the court held that the failure to disclose Brady materials was not cause for reversal because the evidence was cumulative of other impeachment evidence used at trial. Defendant's sentence was properly enhanced under § 3B1.1(a) because he led and organized a conspiracy of five or more participants. Counsel's failure to request a downward departure based on defendant's status as a deportable alien was not evidence of ineffectiveness because the court indicated that it would not have granted a downward departure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

Larsen v. United States Navy


No. 07-5196


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


525 F.3d 1; 381 U.S. App. D.C. 69; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 9626; 103 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 180


March 7, 2008, Argued 
April 29, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Claim dismissed by Larsen v. United States Navy, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 62492 (D.D.C., Aug. 12, 2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Larsen v. Dep't of the Navy, 555 U.S. 1071, 129 S. Ct. 738, 172 L. Ed. 2d 728, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 8956 (2008)
Reaffirmed Larsen v. United States Navy, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 1272 (D.C. Cir., Jan. 17, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 02cv02005).
Larsen v. United States Navy, 486 F. Supp. 2d 11, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 31167 (D.D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, three applicants to the Navy Chaplain Corps, filed an action alleging that the Navy maintained a religious quota system for choosing chaplains and that under this system the Navy illegally refused to hire them because they were non-liturgical Protestants. Plaintiffs appealed from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia challenging the district court's rulings.


OVERVIEW: The instant court lacked jurisdiction to evaluate the merits of the district court's substantive holdings because the entire case was moot. The case was moot because in their complaint plaintiffs challenged only the legality of the Navy's alleged Thirds Policy, but they admitted that the Thirds Policy ended in 2001 and that the Navy now maintained no religious quotas. Because plaintiffs never alleged that the Navy was likely to or even considering reinstituting the Thirds Policy, there was no reasonable expectation that the alleged violation would recur. The Navy's continued use of the Chaplain Accession and Recall Eligibility Board was irrelevant because the Board no longer applied any type of religious quota system. Because the Navy already eliminated the Thirds Policy and plaintiffs never alleged that the Navy would reinstitute it, any injunction or order declaring it illegal would have accomplished nothing--amounting to exactly the type of advisory opinion Article III of the United States Constitution prohibited. Because plaintiffs' claim relied on their first being hired as Navy chaplains--a vital condition that had yet to occur--it was unripe.


OUTCOME: The instant court remanded the case to the district court with instructions to dismiss plaintiffs' claim as moot.

Mich. Gambling Opposition v. Kempthorne


No. 07-5092


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


525 F.3d 23; 381 U.S. App. D.C. 91; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 9629; 38 ELR 20102


October 19, 2007, Argued 
April 29, 2008, Decided

Stay granted by Mich. Gambling Opposition v. Kempthorne, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 28181 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 15, 2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Mich. Gambling Opposition v. Kempthorne, 555 U.S. 1137, 129 S. Ct. 1002, 173 L. Ed. 2d 293, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 760 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 05cv01181).
Mich. Gambling Opposition v. Norton, 477 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 15430 (D.D.C., 2007)



DISPOSITION:  The judgment of the district court was affirmed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant antigambling group that asserted that the National Environmental Protection Act (NEPA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 4321 et seq., was violated and that 25 U.S.C.S. § 465 was unconstitutional, appealed entry of summary judgment by the United States District Court for the District of Columbia in favor of appellees, the Secretary of the Interior (Secretary), the Bureau of Indian Affairs (BIA), and the National Indian Gaming Commission.


OVERVIEW: The case arose after the BIA decided to take 147 acres of land in Wayland Township, Michigan, into trust for use by an Indian tribe who planned to construct and operate a Class III casino. In regards to the group's first NEPA argument, the 40 C.F.R. § 1501.4(b)-(c) process--environmental assessment (EA) preparation followed by a decision on whether to prepare an environmental impact statement (EIS)--was applicable. The Department of the Interior (DOI) followed those procedures and lawfully determined not to prepare an EIS on the basis of the EA. Alternatively, the group contended unsuccessfully that it was arbitrary or capricious for the DOI to issue Findings of No Significant Impact without having prepared an EIS because, as the group alleged, two intersections would continue to experience Level of Service F at certain times, even after mitigation measures. 25 U.S.C.S. § 465 was not an unconstitutional delegation of authority. The statute provided the intelligible principle of providing lands sufficient to enable Indians to achieve self-support and ameliorating the damage resulting from prior federal policy.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

Pharm. Care Mgmt. Ass'n v. District of Columbia


No. 07-7062


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


522 F.3d 443; 380 U.S. App. D.C. 418; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 8386


March 7, 2008, Argued 
April 18, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cv01082).
Pharm. Care Mgmt. Ass'n v. District of Columbia, 477 F. Supp. 2d 86, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 15268 (D.D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant association, a trade organization representing pharmacy benefit managers, sought review of a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, which granted summary judgment to appellee, the District of Columbia, in the association's suit seeking to enjoin the enforcement of the District of Columbia's AccessRx Act of 2004, D.C. Code § 48-831.01 et seq.


OVERVIEW: While the association's preliminary injunction was pending, the United States Court of Appeals for the First Circuit upheld a Maine law that was very similar to the AccessRx Act of 2004. The suit concerning the Maine law had been brought by the association. The district court thus held that the instant suit was barred under the doctrine of collateral estoppel. On appeal, the court disagreed, noting that in a non-mutual case, an issue was not precluded if it was one of law and treating it as conclusively determined would inappropriately foreclose opportunities for obtaining reconsideration of the legal rule upon which it was based. This case concerned questions of law and involved non-mutual estoppel because the District had not been party to the first suit. Further, practical considerations counseled against the application of collateral estoppel because the Department of Labor had proposed regulations that would require employee benefit plans to disclose information similar to the disclosures required by the AccessRx Act of 2004, which might impact the question in the instant case of whether the Employee Retirement Income Security Act of 1974 preempted the AccessRx Act of 2004.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the district court's order and remanded the case for reconsideration on the merits.

 

249 of 475 DOCUMENTS



Griffith v. Lanier


No. 07-7072


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


521 F.3d 398; 380 U.S. App. D.C. 297; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 7149; 27 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 753


February 15, 2008, Argued 
April 4, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 06cv01223).
Griffith v. Lanier, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 22176 (D.D.C., Mar. 28, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff police volunteers appealed the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's dismissal of their claims against defendant police chief challenging, under the First Amendment, D.C. Metropolitan Police Department General Order 101.03's limitation of their rights to organize as a union and argued the Order's at-will dismissal provision violated due process. They also appealed the substitution of the new chief.


OVERVIEW: The new chief's taking office triggered Fed. R. Civ. P. 25(d), which automatically substituted the successor of a public officer named in his "official capacity." The volunteers never sought to assert claims against the former chief in his personal capacity. The Order restricted the "right to organize for collective bargaining purposes," which was naturally understood as the right to engage in collective bargaining -- i.e., the right to force labor negotiations. It left the volunteers' First Amendment rights intact because while the U.S. Constitution guaranteed workers the right individually or collectively to voice their views to their employers, it did not afford them the right to compel employers to engage in a dialogue or even to listen. Because District of Columbia law did not generally treat the volunteers as "members of the police force" in the sense necessary to protect their tenure under D.C. Code § 5-127.01, the volunteers remained subject to at-will dismissal, and they lacked the statutorily-protected property interest necessary to ground a due process challenge.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the district court.

Belbacha v. Bush


No. 07-5258


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


520 F.3d 452; 380 U.S. App. D.C. 245; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 5486


December 6, 2007, Argued 
March 14, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by, Injunction granted at Belbacha v. Bush, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 124062 (D.D.C., June 10, 2008)
Motion denied by In re Guantanamo Bay Detainee Litig., 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 145339 (D.D.C., Mar. 9, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 05cv02349).
Bacha v. Bush, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 67128 (D.D.C., July 27, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an Algerian national detained at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba, as an enemy combatant (detainee), filed an interlocutory appeal challenging a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, preliminarily declining to grant him interim relief by barring his transfer to Algeria, where he alleged he would be tortured, while his habeas corpus petition was pending. Appellees included the U.S. President.


OVERVIEW: When it refused to bar the detainee's transfer to Algeria, the district court had not yet ruled on the detainee's habeas petition because the constitutionality of the Military Commissions Act of 2006 (MCA), Pub. L. No. 109-366, 120 Stat. 2600, which removed lower court jurisdiction over detainees' habeas petitions, was pending review by the U.S. Supreme Court. The appeals court treated the district court's order declining to bar the detainee's transfer as a denial of a preliminary injunction, which was appealable under 28 U.S.C.S. § 1292(a)(1). The appeals court held that the district court had authority to grant the detainee interim relief because the Suspension Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 9, cl. 2, colorably protected his pending habeas corpus claims. Additionally, § 7(a) of the MCA, 28 U.S.C.S. § 2241(e), did not displace the district court's remedial powers under the All Writs Act, 28 U.S.C.S. § 1651, to issue an auxiliary writ in aid of jurisdiction that already existed, i.e., the jurisdiction to determine whether the MCA was constitutional. Otherwise, the detainee's transfer would make it impossible for the district court to entertain his colorable constitutional claim.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's denial of interim relief and remanded for the district court to decide whether a preliminary injunction should be issued in the detainee's favor.

Nat'l Mining Ass'n v. Kempthorne


No. 06-5199


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


512 F.3d 702; 379 U.S. App. D.C. 268; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 798; 38 ELR 20016


September 24, 2007, Argued 
January 15, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Nat'l Mining Ass'n v. Kempthorne, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 8842 (U.S., Dec. 1, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 00cv00283).
Nat'l Mining Ass'n v. Scarlett, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 25874 (D.D.C., 2006)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On cross-motions for summary judgment, the United States District Court for the District of Columbia found "valid existing rights" (VER) in 30 U.S.C.S. § 1272(e) of the Surface Mining Control and Reclamation Act (SMCRA) ambiguous and deferred to defendant Secretary of the Interior's interpretation. Plaintiff mining association appealed, challenging the interpretation in 30 C.F.R. § 761.5 as foreclosing surface mining in sensitive areas.


OVERVIEW: Over 30 years, VER had been interpreted by five administrations. That Congress presented so wide a range of plausible interpretations to a rulemaking agency showed a delegation of the power to make reasonable adjustments. VER was subject to divergent interpretations; it was ambiguous. Alternative definitions of "right" indicated that the statute was open to interpretation. The Secretary had considered the matter in a detailed and reasoned fashion as evidenced by his 72-page preamble to § 761.5, and the interpretation was reasonable. Protecting against the harmful effects of surface mining was the primary aim of the SMCRA. Several uses of "property" in the SMCRA ran counter to miners' property rights. In 30 U.S.C.S. § 1272(e), Congress simply declared surface mining off limits for those who had no valid existing rights. It created no process that was denied. Takings claims could be pursued in the United States Court of Federal Claims; such challenges raised no serious question to preclude Chevron deference. No "identifiable class" of miners was shown whose takings claims would expose the government to huge liability; thus, there was no serious constitutional problem to be avoided.


OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was affirmed.

 

252 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Sheehan


No. 07-3002


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


512 F.3d 621; 379 U.S. App. D.C. 187; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 479


October 16, 2007, Argued 
January 11, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 05mj00649-17).
Sheehan v. United States, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 91439 (D.D.C., Dec. 18, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A magistrate convicted appellant demonstrator of demonstrating at the White House without a National Park Service permit in violation of 36 C.F.R. § 7.96(g)(2). The United States District Court for the District of Columbia affirmed. Appealing, the demonstrator argued the permit requirement was facially unconstitutional under the First Amendment and there was no evidence of mens rea because the case was prosecuted on a strict liability premise.


OVERVIEW: Nothing significant in the regulations or legislative history indicated that a strict liability regime was adopted. Thus, a mens rea requirement was implied and § 7.96(g)(2) was not facially unconstitutional for want of a mens rea requirement. But, the prosecution proceeded on the erroneous strict liability premise, thus relieving the prosecution of its burden to prove mens rea, i.e., what the demonstrator knew, heard, and intended. The Government successfully objected to attempts to offer evidence of knowledge and intent. The Government was allowed to prosecute the demonstrator for a crime that did not exist, thus, she was denied a meaningful opportunity to present a complete defense. It was not harmless error. The verdict was not rendered by a jury, so no jury instructions or other steps were readily available to lessen the gravity of the magistrate's erroneous legal conclusion. While the demonstrator had testified she "didn't know she needed a permit," that may have only indicated that she thought the original permit, that included the White House sidewalk, was still in effect. A new trial was warranted.


OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was affirmed except as to the claim of error for lack of evidence of mens rea because the case was prosecuted on a strict liability premise. The judgment on that issue was reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial.

Rasul v. Myers


No. 06-5209 Consolidated with 06-5222


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


512 F.3d 644; 379 U.S. App. D.C. 210; 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 509


September 14, 2007, Argued 
January 11, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Remanded by Rasul v. Myers, 129 S. Ct. 763, 172 L. Ed. 2d 753, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 9139 (U.S., 2008)
Petition denied by Shafiq Rasul v. Rumsfeld, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 11134 (D.C. Cir., Feb. 13, 2009)
On remand at, Reaffirmed Rasul v. Myers, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 8980 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 24, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cv01864).
Rasul v. Rumsfeld, 433 F. Supp. 2d 58, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 26665 (D.D.C., 2006)
Rasul v. Rumsfeld, 414 F. Supp. 2d 26, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 4275 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed claims under the Alien Tort Statute (ATS), the Geneva Conventions, and the Fifth and Eighth Amendments, filed by plaintiff former detainees at the United States Naval Base at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba, against defendants, the Secretary of Defense and military officers, that alleged illegal detention and torture, but denied dismissal of the RFRA claim. Both sides appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Attorney General had certified under 28 U.S.C.S. § 2679(d)(1) that the alleged conduct was incidental to authorized conduct, and the alleged abuse was tied exclusively to being detained and interrogated in a military prison. Even serious criminal conduct against the detainees could have been foreseeable. Because the alleged conduct came within the scope of defendants' employment, the ATS and Geneva Conventions claims were properly restyled as claims against the United States governed by the Federal Tort Claims Act and were properly dismissed for lack of subject matter jurisdiction for a failure to exhaust under 28 U.S.C.S. § 2675(a). Any Fifth and Eighth Amendment claims were subject to qualified immunity as it was not clear that those rights applied to non-resident aliens. Precedent regarding leased military bases supported the conclusion that Guantanamo was not a United States territory. The RFRA did not expand the First Amendment's grant of religious freedom rights, thus, "person" under the RFRA was interpreted consistent with other constitutional provisions to exclude non-resident aliens.


OUTCOME: The district court's dismissal of the ATS, Geneva Conventions, and constitutional claims was affirmed. The denial of the motion to dismiss the RFRA claim was reversed.


 

254 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Campaign v. FDA


No. 06-5333


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


511 F.3d 187; 379 U.S. App. D.C. 158; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 29857


October 12, 2007, Argued 
December 28, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Costs and fees proceeding at, Motion granted by Campaign for Responsible Transplantation v. United States FDA, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 4161 (D.D.C., Jan. 22, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 00cv02849).
Campaign v. United States FDA, 448 F. Supp. 2d 146, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 62615 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant not-for-profit organization had moved for attorney's fees, pursuant to 5 U.S.C.S. § 552(a)(4)(E). The United States District Court for the District of Columbia denied the request for fees on the ground that the not-for-profit organization had not substantially prevailed in its litigation. The not-for-profit organization appealed.


OVERVIEW: The not-for-profit organization submitted a request to the Food and Drug Administration (FDA) under the Freedom of Information Act, seeking records regarding applications to conduct clinical trials that involve xenotransplantation and past and present clinical trials involving xenotransplantation. The instant court held that the trial court's decision that FDA's Vaughn indices were inadequate, and subsequent order requiring FDA to produce new indices, did not constitute judicial relief under the Buckhannon standard. Therefore, the not-for-profit organization could not successfully claim to be a prevailing party based upon those decisions. However, the not-for-profit organization was a prevailing party by virtue of a September 24, 2004 memorandum opinion and order. The trial court's final order in this case--requiring FDA to release documents--was sufficient judicial relief to make the not-for-profit organization a prevailing party. This was not a case in which FDA voluntarily released the documents covered by the trial court's order. The agency released the disputed documents only after the order was issued, and it released the documents pursuant to that order.


OUTCOME: The denial of the request for attorney's fees under FOIA was reversed.

Biggerstaff v. FCC


No. 06-1191, Consolidated with No. 06-1251


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


511 F.3d 178; 379 U.S. App. D.C. 149; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 29855; 43 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 630


November 20, 2007, Argued 
December 28, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of an Order of the Federal Communications Commission.
In re Rules, 21 F.C.C.R. 3787, 2006 FCC LEXIS 1713 (F.C.C., 2006)
In re Rules & Regulations Implementing the Tele. Consumer Prot. Act of 1991, 7 F.C.C.R. 8752, 1992 FCC LEXIS 7019 (F.C.C., 1992)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners filed petitions for review of a final rule of respondent Federal Communications Commission (FCC).


OVERVIEW: The final rule implemented the Junk Fax Prevention Act of 2005 (JFPA), 47 U.S.C.S. § 227, by adopting an exemption to a prohibition against unsolicited telephone facsimile advertisements for entities with whom the recipient had an established business relationship (EBR). The court concluded that one petitioner's challenge to the validity of the EBR exemption fell outside the scope of the 2006 rulemaking, and the second petitioner lacked Article III standing. As to the first petitioner, the FCC's prospective rescission of the EBR exemption in 2003 placed petitioner's challenge to that exemption outside the scope of the 2006 rulemaking. Given the absence of any indication in either notice of proposed rulemaking that the FCC intended to re-examine its statutory authority in 1992 to adopt an EBR exemption, there was no basis for the court to conclude that the FCC reopened the issue in the 2006 rulemaking. The second petitioner failed to demonstrate that facsimile advertisers would respond differently if the court were to require the FCC to revise its order to strike references to "permit" and substitute "does not prohibit" in referring to the JFPA's relationship to unsolicited faxes.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petitions.

 

256 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Munsell v. Dep't of Agric.


No. 06-5261 Consolidated with 06-5262


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


509 F.3d 572; 379 U.S. App. D.C. 45; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 28541


September 24, 2007, Argued 
December 11, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cv01745).
Munsell v. United States Dep't of Agric., 435 F. Supp. 2d 149, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 38692 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a meat processing plant, its president, and a trade association, appealed a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia dismissing their claims against defendants, the U.S. Department of Agriculture (USDA) and its Food Safety and Inspection Service (FSIS), for lack of jurisdiction. The parties sought declaratory and injunctive relief under the Administrative Procedure Act and money damages under Bivens.


OVERVIEW: The president alleged that FSIS officials violated the First Amendment by using USDA enforcement powers to retaliate for statements he made about USDA's handling of a bacterial outbreak at the plant. The trade association represented small meat processors subject to USDA inspection. On appeal, the court held that the district court erred by finding that the failure to exhaust administrative remedies deprived it of subject matter jurisdiction under the Federal Crop Insurance Reform and Department of Agriculture Reorganization Act of 1994, 7 U.S.C.S. § 6912(e). The Act did not create a jurisdictional bar to judicial review of USDA actions but merely established a mandatory, but nonjurisdictional, exhaustion requirement. However, the president and the plant lacked standing under U.S. Const. art. III because they failed to exhaust their remedies under 9 C.F.R. § 306.5. Their injunctive and declaratory relief claims were moot because the plant had been sold, and their Bivens claims were subject to dismissal under Fed. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6) for failure to exhaust administrative remedies under 7 U.S.C.S. § 6912(e). The trade association had standing to sue but its claims were unripe.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the parties' actions.

Parker v. District of Columbia


No. 04-7041


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 22872



September 25, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
03cv00213.
Parker v. District of Columbia, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 12467 (D.C. Cir., May 24, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was appellants' motion to lift stay of mandate.


OVERVIEW: Appellants' contended appellees' petition for certiorari conceded the unconstitutionality of D.C. Code § 7-2507.02 as it required the disassembling of shotguns and rifles or the placement of trigger locks, making such arms practically useless for self defense. Therefore, appellants argued, the court's mandate holding this provision unconstitutional should issue. But the court's opinion did not specifically address the constitutionality of that statute as it applied to shotguns and rifles because the only plaintiff the court concluded had standing under precedent complained solely about the restrictions on ownership and use of a handgun. At least one other plaintiff did address § 7-2507.02 as it applied to shotguns but she did not have the same injury--the denial of a license.


OUTCOME: The motion was denied.

Kleiman & Hochberg, Inc. v. United States Dep't of Agric.


No. 06-1283


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


497 F.3d 681; 378 U.S. App. D.C. 166; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 19278


March 13, 2007, Argued 
August 14, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Kleiman & Hochberg, Inc. v. USDA, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 26148 (D.C. Cir., Nov. 6, 2007)
Rehearing denied by Kleiman & Hochberg, Inc. v. USDA, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 26152 (D.C. Cir., Nov. 6, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hirsch v. Dep't of Agric., 170 L. Ed. 2d 541, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 2969 (U.S., Mar. 31, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of an Order of the Department of Agriculture.
Kleiman & Hochberg, Inc. v. Dep't of Agric., 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 13040 (D.C. Cir., June 1, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a wholesale produce merchant and its president, sought review pursuant to 28 U.S.C.S. § 2342(2) of an order of respondent, the United States Department of Agriculture, which revoked the merchant's license to do business under the Perishable Agricultural Commodities Act (PACA), 7 U.S.C.S. § 499a et seq., and triggered restrictions on the president's ability to work in the produce industry.


OVERVIEW: The administrative proceedings resulted after a part owner and officer of the merchant pleaded guilty to bribing a federal produce inspector and admitted making similar payments for over a decade. A Judicial Officer agreed that the merchant had violated 7 U.S.C.S. § 499b(4) and that the president and other officers were responsibly connected to the merchant under the respondeat superior provision of 7 U.S.C.S. § 499h(b) to bar the president from working for any other licensee for at least a year. The court denied the petition for review under the deferential standard of 5 U.S.C.S. § 706(2). The Judicial Officer properly rejected petitioners' claim that the bribes made over a decade were the result of extortion. Even if the payments were induced by extortion, the type of extortion was not a reasonable cause under PACA for the merchant's failure to perform the implied duty to refrain from bribing produce inspectors. Further, the court found no fault in the application of 7 U.S.C.S. § 499p to the impose liability on the merchant because the statute required imputation of wrongful conduct to the licensee merchant in every case and not simply in cases where damage was done.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for review.

United States v. Booker


No. 06-3030


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


496 F.3d 717; 378 U.S. App. D.C. 126; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 18989


May 15, 2007, Argued 
August 10, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by United States v. Booker, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 27878 (D.C. Cir., Nov. 28, 2007)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by United States v. Booker, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 27882 (D.C. Cir., Nov. 28, 2007)
Vacated by, Remanded by, Motion granted by Booker v. United States, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 3323 (U.S., May 4, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 03cr00225-01).
United States v. Booker, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 45522 (D.D.C., Oct. 10, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who pled guilty to one count of possessing with intent to distribute marijuana, in violation of 21 U.S.C.S. § 841(a)(1), (b)(1)(D), sought review of a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, which denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence seized from his vehicle following his arrest. Defendant also appealed his guilty plea on the ground that it lacked a sufficient factual basis.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped by three officers who thought that he was violating traffic laws by having his front license plate displayed inside the windshield. By the time the officers were able to catch up with defendant, his car was stopped and defendant and his passenger had started to walk away from the car. The passenger fled. Defendant was arrested for driving without a license, and a search of the car revealed marijuana. The court held that (1) although defendant did not violate any traffic law through the placement of his license plate because it was a dealer's tag, and defendant complied with the law requiring dealer's tags to be displayed on the back of the vehicle, because the officers had an objectively reasonable belief that defendant was violating the law, the traffic stop was reasonable for U.S. Const. amend. IV purposes; (2) the search of the vehicle, which occurred just minutes after defendant was arrested, was permitted as a search incident to a lawful arrest; and (3) defendant was unable to show that the district court plainly erred in accepting his guilty plea because he conceded that he could not show that but for the error, he would not have entered the plea.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment.

United States v. Bowman


No. 05-3093


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


496 F.3d 685; 378 U.S. App. D.C. 94; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 18652


November 7, 2006, Argued 
August 7, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cr00060-01).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After entering a conditional plea of guilty, defendant appealed a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, which denied his motion to dismiss a drug and firearm indictment against him. Defendant argued that a magistrate's dismissal with prejudice of the original complaint under the Speedy Trial Act, 18 U.S.C.S. §§ 3161(b), 3162(a)(1), precluded prosecution under a subsequent indictment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested at a license-and-inspection roadblock after an officer saw him violate an open container law and after he resisted a pat-down search. Cocaine and a gun were seized from his person. A magistrate dismissed with prejudice a drug and firearms complaint because the government failed to file an indictment within 30 days of arrest under the Speedy Trial Act. Defendant was later indicted. The district court found that the magistrate lacked authority under the Federal Magistrate's Act, 28 U.S.C.S. § 636(b)(1)(A), to dismiss the complaint with prejudice and refused to dismiss the indictment. On appeal, the court declined to address whether the magistrate had authority to dismiss the complaint with prejudice because it agreed with the district court that the magistrate, by summarily signing the dismissal order without holding a hearing, failed to give adequate reasons for dismissal as required by 18 U.S.C.S. § 3162(a)(1), (2). The district court was not precluded by local court rule from reviewing the magistrate's dismissal order. Based on insufficient evidence in the record, the court remanded on the question of whether the roadblock stop violated the Fourth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment that the magistrate judge improperly dismissed the complaint with prejudice and affirmed the court's denial of defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment. Because there were insufficient findings and evidence from which to assess the constitutionality of the roadblock, the court remanded to the district court for further proceedings.

United States v. Rayburn House Office Bldg.


No. 06-3105


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


497 F.3d 654; 378 U.S. App. D.C. 139; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 18488


May 15, 2007, Argued 
August 3, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended August 8, 2007.
Later proceeding at United States v. Rayburn House Office Bldg., 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 19572 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 14, 2007)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by United States v. Rayburn House Office Bldg., 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 26295 (D.C. Cir., Nov. 9, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by United States v. Rayburn House Office Bldg., 128 S. Ct. 1738, 170 L. Ed. 2d 539, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 3025 (U.S., 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 06mj00231).
In re Search of the Rayburn House Office Bldg. Rm No. 2113, 432 F. Supp. 2d 100, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 46131 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant congressional office appealed a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia denying its motion under Fed. R. Crim. P. 41(g), which sought the return of all materials seized by appellee United States and agents of the Executive Branch that executed a search warrant for nonlegislative materials in the congressional office of a sitting Member of Congress. No Fourth Amendment search issue was raised.


OVERVIEW: At issue was whether the procedures under which the search of the congressional office was conducted protected the legislative privilege in the Speech or Debate Clause, U.S. Const. art. 1, § 6, cl. 1. The district court gave prior approval for a warrant procedure that required a Justice Department filter team to scrutinize seized paper and electronic documents for privilege prior to their being turned over to the prosecution. On appeal, the court held that the Clause's absolute testimonial privilege barred disclosure of written and electronic legislative materials. The filtering procedures outlined in the warrant affidavit violated the Clause because they denied the Member any opportunity to identify and assert the privilege with respect to legislative materials before their compelled disclosure to Executive agents. However, the remedy for violating the Clause did not require the return of all documents seized. Although the Clause required that all privileged materials be returned to the Member, unprivileged materials were properly retained by the prosecution under Fed. R. Crim. P. 41(g) because the member did not demonstrate irreparable harm.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that all legislative materials, originals and copies, that were privileged under the Speech and Debate Clause and seized pursuant to a warrant search of the Member's congressional office, be returned to the Member. The court continued the district court's order barring federal agents who executed the warrant from disclosing the contents of documents seized and from being involved in the criminal prosecution of the Member.

Aeronautical Repair Station Ass'n v. FAA


No. 06-1091, Consolidated with 06-1092


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


494 F.3d 161; 377 U.S. App. D.C. 329; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 16920; 26 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 660


March 28, 2007, Argued 
July 17, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Aeronautical Repair Station Ass'n v. FAA, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 22627 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 21, 2007)
Rehearing denied by Aeronautical Repair Station Ass'n v. FAA, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 22633 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 21, 2007)
Writ of mandamus denied In re Aeronautical Repair Station Ass'n, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 7669 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 13, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of a Final Rule of the Federal Aviation Administration.
Aeronautical Repair Station Ass'n v. FAA, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 7084 (D.C. Cir., Mar. 23, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner companies sought review of a final rule of respondent Federal Aviation Administration (FAA), which amended drug and alcohol testing regulations, on the ground that it impermissibly expanded the scope of employees tested under 49 U.S.C.S. § 45102(a)(1) of the Omnibus Transportation Employee Testing Act (Act); Administrative Procedure Act (APA), 5 U.S.C.S. §§ 701-06; and the Fourth and Fifth Amendments of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The 2006 rule amended FAA regulations to mandate that air carriers require tests of all employees of its contractors and subcontractors, at any tier, who performed safety-related functions. The court upheld the substance of the rule because the FAA reasonably included all subcontractors among the contractors whose employees were "other air carrier employees" subject to testing in 49 U.S.C.S. § 45102(a)(1). The rule did not violate the APA because the FAA gave the companies opportunity to comment and reasonably determined that it was inconsistent with aviation safety to treat employees of certificated and noncertificated contractors differently. The court held that the rule did not subject employees of noncertificated subcontractors to an unreasonable search under the Fourth Amendment or violate the Due Process Clause of the Fifth Amendment because the regulated enterprise had the ability to clarify the rule's meaning. However, the court remanded the case for the FAA to conduct an analysis required by 5 U.S.C.S. § 603(a) of the Regulatory Flexibility Act, treating the contractors and subcontractors as regulated entities who had responsibilities directly imposed on them by the rule.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the substance of the rule but remanded the case for the limited purpose of conducting the analysis required under the Regulatory Flexibility Act, treating the contractors and subcontractors as regulated entities.

Murphy v. IRS


No. 05-5139


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


493 F.3d 170; 377 U.S. App. D.C. 197; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 15816; 2007-2 U.S. Tax Cas. (CCH) P50,531; 100 A.F.T.R.2d (RIA) 5075; 46 A.L.R. Fed. 2d 783


April 23, 2007, Argued 
July 3, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Murphy v. IRS, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 22173 (D.C. Cir., 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Murphy v. IRS, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 3544 (U.S., Apr. 21, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 03cv02414).
Murphy v. IRS, 362 F. Supp. 2d 206, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 4309 (D.D.C., 2005)



DISPOSITION: The panel's ruling was vacated and the judgment of the district court was affirmed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A United States Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit panel, holding that appellant taxpayer's compensatory damages award was not exempt under I.R.C. § 104(a)(2), and not "income" under the Sixteenth Amendment, reversed the grant of summary judgment to appellees United States and Internal Revenue Service (IRS). The government's motion for rehearing en banc was granted.


OVERVIEW: The award was not received on account of personal physical injuries excludable from gross income under I.R.C. § 104(a)(2). Although she may have suffered physical symptoms of stress, the award focused on mental pain and anguish. Gross income under I.R.C. § 61 included compensatory damages for non-physical injuries. The court had to presume that when I.R.C. § 104(a) was amended in 1966, the presumption that it was intended to have real and substantial effect suggested that I.R.C. § 61 was to be read to include an award for nonphysical harms. Thus, § 61 implicitly included a nonphysical damages award such as the taxpayer received, regardless if it was an accession to wealth. A tax upon such damages was within Congress's power. The taxpayer was taxed only after receiving a compensatory award, thus, it was akin to a duty upon the facilities made use of and actually employed in the transaction. The tax laid upon an award of damages for a nonphysical personal injury operated with "the same force and effect" throughout the United States and therefore was uniform under U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 1.


OUTCOME: The panel's ruling was vacated and the judgment of the district court was affirmed.

In re Sealed Case


No. 04-5313


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


494 F.3d 139; 377 U.S. App. D.C. 307; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 17256


December 14, 2006, Argued 
June 29, 2007, Decided 
July 20, 2007, Unsealed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Horn v. Huddle, 636 F. Supp. 2d 10, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 101902 (D.D.C., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 94cv01756).
In re Sealed Case, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 15798 (D.C. Cir., June 29, 2007)



DISPOSITION:  The court reversed the dismissal of the complaint against the State Department employee and remanded the case to the district court to reinstate the complaint against him and consider whether the case could proceed. The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint against the CIA employee. The court vacated the order dismissing, as moot, the attache's motion regarding processing his secretaries for security clearance.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attache sought review of a decision of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, which granted appellee government's Fed. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6) motion to dismiss his Bivens complaint that appellee employees engaged in electronic eavesdropping in violation of the Fourth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The complaint alleged that employees of the State Department and Central Intelligence Agency (CIA) eavesdropped on the attache's telephone conversation. The court affirmed the dismissal of the claim against the classified CIA employee. The court held that the district court, after an ex parte and in camera review, did not abuse its discretion in sustaining the state secrets privilege of the United States as to reports on internal investigations of the attache's allegations because releasing the reports would have risked revealing covert operatives. The court reversed the dismissal of the claim against the State Department employee because it was peripheral to national security concerns and the attache, without using the privileged information, could have established a prima facie case through inconsistencies in the employee's explanation of how he learned of the conversation and the employee's cable that included the attache's conversation in quotation marks and ellipses, creating an inference that the conversation was transcribed. The court held that the district court made no finding that a valid defense for the State Department employee precluded the continuation of the case.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the complaint against the State Department employee and remanded the case to the district court to reinstate the complaint against him and consider whether the case could proceed. The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint against the CIA employee. The court vacated the order dismissing, as moot, the attache's motion regarding processing his secretaries for security clearance.

United States v. Proctor


No. 05-3132


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


489 F.3d 1348; 376 U.S. App. D.C. 512; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 14359


March 27, 2007, Argued  
June 19, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cr00427-01).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of violating 18 U.S.C.S. § 922(g)(1). He appealed the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's denial of his motion to suppress evidence obtained during an inventory search of his vehicle following his arrest for moving violations. He was alone in the vehicle when he was arrested for operating a vehicle while intoxicated (OWI) and driving under the influence (DUI).


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the arresting officers failed to follow standard police procedures when they impounded his car and subsequently retrieved a weapon from a trash bag found in the trunk. Prior to impoundment, defendant was not afforded the opportunity to arrange for the car's removal. An officer testified that the officers were required to impound the vehicle because no one was present to remove it, defendant was not the owner, and the police were not going to wait for the owner to remove it. The officer testified that he did not believe that had a choice but to impound the vehicle. The impoundment decision violated D.C. Metro. Police Gen. Order No. 602.1 (602.1). Additionally, since defendant was arrested for OWI and DUI, the impoundment procedures in the Comprehensive Anti-Drunk Driving Amendments Act of 1991 (Act), D.C. Code Ann. § 50-2201.05(c-1) rendered 602.1's impoundment procedures obsolete. The officers' impoundment decision led to an inventory search that also violated 602.1. Since the vehicle was taken to a private impound lot, a search of the vehicle was expressly forbidden by 602.1.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was reversed.

 

266 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Sheet Metal Workers' Int'l Ass'n, Local 15 v. NLRB


No. 06-1028, Consolidated with 06-1072


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


491 F.3d 429; 377 U.S. App. D.C. 38; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 14361; 182 L.R.R.M. 2009; 154 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P10,861


February 5, 2007, Argued  
June 19, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Costs and fees proceeding at, Request granted Sheet Metal Workers' Int'l Ass'n, Local 15 v. NLRB, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 19201 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 10, 2007)
On remand at, Complaint dismissed at Sheet Metal Workers Int'l Ass'n, Local 15, 2011 NLRB LEXIS 254 (May 26, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Petition for Review and Cross-Application for Enforcement of an Order of the National Labor Relations Board.
Sheet Metal Workers Int'l Ass'n, Local 15, 346 N.L.R.B. 199, 2006 NLRB LEXIS 3 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner union local filed a petition for review of respondent National Labor Relations Board's decision and order finding the union had violated the National Labor Relations Act (NLRA) and barring the union from picketing intervenor hospital and from unqualifiedly threatening to picket respondent employer's premises. The Board cross-applied for enforcement of its order.


OVERVIEW: In the course of a labor dispute with the employer, the union gave written notice to a department store for which the employer was performing mechanical work that the union would be compelled to publicize its dispute at two construction locations. The union also staged a mock funeral at a hospital that was using nonunion workers for a project. The Board found that the union violated the NLRA. On appeal, the court held that the union did not commit an unfair labor practice under 29 U.S.C.S. § 158(b)(4)(ii)(B) because it had not threatened the store but informed it of its intent to lawfully protest. The union had no statutory obligation to assure the hospital, a neutral employer, that its picketing would be lawful or limited to a reserved gate. The mock funeral procession, a type of secondary boycott, was not the functional equivalent of picketing. Although a videotape showed that some protesters carried signs urging the public not to patronize the hospital, the procession was not intimidating because it was orderly and occurred 100 feet from the hospital. Applying a First Amendment analysis, the court held that the demonstration was not prohibited by 29 U.S.C.S. § 158(b)(4)(ii)(B).


OUTCOME: The court granted the union's petition for review of the Board's decision and order and denied the Board's cross-application for enforcement. The court remanded for further proceedings.

 

267 of 475 DOCUMENTS



Doe v. District of Columbia


No. 05-7190


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


489 F.3d 376; 376 U.S. App. D.C. 368; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 13701


February 6, 2007, Argued  
June 12, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Doe v. District of Columbia, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 23603 (D.C. Cir., Oct. 3, 2007)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Doe v. District of Columbia, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 23605 (D.C. Cir., Oct. 3, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 01cv02398).
Jane Does I through III v. District of Columbia, 232 F.R.D. 18, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 28796 (D.D.C., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Class action plaintiffs, intellectually disabled persons who lived in defendant District of Columbia (D.C.) facilities and received medical services from the D.C., appealed a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, permanently enjoining defendant Mental Retardation and Developmental Disabilities Administration (MRDDA) from authorizing elective surgeries for patients under its 2003 policy.


OVERVIEW: The class plaintiffs, who never had the mental capacity to make medical decisions for themselves, argued that the 2003 MRDDA policy violated the Health Care Decisions Act, D.C. Code § 21-2201 et seq., as well as their Fifth Amendment and substantive due process rights, by authorizing surgeries without considering their wishes. The district court agreed. On appeal, the court held that the 2003 policy comported with the Act because the policy permitted the MRDDA administrator to authorize medical decisions only if two physicians certified that a surgery was clinically indicated to maintain the health of the patient; the caregivers had made efforts to discuss the surgery with the patient at the level of patient comprehension; and no guardian, family member, or other close relative, friend, or associate was available to consent. The "best interests" rather than the "known wishes" standard applied, once the administrator had ascertained that a patient was unable to make an informed decision on his or her own behalf, because considering the wishes of a patient who had always lacked mental capacity could result in the incorrect denial of medical treatment or cause death or serious injury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's grant of summary judgment, vacated the district court's injunction, and directed the entry of judgment for D.C. and MRDDA with respect to the class plaintiffs' claims for declaratory and injunctive relief. The court declined to address the class plaintiffs' individual damage claims that stemmed from incidents that had occurred more than 10 years previously.


 

268 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Public Citizen v. United States Dist. Court


No. 06-5232


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


486 F.3d 1342; 376 U.S. App. D.C. 222; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 12342


February 9, 2007, Argued  
May 29, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Public Citizen v. Usdc Dc, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 12929 (U.S., Dec. 10, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 06cv00523).
Public Citizen v. Clerk, United States Dist. Court, 451 F. Supp. 2d 109, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 55740 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant consumer advocacy group sought judicial review of appellee United States District Court for the District of Columbia's dismissal of its complaint challenging the validity of the Deficit Reduction Act of 2005 (Act), Pub. L. No. 109-171, 120 Stat. 4 (2006), and denial of its motion for summary judgment. The district court held that the group's bicameralism claim was foreclosed by the United States Supreme Court's Marshall Field decision.


OVERVIEW: The group asserted that the Act was invalid because the bill that was presented to the President did not first pass both chambers of Congress in the exact same form. It asserted that it was irrelevant that the Speaker of the House and the President pro tempore of the Senate both signed a version of the proposed legislation identical to the version signed by the President. The group also argued that it did not matter that the congressional leaders' signatures attested that indistinguishable legislative text passed both houses. In the present case, the record contained a copy of the Act bearing the signatures of then Speaker of the House of Representatives and the President pro tempore of the Senate. Where such an attested enrolled bill existed, the Marshall Field decision required that the judicial department act upon that assurance and accept the bill as having passed Congress. The group argued unsuccessfully to distinguish and narrow the Marshall Field decision. There was no doubt that the application of the group's theory to the case was positively foreclosed by the Marshall Field decision.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.
