Rumber v. Dist. of Columbia


No. 06-7004


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


487 F.3d 941; 376 U.S. App. D.C. 255; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 12200


April 19, 2007, Argued  
May 25, 2007, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] THIS OPINION IS SUBJECT TO FORMAL REVISION BEFORE PUBLICATION IN THE FEDERAL REPORTER OR U.S.APP.D.C. REPORTS. USERS ARE REQUESTED TO NOTIFY THE CLERK OF ANY FORMAL ERRORS IN ORDER THAT CORRECTIONS MAY BE MADE BEFORE THE BOUND VOLUMES GO TO PRESS.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Franco v. Nat'l Capital Revitalization Corp., 930 A.2d 160, 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 398 (D.C., 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cv01170).
Rumber v. District of Columbia, 427 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 33360 (D.D.C., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case arising from a decision by appellee District of Columbia (District), to renovate a shopping center, appellants, several property owners, tenants, and a shopping center employee, sought judicial review of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's dismissal of their complaint on the ground that the just compensation challenge was not ripe.


OVERVIEW: Although the complaint included a just compensation challenge, a central allegation in the complaint was that the taking would not serve a public purpose. The district court granted the District's Fed. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(1) motion to dismiss, holding that its review of Fifth Amendment challenges was premature until a taking occurred, and the District provided, or fails to provide, compensation. In treating appellants' claims under the Fifth Amendment as just compensation claims, the district court overlooked their public use claim, which was not subject to the Williamson County decision's ripeness requirements. While appellants also raised due process and equal protection claims, since their focus was on their public use claim, only the public use claim was remanded to the district court. Finally, appellant's challenge to the District's authority was without merit.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of appellants' public use claim as unripe was reversed, and that part of the case was remanded. Upon remand, the district court should address the District's other grounds for dismissal of the complaint, including the standing of individual appellants, res judicata as may arise from the condemnation proceedings in the District of Columbia courts, and, in its discretion, abstention.

 

270 of 475 DOCUMENTS


In re Fashina


No. 06-3002


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


486 F.3d 1300; 376 U.S. App. D.C. 180; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 11091


January 18, 2007, Argued  
May 11, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Petition for Leave to File Second or Successive § 2255 Habeas Petition. (No. 94cr00025-03).
United States v. Fashina, 1998 U.S. App. LEXIS 10652 (D.C. Cir., May 4, 1998)
In re Fashina, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 553 (D.C. Cir., Jan. 9, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed for leave to file a second or successive 28 U.S.C.S. § 2255 habeas petition, asserting that his sentence was illegal under Booker. The inmate filed his first habeas petition prior to the passage of the Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act of 1996 (AEDPA) and claimed that this request for leave therefore should be evaluated under the pre-AEDPA "cause and prejudice" standard.


OVERVIEW: The inmate conceded that he did not meet either of the standards in 28 U.S.C.S. § 2255 for filing a second or successive habeas corpus petition. Under the pre-AEDPA standard, the inmate could not show prejudice because Booker was not retroactive. That being the case, the appellate court did not need to consider whether the inmate had shown cause. While Booker announced a new rule, neither exception to the general rule of non-retroactivity applied to make Booker retroactive on collateral review. The inmate contended that Booker was substantive because it made the provisions of the U.S. Sentencing Guidelines that altered the applicable sentencing range based on the finding of a particular fact an element of the underlying crime that must be proved beyond a reasonable doubt to a jury. That contention misapprehended the significance of Booker. As to whether Booker was a watershed rule, the inmate argued that Booker was on par with Gideon. Both his arguments in support of the contention failed. Booker did not meet the criteria for retroactive application.


OUTCOME: The petition was denied.

We the People Found., Inc. v. United States


No. 05-5359


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


485 F.3d 140; 376 U.S. App. D.C. 117; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 10849; 2007-1 U.S. Tax Cas. (CCH) P50,523; 99 A.F.T.R.2d (RIA) 2678


October 6, 2006, Argued  
May 8, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*140] 
Motion dismissed by We the People Found., Inc. v. United States, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 11359 (D.C. Cir., May 11, 2007)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by We the People Found., Inc. v. United States, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 18716 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 3, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Schulz v. United States, 552 U.S. 1102, 128 S. Ct. 939, 169 L. Ed. 2d 733, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 47 (2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by We the People Found., Inc. v. United States, 552 U.S. 1102, 128 S. Ct. 939, 169 L. Ed. 2d 733, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 181 (2008)
Related proceeding at Azis v. United States IRS, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 99402 (S.D. Fla., May 3, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cv01211).
We the People v. United States, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 20409 (D.D.C., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, numerous individuals and an organization, appealed a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia that alleged that defendant United States violated their right to petition for redress of grievances under the First Amendment. The suit sought an injunction permitting plaintiffs to withhold payment of income taxes until their petitions were addressed.


OVERVIEW: Since 1999, plaintiffs engaged in a nationwide effort to get the government to answer specific questions regarding what plaintiffs viewed as the government's violation of the taxing, war powers, money, and privacy clauses of the Constitution. Plaintiffs submitted petitions with extensive lists of inquiries to various government agencies in the Legislative and Executive Branches but never received responses. In particular, plaintiffs contended that the President, the Attorney General, the Secretary of the Treasury, the Commissioner of the Internal Revenue Service, and Congress neglected their responsibilities under the First Amendment to respond to plaintiffs' petitions. The court disagreed. U.S. Supreme Court precedent unequivocally held that the First Amendment's Petition Clause did not provide a right to a response or official consideration. The Anti-Injunction Act, 26 U.S.C.S. § 7421(a), precluded plaintiffs' second claim, which sought to enjoin the government from the collection of taxes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

Boehner v. McDermott


No. 04-7203


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


484 F.3d 573; 376 U.S. App. D.C. 75; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 10001; 35 Media L. Rep. 1705; 38 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 130


October 31, 2006 & January 25, 2007, Argued  
May 1, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McDermott v. Boehner, 128 S. Ct. 712, 169 L. Ed. 2d 571, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 12832 (U.S., 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 98cv00594).
Boehner v. McDermott, 332 F. Supp. 2d 149, 2004 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 16442 (D.D.C., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A United States Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit panel affirmed a grant of summary judgment to plaintiff United States House of Representatives member against defendant opposition member, on a claim that the opposition member violated 18 U.S.C.S. § 2511(1)(c) by disclosing a tape recording of an illegally intercepted conversation in which the member had participated. The decision was vacated and the case was reheard en banc.


OVERVIEW: Assuming, arguendo, that the opposition member lawfully obtained the tape, the en banc court formed the question as whether the opposition member had a First Amendment right to disclose it to the media at that time given the circumstances of his receipt of the tape, the ongoing proceedings before the House Committee on Standards of Official Conduct (Committee) regarding another member who also had participated in the taped conversation, and the opposition member's position as a member of the Committee. The opposition member was subject to the Committee's rules on disclosing information and the House of Representatives' ethics rules. Just as the First Amendment did not protect him from House disciplinary proceedings, it did not protect him from civil suit liability, which rested on a violation of a federal statute. When he became a member of the Committee, he accepted a duty of confidentiality that covered his receipt and handling of the recording. He thus had no First Amendment right to disclose the tape to the media. And, a subcommittee had found that the disclosure not only violated the spirit of the rules but also the opposition member's obligations as Ranking Minority Member.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

273 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Powell


No. 05-3047


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


483 F.3d 836; 376 U.S. App. D.C. 30; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 8690


January 25, 2007, Argued  
April 17, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Powell v. United States, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 12424 (U.S., Nov. 26, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cr00164-01).
United States v. Powell, 371 U.S. App. D.C. 347, 451 F.3d 862, 2006 U.S. App. LEXIS 15763 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The government filed a motion seeking a rehearing en banc of an appellate panel's decision holding that a warrantless search of defendant's car was not a search incident to arrest, an exception to the Fourth Amendment's warrant requirement, and reversing a judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia that denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: Police officers saw defendant urinating outside a car in an industrial area in violation of D.C. Code § 22-1321 (2001). Noticing a passenger inside the car, an officer leaned part of his upper body inside the open window and, using a flashlight, saw open containers of an alcoholic beverage. A vehicle search revealed a loaded pistol in a backpack on the back seat. Defendant was charged with a firearms violation, possession of an open container of alcohol, and urinating in public. The district court denied his motion to suppress evidence based on an illegal warrantless search. The panel majority reversed, reasoning that even when police had probable cause to arrest, they could not conduct a warrantless search incident to arrest before taking a suspect into custody. Sitting en banc, the appeals court disagreed. Under the U.S. Supreme Court's Rawlings rule, a warrantless search could constitutionally precede an arrest as long as a formal arrest followed quickly on the heels of the search. Police reasonably inferred that defendant was a recent occupant of the car. As the officers had probable cause to arrest defendant for urinating in a public place, the search was valid.


OUTCOME: The en banc court reversed the panel's decision, held that the district court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress, and affirmed the judgment of conviction.

United States v. Watson


No. 04-3082 & 04-3090


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


483 F.3d 828; 376 U.S. App. D.C. 22; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 8467


November 17, 2006, Argued  
April 13, 2007, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] THIS OPINION IS SUBJECT TO FORMAL REVISION BEFORE PUBLICATION IN THE FEDERAL REPORTER OR U.S. APP. D.C. REPORTS. USERS ARE REQUESTED TO NOTIFY THE CLERK OF ANY FORMAL ERRORS IN ORDER THAT CORRECTIONS MAY BE MADE BEFORE THE BOUND VOLUMES GO TO PRESS.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by, Subsequent appeal at United States v. Watson, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 28104 (D.C. Cir., May 12, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 03cr00146-01).
United States v. Watson, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 13780 (D.C. Cir., July 1, 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for violating 18 U.S.C.S. §§ 844(e) and 1361, arguing that the rule established in the Batson decision requiring heightened scrutiny of peremptory challenges on the basis of race should be extended to the blind. The government appealed the sentence imposed by the United States District Court for the District of Columbia following resentencing in light of the Blakely decision.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought extension of the Batson rule to the blind, on the ground that, in the Lane decision, the United States Supreme Court recognized that states deprived disabled individuals of a fundamental right by excluding them from jury service. The reasoning in the Lane decision did not support defendant's contention that the exercise of peremptory challenges of individual jurors on the basis of disability related to the particular case triggered heightened scrutiny or violated the Batson rule. Defendant's alternate contention failed that the prosecutor's peremptory strikes were not rational because the charges against him did not rely on visual evidence and to the extent the government chose to introduce such evidence it was, by reason of technical defects, virtually useless to the jury. It was not necessarily irrational to think that a person was likely to acquire a more accurate understanding of a scene by seeing it rather than merely hearing about it. The government argued that the district court committed a Fanfan error at resentencing. The district court plainly erred by failing to appreciate the sentencing authority that it retained after the Booker decision.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed, but the case was remanded for resentencing.

 

275 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Andrews


No. 03-3030


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


479 F.3d 894; 375 U.S. App. D.C. 247; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 6754


February 6, 2007, Argued  
March 23, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Andrews v. United States, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 10714 (U.S., Oct. 1, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 02cr00255-01).
United States v. Andrews, 2005 U.S. App. LEXIS 20242 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 19, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was convicted of being a felon in possession of a firearm under 18 U.S.C.S. § 922(g)(1). In determining his sentence, the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia found that his prior conviction of first-degree sexual abuse of a ward constituted a crime of violence, U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 4B1.2(a), which led to a significant enhancement under U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 2K2.1(b)(2). This appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant did not challenge the finding that first-degree sexual abuse of a ward was a crime of violence. Thus, the issue was reviewed for plain error only, Fed. R. Crim. P. 52(b), and was limited to whether oral sex with a ward, as a categorical determination, presented a serious potential risk of physical injury to another, U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 4B1.2(a)(2). Two factors, sufficient to withstand plain error review, suggested that the crime, as defined in D.C. Code §§ 22-3013 (formerly D.C. Code § 22-4113) and § 22-4101(8) (1998) (repealed 2000), presented a serious potential risk of physical injury. First, there was a potential risk of injury that was inherent in the coercive nature of the supervisor-ward relationship. Since consent was not a defense to the crime, D.C. Code § 22-3017 (formerly D.C. Code § 22-4117), District of Columbia legislators apparently believed there was an inherent power imbalance in the relationship. There was also a risk of exposing the victim to sexually transmitted infections. It was not obvious, then, that first-degree sexual abuse of a ward did not constitute a crime of violence, and the district court did not plainly err in so finding.


OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was affirmed.

Wilburn v. Robinson


No. 05-7110


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


480 F.3d 1140; 375 U.S. App. D.C. 257; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 6397; 89 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P42,822; 25 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1532; 68 Fed. R. Serv. 3d (Callaghan) 1218


October 10, 2006, Argued  
March 20, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Motion granted by Wilburn v. Robinson, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 12473 (D.C. Cir., May 24, 2007)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Wilburn v. Robinson, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 18704 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 3, 2007)
Rehearing denied by Wilburn v. Robinson, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 18708 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 3, 2007)



DISPOSITION:  The court affirmed the district court's judgment.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public employee filed a 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action against appellee, a mayor's former chief of staff, in his individual and official capacities. The employee alleged that she was not selected for a position in retaliation for the exercise of her First Amendment rights. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia granted the chief of staff's motion for summary judgment. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: While acting as the interim director of the District of Columbia's Office of Human Rights (OHR), the employee suggested that the denial of her salary requests for OHR job applicants was motivated by the racial and gender discrimination. The employee claimed that her supervisor told her that the employee was not selected to be the permanent director due to the chief of staff's retaliation against the employee for her discrimination allegations. Although the employee's notice of appeal was not filed within 30 days of the summary judgment order, the court determined that the appeal was timely because the employee's Fed. R. Civ. P. 60(b) motion for reconsideration was deemed to have tolled the 30-day period where the chief of staff failed to assert the timeliness defense afforded by Fed. R. App. P. 4(a)(4)(A)(vi). The court found that the district court erred in granting summary judgment based on its conclusion that the supervisor's statements to the employee were not admissible as an admission of the District. The court held that the employee could not establish a First Amendment claim because the employee did not speak as a citizen when she made the discrimination allegations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment.

Parker v. District of Columbia


No. 04-7041


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


478 F.3d 370; 375 U.S. App. D.C. 140; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 5519


December 7, 2006, Argued  
March 9, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Parker v. District of Columbia, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 11029 (D.C. Cir., May 8, 2007)
Stay granted by Parker v. District of Columbia, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 12467 (D.C. Cir., May 24, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by District of Columbia v. Heller, 552 U.S. 1035, 128 S. Ct. 645, 169 L. Ed. 2d 417, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 12324 (2007)
Affirmed by District of Columbia v. Heller, 554 U.S. 570, 128 S. Ct. 2783, 171 L. Ed. 2d 637, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 5268 (2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Parker v. District of Columbia, 554 U.S. 932, 128 S. Ct. 2994, 171 L. Ed. 2d 910, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 5279 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 03cv00213).
Parker v. District of Columbia, 311 F. Supp. 2d 103, 2004 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 5268 (D.D.C., 2004)



DISPOSITION: The appellate court held that only one resident, a special police officer had standing. The district court's judgment was reversed and the case was remanded with an order to grant summary judgment to the special police officer.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant District of Columbia (District) residents challenged appellee District's gun laws, D.C. Code §§ 7-2502.02(a)(4), 7-2507.02, 22-4504 (insofar as § 22-4504 prevented carrying a pistol in a registrant's home). The United States District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed the complaint, holding that the Second Amendment bestowed no individual rights except, perhaps, when serving in an organized militia such as the National Guard.


OVERVIEW: There were no allegations the residents were singled out for prosecution and nothing showed they were punished for their challenges. Only one resident, a police officer, had applied for and been denied a handgun registration. Only he had an injury independent of prospective enforcement, and an injury to which the pre-enforcement standing requirements did not apply. Since D.C. Code §§ 7-2507.02, 22-4504, amounted to further conditions on his registration, he had standing. Handgun registration was not completely prohibited. "The right of the people," in the Second Amendment, when read intratextually and in light of United States Supreme Court precedent, led to the conclusion that the right was individual. All other Bill of Rights provisions, excepting the Tenth Amendment, protected rights citizens enjoyed in their individual capacity. The Second Amendment's operative clause included a private meaning for "bear Arms." D.C. Code § 7-2507.02 would reduce a pistol to being useless. It, like the bar on carrying a pistol in the home under D.C. Code § 22-4504, amounted to a complete unconstitutional prohibition on the lawful use of handguns for self-defense.


OUTCOME: The appellate court held that only one resident, a special police officer had standing. The district court's judgment was reversed and the case was remanded with an order to grant summary judgment to the special police officer.

Decatur Liquors, Inc. v. District of Columbia


No. 05-7099 Consolidated with 05-7100


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


478 F.3d 360; 375 U.S. App. D.C. 130; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 4240


February 2, 2007, Argued  
February 27, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Decatur Liquors, Inc. v. District of Columbia, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 9982 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 24, 2007)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Decatur Liquors, Inc. v. District of Columbia, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 9984 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 24, 2007)
Rehearing denied by Decatur Liquors, Inc. v. District of Columbia, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 9989 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 24, 2007)
Costs and fees proceeding at, Request granted Decatur Liquors, Inc. v. District of Columbia, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 9992 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 25, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cv01971).
DeCatur Liquors, Inc. v. District of Columbia, 384 F. Supp. 2d 58, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 15649 (D.D.C., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the United States District Court for the District of Columbia granted summary judgment in favor of appellee liquor licensees on their claim that the Omnibus Alcoholic Beverage Amendment Act of 2004, D.C. Code § 25-341 (2004), violated the District of Columbia's (District) Home Rule Act's (Act) two reading requirement, appellants, the District and the Alcoholic Beverage Regulation Administration, sought judicial review.


OVERVIEW: Although the Act contained elements of federal law, neither party suggested that the two readings requirement was anything other than local law. The challenged action was a moratorium on the sale of certain single container liquor sales within a particular ward of the District. The licensees' most substantial claim that the District violated the Fifth Amendment's Due Process Clause by denying the affected licensees individualized notice and an opportunity to be heard was foreclosed by the United States Supreme Court's Bi-Metallic decision. The licensees' allegations that: (1) there was a violation of the substantive component of the Fifth Amendment's Due Process Clause, (2) the moratorium violated the equal protection component of the Fifth Amendment's Due Process Clause, (3) the moratorium effected an unconstitutional taking under the Fifth Amendment, and (4) the statute was void for vagueness all failed. In light of the insubstantiality of the licensees' federal claims, the district court lacked federal-question jurisdiction over those claims and thus supplemental jurisdiction over the claim under the Act.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was reversed, and the case was remanded with instructions to dismiss the licensees' claim under the Act without prejudice.

DKT Int'l, Inc. v. United States Agency for Int'l Dev.


No. 06-5225


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


477 F.3d 758; 375 U.S. App. D.C. 123; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 4241; 46 A.L.R. Fed. 2d 807


January 11, 2007, Argued  
February 27, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Dkt Int'l v. United States Agency for Int'l Dev., 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 10551 (D.C. Cir., May 2, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 05cv01604).
DKT Int'l v. United States Agency for Int'l Dev., 435 F. Supp. 2d 5, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 30207 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the United States Agency for International Development and its administrator (collectively USAID), sought review of a decision of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, which struck down a funding condition under 22 U.S.C.S. § 7631(f) of United States Leadership Against HIV/AIDS, Tuberculosis, and Malaria Act. Appellee, an international organization, was denied funding for its refusal to comply with provision.


OVERVIEW: The organization provided family planning and HIV/AIDS prevention programming in foreign countries and refused to certify that it had a policy opposing prostitution, as required under § 7631(f). The district court found that the certification violated the organization's First Amendment rights. On review, the court reversed. The government's objective under 22 U.S.C.S. § 7601 of the Act was to eradicate HIV/AID, and a means for accomplishing the objective was to speak out against legalizing prostitution and to foster behavioral change under § 7601(22)(E) and to spread educational messages under 22 U.S.C.S. § 7611. The USAID did not violate the First Amendment because the government's own message was being delivered and the government was entitled to take appropriate steps to ensure that its message was neither garbled nor distorted, especially where the government was speaking on matters with foreign policy implications. The Act did not compel the organization to advocate the government's position on prostitution and sex trafficking; it only required that if the organization wanted to receive grant funds, it had to communicate the message the government chose to fund.


OUTCOME: We therefore reverse the district court.

Boumediene v. Bush


No. 05-5062, Consolidated with 05-5063, No. 05-5064, Consolidated with 05-5095, 05-5096, 05-5097, 05-5098, 05-5099, 05-5100, 05-5101, 05-5102, 05- 5103, 05-5104, 05-5105, 05-5106, 05-5107, 05-5108, 05-5109, 05-5110, 05-5111, 05- 5112, 05-5113, 05-5114, 05-5115, 05-5116


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Boumediene v. Bush, 549 U.S. 1328, 127 S. Ct. 1478, 167 L. Ed. 2d 578, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 3783 (2007)
Subsequent appeal at, Motion granted by, in part Odah v. United States, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 8643 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 11, 2007)

Vacated by, Remanded by Boumediene v. Bush, 282 Fed. Appx. 844, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 13617 (D.C. Cir., 2008)
Vacated by, Remanded by Khaled A. F. Al Odah v. United States, 282 Fed. Appx. 844, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 13616 (D.C. Cir., 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, (No. 04cv01142) (No. 04cv01166). Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, (No. 02cv00828) (No. 02cv00299) (No. 02cv01130) (No. 02cv01135) (No. 02cv01136) (No. 02cv01137) (No. 02cv01144) (No. 02cv01164) (No. 02cv01194) (No. 02cv01227) (No. 02cv01254).
Khalid v. Bush, 355 F. Supp. 2d 311, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 749 (D.D.C., 2005)
In re Guantanamo Detainee Cases, 355 F. Supp. 2d 443, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 1236 (D.D.C., 2005)



DISPOSITION: The district courts' decisions were vacated, and the detainees' cases were dismissed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants and cross-appellants, alien detainees, filed habeas corpus petitions in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. The dismissal of some detainees' claims was reversed; on remand to the district court, those detainees' claims were dismissed in part. Other detainees' cases were dismissed in their entirety. In consolidated appeals, the detainees and the government sought review.


OVERVIEW: The detainees were aliens who were captured outside the United States and detained as enemy combatants at the Guantanamo Bay Naval Base in Cuba. The court of appeals found that the Military Commissions Act of 2006 (MCA) amended 28 U.S.C.S. § 2241(e) so as to eliminate jurisdiction in the federal courts over the detainees' claims. The MCA, Pub. L. No. 109-366, § 7(b), 120 Stat. 2600, provided that the amendment to the habeas statute was applicable to all cases, without exception, that were pending on or after the date of the MCA's enactment where those cases related to any aspect of the detention of an alien who had been detained by the United States as an enemy combatant since September 11, 2001. The MCA's deprivation of jurisdiction over the detainees' habeas petitions did not violate the Suspension Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 9, cl. 2; the Suspension Clause protected the writ as it existed in 1789, and habeas corpus would not have been available in 1789 to aliens held at an overseas military base leased from a foreign government. Further, the U.S. Constitution did not confer rights on aliens without property or presence within the United States.


OUTCOME: The district courts' decisions were vacated, and the detainees' cases were dismissed.

 

281 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Pickett


No. 05-3179


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


475 F.3d 1347; 374 U.S. App. D.C. 476; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 3272


November 20, 2006, Argued  
February 13, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by United States v. Pickett, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 7829 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 3, 2007)
Rehearing denied by United States v. Pickett, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 12225 (D.C. Cir., May 21, 2007)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by United States v. Pickett, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 12231 (D.C. Cir., May 21, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by United States v. Pickett, 128 S. Ct. 870, 169 L. Ed. 2d 785, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 588 (U.S., Jan. 7, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 01cr00181-01).
United States v. Pickett, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 8040 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 21, 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to distributing more than five grams of crack cocaine, in violation of 21 U.S.C.S. § 841(a)(1) , (b)(1)(B)(iii). On remand, the United States District Court for the District of Columbia sentenced him to 121 months of imprisonment. Defendant appealed his sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant agreed that for sentencing purposes he was accountable for more than 50 grams but less than 150 grams of crack. Using the U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual (Guidelines), the district court, on remand, calculated defendant's sentencing range as 121 to 151 months, and sentenced him to 121 months. The issue on appeal was whether the district court committed a legal error when it declined to consider the 100-to-1 ratio perpetuated in U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 2D1.1 and the problems it raised in sentencing crack cocaine dealers. The appellate court found that the district court erred when it refused to evaluate whether sentencing defendant in accordance with § 2D1.1, and its 100-to-1 ratio, would effectuate the purposes of sentencing set forth in 18 U.S.C.S. § 3553(a). The Sentencing Commission had candidly and forthrightly exposed the weaknesses and failings of its Guideline with respect to crack cocaine sentencing. That sentences after Booker would not be as uniform as sentences before Booker was doubtless true, but this was a consequence of the United States Supreme Court's decision to treat the Guidelines as advisory rather than mandatory.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was vacated and the case was remanded for resentencing.

 

282 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Islamic Am. Relief Agency v. Gonzales


No. 05-5447


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


477 F.3d 728; 375 U.S. App. D.C. 93; 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 3269


November 20, 2006, Argued  
February 13, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Islamic Am. Relief Agency v. Keisler, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 10499 (U.S., Oct. 1, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cv02264).
Islamic Am. Relief Agency v. Unidentified FBI Agents, 394 F. Supp. 2d 34, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 21570 (D.D.C., 2005)



DISPOSITION: The court affirmed in all respects except that it remanded to the district court to consider whether the relief agency should be permitted to amend its complaint.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, an Islamic relief agency, appealed a summary judgment from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia in favor of government defendants including the Office of Foreign Assets Control in the Department of the Treasury (OFAC), in the agency's Administrative Procedure Act, 5 U.S.C.S. § 701 et seq., suit challenging the blocking of its assets after its parent organization was designated a sponsor of terrorism.


OVERVIEW: The assets of the relief agency, which was located in the U.S., were blocked after its parent organization, which was located overseas, was designated a Specially Designated Global Terrorist (SDGT). Although the relief agency was not independently designated an SDGT, OPAC considered it to be a branch office of the designated organization. On an issue of first impression. the court rejected the relief agency's argument that the "alias test," which required proof of whether a designated organization controlled a non-designated organization, applied. The government's theory was that the relief agency was a branch office of a single global terrorist organization. Reviewing the unclassified record, the court agreed. The International Emergency Economic Powers Act, 50 U.S.C.S. § 1701(a)(1)(B), granted OPAC the authority to block terrorist assets. Under the APA's deferential standard of review, the government defendants did not act arbitrarily. Under the First and Fifth Amendments, the agency had no protected right to fund terrorism. However, under Fed. R. Civ. P. 15(a), the district court failed to consider whether the agency could amend its complaint to add a prayer for attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in all respects except that it remanded to the district court to consider whether the relief agency should be permitted to amend its complaint.

United States v. Lewis


No. 13-CO-1456


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


147 A.3d 236; 2016 D.C. App. LEXIS 369


June 30, 2015, Argued En Banc 
September 29, 2016, Decided 
September 29, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-10190-13). (Hon. Robert I. Richter, Trial Judge).
United States v. Nash, 100 A.3d 157, 2014 D.C. App. LEXIS 393 (D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the trial court erred in suppressing evidence as the fruit of an unlawful search under the Fourth Amendment. HOLDINGS: [1]-Under Arizona v. Gant, the vehicle search was lawful as incident to the arrest of a passenger of the car defendant was driving, since officers had probable cause to arrest the passenger for possession of an open container of alcohol and were thus entitled to search the car for evidence of the offense of arrest, even though the search preceded the passenger's arrest, since the arrest followed quickly on the heels of the search; [2]-The fact that it was unclear whether, at the time of the search, the officers intended to arrest the passenger was immaterial because the legality of the search was determined based on the objective circumstances, not the officers' subjective intent.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed.

February 18, 2016, Filed

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Because the police seized defendant without reasonable articulable suspicion, and because that unconstitutional intrusion in violation of the Fourth Amendment led to the recovery of brass knuckles and to the subsequent search of defendant's person and his car incident to arrest, a new trial was required at which the fruits of the impermissible seizure would be suppressed; [2]-Defendant was seized for Fourth Amendment purposes when he was stepped out of his car and his seizure was not justified by the specific and articulable facts required under Terry v. Muir v. D.C.


No. 11-CT-1619


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


129 A.3d 265; 2016 D.C. App. LEXIS 1


June 25, 2015, Submitted 
January 14, 2016, Decided 
January 14, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CTF-4447-09). (Hon. Anthony C. Epstein, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-It was reasonably probable that instructional error misled the jury and caused it to convict defendant of OWI (D.C. Code § 50-2201.05(b)(2) (2009)) even though the jury actually found the government failed to prove an essential element of the offense beyond a reasonable doubt, as the instruction told the jury that it could find defendant guilty of OWI if he was impaired by the consumption of alcohol in any way, in contrast to the "appreciable degree" of impairment required to find him guilty of DUI; there was no question that such an error seriously undermined the fairness of the proceeding, as the jury's acquittal of defendant on an DUI charge showed there was a reasonable probability that but for the erroneous instruction, the jury would have had reasonable doubt respecting defendant's guilt of OWI.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed and remanded.

Blair v. United States

DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


114 A.3d 960; 2015 D.C. App. LEXIS 166


April 9, 2014, Argued 
May 7, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Blair v. Blair, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 2355 (U.S., Apr. 4, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF1-18057-11). (Hon. Thomas J. Motley, Motion Judge). (Hon. Ronna Lee Beck, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where a prison employee took defendant's DNA sample in violation of 42 U.S.C.S. § 14135b, and the FBI matched defendant's DNA to that found on a sexual assault victim, the judge who granted the government's warrant application properly declined to apply the exclusionary rule, as he found that government officials had acted in good faith and that application of exclusionary rule would not deter conduct; moreover, by the time defendant was arrested, D.C. Code § 22-4151 had been amended such that the taking of his DNA would have been lawful; [2]-The evidence was sufficient to convict defendant of first-degree sexual abuse (D.C. Code § 22-3002(a)(1)), as the victim's testimony that her assailant pushed "into" her and then tried to push himself "further inside" of her permitted the jury to infer that defendant penetrated her labia majora, the outer area of her vulva.


OUTCOME: Defendants' convictions were affirmed.

 

297 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Stackhouse v. D.C. Dep't of Empl. Servs.


No. 13-AA-1481


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


111 A.3d 636; 2015 D.C. App. LEXIS 96


January 13, 2015, Submitted 
March 19, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] (CRB-108-13).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The District of Columbia Compensation Review Board (CRB) reasonably interpreted D.C. Code § 1-623.23(d) (2012) as implying that a workers' compensation claimant's right to benefits was lost during the period between a medical examination that he missed and a later medical examination that he attended as D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 7, § 124.9 (2015) provided that benefits were to be reinstated as of the date of compliance, but did not mention retroactive payments; [2]-The Federal Employees' Compensation Appeals Board had interpreted identical language in 5 U.S.C.S. § 8123(d) (2012) to preclude retroactive payment of benefits that were suspended for failure to submit to a medical examination; [3]-The claimant's argument that the failure to provide him retroactive benefits constituted a taking without just compensation under the Fifth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. V, was rejected.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.

Fadul v. District of Columbia


No. 13-CT-226


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


106 A.3d 1093; 2015 D.C. App. LEXIS 11


November 18, 2014, Submitted 
January 22, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CTF-18039-12). (Hon. Truman A. Morrison III, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 16 did not apply to the failure to preserve a radio run; [2]-Assuming the Jencks Act, 18 U.S.C.S. § 3500, was violated, no sanctions against the government were required as the failure to provide the radio run calling for an alcohol-certified officer was not due to the government's negligence or bad faith, and the potential Jencks recording was unlikely to contain any material description of the facts; [2]-Sufficient evidence that defendant operated or was in physical control of the vehicle under D.C. Code § 50-2206.11 (2012) supported defendant's conviction as he was sleeping in the driver's seat of a parked car with the engine idling; [3]-Section 50-2206.11 was not unconstitutionally vague as defendant could have reasonably understood that sleeping in the driver's seat of a parked car with the engine idling constituted operation of the vehicle.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Thanos v. District of Columbia


Nos. 12-CV-324 & 12-CV-325


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


109 A.3d 1084; 2014 D.C. App. LEXIS 527


January 22, 2014, Argued 
December 31, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB 6897-08 & CAB 856-11). (Hon. Brook Hedge, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court properly found that appellant's property was a prostitution-related nuisance under the Drug or Prostitution-Related Nuisance Abatement Act, D.C. Code § 42-3101 et seq., and entered a permanent injunction because the evidence established that appellant knew its tenant used the property for prostitution, and that the community was adversely impacted by customers banging on the door in the middle of the night; [2]-That the District was represented by a lawyer acting pro bono did not bar the court from awarding it counsel fees under D.C. Code § 42-3110(b)(1); [3]-As § 42-3110(b)(7) authorized the court to order income disgorgement as an equitable remedy to deprive appellant of its ill-gotten revenues on the theory that it should not be permitted to retain the spoils from a prostitution-related nuisance, the court erred in holding that it lacked such authority.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part and reversed in part.

United Dominion Mgmt. Co. v. District of Columbia Rental Hous. Comm'n


Nos. 13-AA-613, 13-AA-959 & 13-AA-960


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


101 A.3d 426; 2014 D.C. App. LEXIS 433

October 16, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petitions for Review of the District of Columbia Rental Housing Commission. (RH-TP-06-28728; RH-TP-06-28707; RH-TP-06-28749).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In affirming the Office of Administrative Hearings' (OAH's) determination that a landlord's purported rent increases were ineffective, the Rental Housing Commission (RHC) erred when it stated that its review of the OAH's statutory interpretation was deferential, but the error was harmless because the RHC in fact conducted a de novo review of the legal issues; [2]-The landlord filed an untimely amended registration form for a claimed rent ceiling increase and over three years later raised the rent under the improperly perfected rent ceiling increase; the RHC properly found that the tenants' challenge to the rent increase was not untimely because D.C. Code § 42-3502.06(e)'s three-year statute of limitations did not begin to run when the landlord filed the amended registration form, but from the date of the demanded rent increase.


OUTCOME: The RHC's orders were affirmed.

 

301 of 475 DOCUMENTS


In re J.W.


No. 12-FS-1771


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


100 A.3d 1091; 2014 D.C. App. LEXIS 430


September 29, 2014, Submitted 
October 9, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (DEL-1326-12). (Hon. Robert R. Rigsby, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court erred in finding that defendant was involved in possessing the implements of crime in violation of D.C. Code § 22-2501 (2012 Repl.) because the statute did not apply to the bolt cutters he possessed; [2]-The statute dealt with the picking of locks, and it seemed likely that defendant would have used the bolt-cutters to sever the chain that secured a scooter rather than to break the lock, but even if the lock was his target, "picking a lock" was generally understood to require skill rather than brute force; [3]-The term "picking a lock" meant the opening of the lock without the use of the original or duplicate keys and without damage to the lock, and bolt cutters were not "lock-picking tools" within the definition.


OUTCOME: Adjudication for possessing implements of crime vacated.

Brocksmith v. United States


No. 12-CF-287


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


99 A.3d 690; 2014 D.C. App. LEXIS 383


May 28, 2014, Argued 
September 18, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF3-14622-11). (Hon. Thomas J. Motley, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a prosecution for assault with intent to commit robbery, in response to a jury question as to whether, in determining the complainant's credibility, the jury could consider she had an incentive not to report the crime for fear exposing herself as a transgender person, the trial court reinstructed the jury it could draw such reasonable inferences from the evidence it felt were justified in light of its experience. The reinstruction did not encourage the jury to engage in improper speculation, as there was evidence from which it could reasonably infer the victim feared reporting the crime due to her gender status; [2]-Though the court erred by not advising defendant after his conviction, pursuant to D.C. OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Whitfield v. United States


No. 11-CF-1451


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


99 A.3d 650; 2014 D.C. App. LEXIS 380


December 18, 2013, Argued 
September 18, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-3007-10). (Hon. Anthony C. Epstein, Motion to Suppress Judge). (Hon. Robert E. Morin, Motion to Dismiss Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion to suppress a gun and ammunition, after which he pled guilty to weapons offenses. HOLDINGS: [1]-Police stopped his vehicle for having a partially obstructed Texas license plate; applying the rule of lenity, the appellate court held that D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 18, §§ 422.5 and 422.6 were ambiguous and prohibited only materials that obstructed the identifying information on the tag itself, not license plate frames that left that information legible and unobstructed; [2]-As the license plate cover on defendant's vehicle did not obscure any part of the plate's identifying information, which did not include the motto "Lone Star State" at the bottom of the plate, police did not have reasonable, articulable suspicion under the Fourth Amendment to stop his vehicle; therefore, his motion to suppress should have been granted.


OUTCOME: The conviction was reversed.

Biotechpharma, LLC v. Ludwig & Robinson, PLLC


No. 13-CV-546


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


98 A.3d 986; 2014 D.C. App. LEXIS 368


October 31, 2013, Argued 
September 4, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB-884-13). (Hon. Brian F. Holeman, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the trial court erred in denying former clients' motion to stay the litigation and compel arbitration of appellee law firm's suit seeking unpaid fees. [1]-The clients had not been required to file a formal petition with the District of Columbia Attorney/Client Arbitration Board in order to request arbitration of the fee dispute; [2]-As the clients signaled their desire for arbitration by filing the motion to compel arbitration, they "requested" arbitration under D.C. Bar R. XIII; accordingly, the firm was deemed to have agreed to arbitrate the fee dispute; [3]-The court of appeals did not exceed its authority in promulgating Rule XIII; [4]-Rule XIII did not violate the firm's Seventh Amendment right to a jury trial, as it gave up that right by practicing law in the District of Columbia.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order was vacated.

Chase Plaza Condo. Ass'n v. JP Morgan Chase Bank, N.A.


Nos. 13-CV-623 & 13-CV-674


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


98 A.3d 166; 2014 D.C. App. LEXIS 317


April 17, 2014, Argued 
August 28, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CA-5826-10). (Hon. Craig Iscoe, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-It was error to grant a bank summary judgment in its action alleging that a foreclosure sale was void because the District of Columbia Condominium Act, D.C. Code § 42-1903.13(a)(2) (2012 Repl.), general principles of foreclosure law, and legislative history supported a conclusion that the condominium association's foreclosure of a condominium unit pursuant to a super-priority lien extinguished the bank's first deed of trust; [2]-A condominium association could extinguish a first deed of trust by foreclosing on its six-month super-priority lien under § 42-1903.13(a)(2); [3]-The terms "senior lien" and "junior lien" were one way of referring to liens with higher and lower priority, and the absence of those terms from § 42-1903.13(a)(2) did not affect the applicability of the rule that foreclosure on a lien with greater priority extinguished liens with lower priority.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment reversed.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A trial court properly denied guarantors' motion to vacate a default judgment that was entered in favor of a lender, arising from its action to enforce their guaranty obligations on a defaulted loan, as the lender's suit was not barred by the restriction on the right to sue contained in D.C. Code § 29-105.02(b) (2012 Repl.) because that statute was preempted by the National Bank Act, 12 U.S.C.S. § 24; [2]-The trial court properly denied the guarantors' request to vacate the default judgment upon consideration of the factors, including their lack of good faith and diligence, their actual notice and concession of the lender's valid claim, and the prejudice to the lender.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Appellant established that he was prejudiced by a plain Apprendi error because reasonable minds could have disagreed about whether the murder was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel; [2]-Although appellant's homicide was without legal justification, it was not committed at random against an unsuspecting member of the community but rather in revenge against the perpetrator of an earlier crime; [3]-Appellant's crime was not clearly within the class of murders discussed by the Committee on the Judiciary when the Council of the District of Columbia enacted its life without possibility of parole (LWOP) statute; [4]-The public reputation of judicial proceedings would suffer if appellant's sentence was allowed to stand because the jury had not made findings coextensive with any of the three aggravating factors on which the trial could based the imposition of LWOP.

312 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Padou v. District of Columbia


No. 12-CV-51


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


77 A.3d 383; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 645


June 4, 2013, Submitted 
October 3, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB-8274-08). (Hon. Craig Iscoe, Trial Judge).
Padou v. District of Columbia, 29 A.3d 973, 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 607 (D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Because group members sufficiently alleged that they suffered injury under D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 24, § 108.7, 108.10, and 108.11, and that injury was likely to be redressed if their constitutional challenges succeed, they had standing to challenge those subsections; [2]-The members did not have standing to challenge the whole of § 108 because they never alleged any facts or presented any evidence indicating that they were injured under any other subsection, and their standing to challenge §§ 108.7, 108.10, and 108.11 did not afford them standing to challenge any and all of the subsections of § 108; [3]-Because those subsections remained unaltered during the litigation, their constitutionality was just as much in question as it was when the members brought their suit; [4]-Thus, the merits of the members' challenges were properly before the trial court.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed and case remanded.

 

313 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Conley v. United States


No. 11-CF-589


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


79 A.3d 270; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 633


November 20, 2012, Argued 
September 26, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-13505-10). (Hon. Craig Iscoe, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether defendant's conviction of violating D.C. Code § 22-2511 (2012), which made it a felony for a person to be in a motor vehicle if the person knew the vehicle contained a firearm, even if he or she had no connection to or control over the weapon and was not involved in any wrongdoing, had to be reversed on grounds § 22-2511 was unconstitutional. HOLDINGS: [1]-The statute violated due process by imposing the burden on the defendant to prove, as an affirmative defense, that his presence in the vehicle was involuntary, thus shifting of the burden of persuasion on a critical component of the crime; [2]-It also violated due process by criminalizing innocent behavior--remaining in the vicinity of a firearm in a vehicle, which the average citizen would not suppose was wrongful--without requiring the government to prove the defendant had notice of a legal duty to behave otherwise.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded with directions that defendant's conviction be vacated.

Jackson v. United States

Parker v. K&L Gates, LLP


No. 11-CV-1578


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


76 A.3d 859; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 619; 36 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1139

September 19, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB-9371-10). (Hon. Gregory E. Jackson, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A partnership contract's arbitration and forum-selection clauses were enforceable against appellant partner because he could read them before signing the contract; [2]-It was not unreasonable to enforce the forum-selection clause because he forfeited any fraud claim, he did not show arbitrating in the forum denied him a remedy or his day in court, and enforcing it violated no strong public policy of the forum; [3]-The arbitration clause covered tort and statutory claims, including a claim under the Age Discrimination in Employment Act, 29 U.S.C.S. § 621 et seq., because it explicitly covered any claim concerning the contract's subject matter; [4]-Cal. OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Devita v. D.C.


No. 12-CV-893


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


74 A.3d 714; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 596


May 7, 2013, Argued 
September 5, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Devita v. District of Columbia, 2014 U.S. LEXIS 7927 (U.S., Dec. 1, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAT-3201-12). (Hon. Judith Bartnoff, Reviewing Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a motorist's application for leave to appeal an administrative adjudication finding him liable for speeding was wrongfully denied. HOLDINGS: [1]-Because, under the District of Columbia Traffic Adjudication Act, D.C. Code § 50-2301.01 et seq., traffic violations detected by the District of Columbia Automated Traffic Enforcement (ATE) System were civil in nature, the motorist was not entitled to the U.S. Const. amend. Code § 50-2302.06(d) (2001), the hearing examiner did not serve as both a prosecutor and a judge; [4]-The ATE System did not violate the Equal Protection Clause.

317 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Ramsey v. United States


No. 11-CF-1485


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


73 A.3d 138; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 496


December 19, 2012, Submitted 
August 15, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-22217-10). (Hon. Robert E. Morin, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: whether an officer illegally searched defendant. HOLDINGS: [1]-the officer's first encounter with defendant in an alley did not violate the Fourth Amendment because he only asked a few questions and reasonably thought defendant committed disorderly conduct by urinating in public, under former D.C. Code § 22-1321 (2001); [2]-the officer's reasonable belief was dispelled because he learned defendant did not urinate and that no one else saw defendant's conduct, so it did not menace public order; [3]-the officer's warrant check violated the Fourth Amendment because defendant did not feel free to leave and the officer no longer had reasonable, articulable suspicion; [4]-a search of defendant pursuant to consent was improper because the consent was contemporaneous with the illegal seizure, with no break in the causal chain, so it was not free from the taint of unlawful detention.


OUTCOME: Conviction reversed.

November 29, 2012, Argued 
June 6, 2013, Decided

OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Vagueness challenge to D.C. Code § 22-2603 (2001) (amended 2009) failed, because defendant knew that communicating on a cell phone violated prison regulations; [2]-First defendant's conviction for obstruction of justice under D.C. 319 of 475 DOCUMENTS


In re A.J.


No. 11-FS-644


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


63 A.3d 562; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 94


February 21, 2013, Argued 
March 28, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (DEL-608-11). (Hon. Maribeth Raffinan, Motions Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the District of Columbia, instituted a juvenile delinquency proceeding in which a 15 year old minor was charged with unlawful possession of a BB gun. A judge in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia held that the minor's incriminating statement was obtained in violation of Miranda and granted the minor's motion to suppress the statement. The judge denied the District's petition for reconsideration. The District appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the District contended that the boy was not in custody for Fifth Amendment Miranda purposes at the time that he made the statement, and that therefore there was no violation of Miranda. The appellate court found that on the record, no person of the minor's age, and in his circumstances, could reasonably have believed that he was under arrest or its equivalent. The appellate court found that the judge erred by failing to distinguish between the relatively simple question whether, at the time the officer inquired if the minor had anything on him, the boy had been seized, which he indisputably had been, and the more complicated issue whether the minor was also in custody as that term was used in Miranda and in the cases applying that decision. Indeed, beyond the bare fact of temporary seizure to transport the minor to his home nearby for purposes of investigation, there was nothing that the officer did that could plausibly be perceived, from the minor's perspective or any other, as coercive, or as though he was being arrested. Because the undisputed evidence showed that the minor had been detained but was not in Miranda custody, the trial court's judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The decision of the trial court was reversed and the case was remanded.

 

320 of 475 DOCUMENTS


In re Martin


No. 11-BG-775


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


67 A.3d 1032; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 96


April 4, 2012, Argued 
March 28, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at In re Martin, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 1928 (Pa., Aug. 28, 2013)
Amended by, Rehearing, en banc, denied by, Motion granted by In re Martin, 91 A.3d 564, 2014 D.C. App. LEXIS 17 (D.C., 2014)
Motion granted by, Amended by In re Martin, 2014 D.C. App. LEXIS 435 (D.C., Oct. 21, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
(BDN-370-04).
SEC v. Enters. Solutions, Inc., 142 F. Supp. 2d 561, 2001 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 7352 (S.D.N.Y., 2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent attorney was charged with violating D.C. R. Prof. Conduct 1.5, 1.15, 1.16, and 8.4. After the hearing committee made its findings, the Board on Professional Responsibility found that the attorney violated D.C. R. Prof. Conduct 1.5(a), 8.4(c), and 8.4(d) and recommended a sixth-month suspension with reinstatement subject to disgorgement of the unreasonable fee. The matter was before the court on the Board's report and recommendation.


OVERVIEW: Once the fact of dispute was conveyed, disputed funds had to be placed in a separate interest-bearing account. D.C. R. Prof. Conduct 1.15(c). Commingling the disputed funds with attorney funds resulted in a violation of D.C. R. Prof. Conduct 1.15(a) and (c). The attorney did not place the disputed funds into a separate account until nine months after he was aware of a dispute. Thus, he commingled disputed funds with his own funds in violation of D.C. R. Prof. Conduct 1.15(a) and (c). Although D.C. R. Prof. Conduct 1.15(c) stated that any funds in dispute shall be deposited in a separate account, it did not specify whether funds must be restored to a separate account if the dispute arose after funds had already been disbursed. In the instant case, the court found that the funds should have been restored to a separate account. The court held that if a client in any future matter, with reasonable promptness, disputed an attorney's fee after the attorney had already withdrawn his fee from the client trust account, the attorney must place the disputed amount in a separate account pursuant to D.C. R. Prof. Conduct 1.15(a). What was reasonable promptness would be a case-specific inquiry.


OUTCOME: The attorney was suspended from the practice of law in the District of Columbia for a period of 18 months.

Hargraves v. United States


No. 08-CF-346, No. 08-CF-376, No. 08-CF-474


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


62 A.3d 107; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 69


June 27, 2012, Argued 
March 7, 2013*, Decided


* The opinion in these consolidated appeals originally issued on January 17, 2013. The court vacated that opinion and substituted this amended opinion on March 7, 2013.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Gilliam v. United States, 187 L. Ed. 2d 200, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 6454 (U.S., 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF1-4544-06, CF1-4545-06, & CF1-4546-06). (Hon. Wendell P. Gardner, Jr., Trial Judge).
Hargraves v. United States, 59 A.3d 934, 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 13 (D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant two appealed his conviction by the Superior Court of the District of Columbia of fleeing from a law enforcement officer. Defendants one and three appealed their convictions of voluntary manslaughter, assault with a deadly weapon, two associated possession of a firearm during a crime of violence (PFCV) charges, carrying a pistol without a license and fleeing.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that the trial court did not violate the District of Columbia Incompetent Defendants Criminal Commitment Act of 2004 (Act), D.C. Code § 24-531.01 et seq., or the Due Process Clauses of the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments, U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV, in finding defendant one competent to stand trial. Defendant one responded favorably to inpatient treatment and medication and was allowed to remain at the hospital instead of returning to the jail. The Act's hearing requirement could be modified with the parties' consent, and neither party objected. The denial of defendants one's and two's motions to sever under D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 14 was proper. They did not identify any specific right that was compromised by the denial. Defendant three's testimony was subject to cross-examination. The jury acquitted defendants of the most serious charges, and differentiated defendant two by convicting him of one minor count. Defendant three's claim that it was error to refuse to allow him to present evidence of the victim's bad acts out of order was rejected. One of defendants one's and three's PFCV convictions were vacated on merger grounds.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. The case was remanded with instructions to vacate one of defendant one's and defendant three's PFCV convictions.

 

322 of 475 DOCUMENTS


James v. United States


No. 11-CO-704


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


59 A.3d 1233; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 21


October 24, 2012, Argued 
January 24, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (FEL-599-97). (Hon. Mary Ellen Abrecht, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of a first-degree murder he committed as a juvenile and was sentenced to a mandatory 30-year term under former D.C. Code § 22-2404 (Supp. 2004), now codified at D.C. Code Ann. § 21-2104. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia denied defendant's brief after construing it as a motion to correct an illegal sentence under D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 35(a). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that defendant did not fit into the categorical exceptions of Roper, Graham, or Miller. The Eighth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. VIII, required the State to give the juvenile non-homicide offender a meaningful opportunity to obtain release based on demonstrated maturity and rehabilitation. Defendant would have that opportunity after his minimum sentence was completed. Defendant's sentence did not guarantee that he would die in prison. The mitigating qualities of defendant's youth were taken into account in § 22-2404(a) as it limited the minimum sentence to 30 years' for offenders under the age of 18 at the time of their offense. The mandatory nature of the sentence did not violate the Eighth Amendment. The trial judge referred the matter for report and sentencing because after service of 30 years', matters in mitigation could be considered by the parole authorities. Defendant kidnapped a 12-year-old boy, drove to an apartment, retrieved a gun, drove to the woods, marched the boy into the middle of the woods, and shot him twice, once in the back of the head in an execution style murder. Defendant's sentence was not grossly disproportionate to his crime.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Hargraves v. United States


No. 08-CF-346, No. 08-CF-376, No. 08-CF-474


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


59 A.3d 934; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 13


June 27, 2012, Argued 
January 17, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by Hargraves v. United States, 62 A.3d 107, 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 68 (D.C., 2013)
Superseded by Hargraves v. United States, 62 A.3d 107, 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 69 (D.C., 2013)
Related proceeding at Gilliam v. United States, 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 784 (D.C., Nov. 21, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF1-4544-06, CF1-4545-06, & CF1-4546-06). (Hon. Wendell P. Gardner, Jr., Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed their convictions by the Superior Court of the District of Columbia in a joint trial which found defendant guilty of fleeing from a law enforcement officer and acquitted him of the other charges. The jury found codefendants guilty of, inter alia, voluntary manslaughter (as a lesser-included offense of first-degree murder), and assault with a dangerous weapon. They were acquitted of murder and other offenses.


OVERVIEW: The charges arose from an encounter between the occupants of two vehicles carrying defendant and codefendants that left one victim dead and another injured. The appellate court held the trial court properly found codefendant competent to stand trial based on an evaluation at a hospital, corroboration by codefendant's satisfactory behavior at trial, his responses to an inquiry, and a doctor's post-trial report and assessment. Counsel's report of communication problems, by itself, did not suffice to raise a substantial doubt about his competence to stand trial. As to severance, because the jury acquitted defendants of the most serious charges and differentiated defendant from codefendants by finding him guilty of a comparatively minor count, there was no prejudice. Further, defendant failed to show that the disparity in the weight of the evidence compromised a specific trial right or prevented the jury from reliably determining his guilt or innocence. The jury clearly did not find him guilty of anything based on the evidence of codefendants' greater culpability.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed. One of each of codefendants' possession of a firearm during a crime of violence convictions should be vacated on merger grounds on remand.

 

324 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Thompson v. United States


No. 11-CM-495


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


59 A.3d 961; 2013 D.C. App. LEXIS 12


December 5, 2012, Argued 
January 17, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CMD-18773-10). (Hon. Marisa Demeo, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction by the Superior Court of the District of Columbia for attempted possession of a prohibited weapon, a knife with a metal knuckles handle, claiming that the weapon did not meet the statutory definition of "knuckles," that D.C. Code § 22-4501(3) was unconstitutionally vague, and that the prohibition infringed upon his Second Amendment rights.


OVERVIEW: The police approached defendant, believing that he matched the description of a suspected robber. Defendant informed the officers that he had a knife and consented to a search of his person. The trial court held that the addition of a blade to the knuckles did not exempt the weapon from the coverage of D.C. Code § 22-4514(a). Defendant acknowledged that his weapon, often called a "trench knife," "did have a handle that had knuckles on it. However, he argued that because the handle of the trench knife was not separable from the blade itself, his weapon did not fit § 22-4501's definition of knuckles. The appellate court could not accept defendant's argument because it ignored both the statute's language and its evident purpose. The statutory definition aptly described the handle of defendant's weapon, which readily served the same purpose as more traditional knuckles. The handle of a trench knife was designed to enhance the force of a blow with a fist. D.C. Code § 22-4501 was not lacking in fair warning such that men of common intelligence must necessarily guess at its meaning and differ as to its application. It therefore was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The conviction was affirmed.

Thorne v. United States


Nos. 11-CF-492 & 11-CF-730


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


55 A.3d 873; 2012 D.C. App. LEXIS 518


October 11, 2012, Argued 
November 15, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Thorne v. United States, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 7170 (U.S., Oct. 7, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-15750-10). (Hon. Anthony C. Epstein, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which convicted him of carrying a pistol without a license in violation of D.C. Code § 22-4504 (2009 Supp.), possession of an unregistered firearm in violation of D.C. Code § 7-2502.01 (2009 Supp.), and unlawful possession of ammunition in violation of D.C. Code § 7-2506.01 (2009 Supp.).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was employed by a corporation that secured for him two Virginia court appointments as a special conservator of the peace. He argued that he was exempt from prosecution under the exceptions for law enforcement officers contained in D.C. Code §§ 22-4505, 7-2502.01(b)(1), and 7-2506.01(a)(2). (2009 Supp.). The court of appeals held that the limited exemptions that permitted law enforcement officers to carry weapons and ammunition in the District of Columbia did not apply to defendant. Defendant, who only possessed authority to act in a law enforcement capacity while he was working for his private employer, was not a "law enforcement officer" within the meaning of the statutory provisions. Outside of specific times when he was working, he did not have general police authority or authorization to carry a gun. Because defendant was neither a law enforcement officer with general authority nor an individual with limited law enforcement authority who was acting within the scope of his authority, he was not considered a law enforcement officer for purposes of § 22-4505. Defendant was also not entitled to the exemptions under §§ 7-2502.01(b)(1) and 7-2506.01(a)(2).


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the judgment.

Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by, Motion granted by, Dismissed by, in part In re Gorbey, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 16699 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 30, 2017)

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. CF2-1552-08. (Hon. Gregory Jackson, Trial Judge).
Gorbey v. State, 2012 W. Va. LEXIS 261 (W. Va., Mar. 30, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of, inter alia, possession of a firearm by a convicted felon; carrying a dangerous weapon; possession of an unregistered firearm; possession of ammunition; manufacture, transfer, use, possession, or transportation of explosives; and attempted manufacture or possession of a weapon of mass destruction. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia denied his motion to vacate or set aside his conviction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court ignored a series of "red flags" that went up during the pretrial and trial proceedings and signaled a need to examine defendant's competency. The appellate court disagreed, holding, inter alia, that defendant's belief that he was the victim of a massive conspiracy did not give the trial court a substantial reason to think that he lacked an understanding of the charges against him or an ability to assist in his defense. In addition, the appellate court noted that defendant was attuned to what was going on during the trial and pretrial proceedings, cooperated with standby counsel, and assisted in his defense. The appellate court also concluded that the trial court did not err by failing to evaluate his mental health in connection with his proceeding pro se and neglecting to ensure he knowingly and voluntarily waived his right to counsel, noting that defendant demonstrated his experience with the trial process, handling as assortment of trial tasks. The trial court did err, however in failing to conduct a Frendak inquiry before sentencing defendant, given the new information presented at the hearing calling into question defendant's mental health.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded for a Frendak inquiry. On remand, the trial court was ordered to vacate one of defendant convictions for carrying a dangerous weapon, unlawful possession of a 0.45 caliber round, and all but one of his other unlawful possession of ammunition convictions.
