Gilliam v. United States


Nos. 08-CF-475, 08-CF-504, 08-CF-505


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


46 A.3d 360; 2012 D.C. App. LEXIS 305


December 14, 2009, Argued 
June 21, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-12586-07, FEL-5911-05, FEL-7333-05). (Hon. Geoffrey M. Alprin, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After three cases against defendant were consolidated for trial, a jury in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia convicted defendant of various offenses involving the possession of weapons and drugs and of failing to obey an order to appear in court. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence, because the search warrant was limited to the house and the officer lacked probable cause to search the truck. The appellate court found no error, concluding that the trial court was permitted to consider that the officer reasonably identified the smell as burnt marijuana and saw smoke emanating from the truck, but was either honestly mistaken or simply overlooked the evidence of smoke material inside the truck. Defendant also argued that the prosecutor, in rebuttal argument, improperly commented on the reasonable doubt standard and that he was prejudiced by the way the judge resolved counsel's objection. The appellate court held that any error was harmless, as it was the comments by the prosecutor and not the court that defendant argued were misleading and the impact of any misstatements was reduced by the judge's instructions that it was the court's responsibility to apprise the jury as to the law. Finally, the appellate court concluded that evidence that defendant did not return in the afternoon and was not dismissed by a judicial officer supported the conviction for failure to appear.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Headspeth v. District of Columbia


No. 09-CF-1277


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


53 A.3d 304; 2012 D.C. App. LEXIS 295



June 7, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
2007 CF2 14313.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury trial in the District of Columbia Superior Court, defendant was convicted of two counts of possession of an unregistered firearm (UF), in violation of D.C. Code § 7-2502.01(a) (2001), and two counts of unlawful possession of ammunition (UA), in violation of D.C. Code § 7-2506.01(a)(3) (2001). He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, 18, was arrested after police found two handguns in his bedroom. He moved to dismiss on the ground the UF and UA statutes infringed on his right to bear arms under the Second Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. II. The trial court held that defendant, not having tried to register the guns, was not a liberty to complain. The appellate court noted that under Plummer v. United States, defendant had standing to raise the Second Amendment defense, notwithstanding his failure to attempt to register the guns. The District argued that he was not entitled to a remand for an evidentiary hearing under Plummer as he did not show he could have satisfied the registration requirements applicable to 18-to-20 year-olds set out in D.C. Code § 7-2502.03(a)(1)(A)-(B). The appellate court disagreed. On remand, defendant would have the burden of proving that, before he was charged, he could have obtained a parent's or guardian's notarized written permission to own and use the handguns and a parent's or guardian's notarized written assumption of civil liability. If he failed to meet that burden, under merger principles, only one of his UF convictions and one of his UA convictions could stand.


OUTCOME: The case was remand for an evidentiary hearing. If the trial court determined that defendant could not have qualified for gun registration, it had to nevertheless vacate one of his UF convictions and one of his UA convictions.

Plummer v. United States


No. 07-CM-1299


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


43 A.3d 260; 2012 D.C. App. LEXIS 155


June 28, 2011, Submitted 
May 10, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CMD 18964-07). (Hon. Truman A. Morrison III, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a bench trial, the Superior Court of the District of Columbia convicted defendant of two counts of deceptive labeling, in violation of D.C. Code § 22-3214.01 (2001). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the trial judge should have recused himself after presiding over defendant's unsuccessful attempt to plead guilty. Defendant argued that the judge's failure to do so denied defendant his due process right to a neutral arbiter. The appellate court concluded that defendant waived the issue by failing to object, even after the judge raised the issue. In addition, while the judge's recusal from sitting as the trial of fact clearly would have been warranted had defendant actually admitted his guilt or otherwise incriminated himself, defendant denied the conduct alleged, necessitating the rejection of his plea. Defendant also challenged the admission of items seized into evidence based on a chain of custody issue. While the chain of custody was less than ideal, there was no evidence the police failed to maintain continuous custody or that the evidence was tampered with; thus, any challenge was to the weight of the evidence and not its admissibility. Finally, the appellate court concluded that the convictions did not merge, noting that differentiation between audiovisual works and sound recordings in the statute's penalty structure.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Patterson v. United States


Nos. 08-CF-876 & 10-CO-1611


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


37 A.3d 230; 2012 D.C. App. LEXIS 64


September 13, 2011, Argued 
February 16, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Amended by, Opinion withdrawn by, in part, Rehearing, en banc, denied by Patterson v. United States, 2012 D.C. App. LEXIS 502 (D.C., Oct. 11, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF3-17813-07). (Hon. Robert I. Richter, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of the District of Columbia entered judgment, upon a jury's verdict, convicting defendant of armed robbery, in violation of D.C. Code § 22-2801 (2001), and possession of a firearm during a crime of violence, in violation of D.C. Code § 22-4502 (Supp. 2011). The trial court denied defendant's D.C. Code § 23-110 new trial motion based on ineffective assistance of counsel. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The direct appeal and defendant's appeal from the trial court's denial of his § 23-110 motion were consolidated for review. With regard to the direct appeal, the trial court did not err in refusing to allow defendant to present an expert witness on the subject of identification testimony. However, the corroborative evidence establishing the accuracy of the victim's identification was notable, thus making any assumed error as to the expert testimony harmless. Next, defendant was not denied a meaningful opportunity to present a defense because he presented his misidentification argument sufficiently through argument and a witness. With regard to defendant's dissatisfaction with his counsel, his statement to the trial court that he "could do better as his own lawyer" was not enough to trigger a Monroe-Farrell inquiry, nor was it a clear and unequivocal declaration of a desire to represent himself. Finally, the trial judge did not plainly err in failing to sua sponte correct the government's use of the term "malarkey." With regard to the § 23-110 motion, an evidentiary hearing was not required because trial counsel's performance was neither deficient nor prejudicial to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and affirmed the order denying the collateral attack without a hearing.

Porter v. United States


No. 09-CO-425


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


37 A.3d 251; 2012 D.C. App. LEXIS 66


March 24, 2011, Argued 
February 16, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] 
Reargued November 9, 2011.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (FEL7540-03). (Hon. Maurice A. Ross, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought D.C. Code § 23-110 relief on the ground that he received ineffective assistance from his trial counsel. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia denied the motion. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that his trial counsel was ineffective in failing to seek suppression of evidence recovered from the scene of an alleged robbery. The trial court assumed that trial counsel should have filed the suppression motion and that the motion would have been successful, but it denied the § 23-110 motion because, in its opinion, the remaining evidence against the defendant was overwhelming. However, the factual record was not sufficiently developed to support a conclusion that a suppression motion would have resulted in the exclusion of a significant amount of evidence. Furthermore, there was a reasonable probability that the outcome of defendant's trial would have been different had some or all of the evidence in question been excluded. Finally, given the deficiencies in the factual record, the appellate court could not have upheld the trial court's denial of the § 23-110 motion on the alternative ground that the motion to suppress would not have resulted in the exclusion of evidence because the evidence seized during a second entry would have been admissible under the plain view doctrine, even though the second entry itself was unlawful.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

Long v. United States


No. 08-CO-1613


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


36 A.3d 363; 2012 D.C. App. LEXIS 20


April 6, 2011, Argued 
February 2, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Long v. United States, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 5765 (U.S., Oct. 7, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F2346-96). (Hon. Kaye K. Christian, Trial Judge).
Long v. United States, 910 A.2d 298, 2006 D.C. App. LEXIS 579 (D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of conspiracy to commit murder, first-degree premeditated murder while armed, assault with a dangerous weapon, and possession of a firearm during a crime of violence. Defendatn's motion for a new trial was vacated. On remand, the Superior Court of the District of Columbia held a hearing and denied the motion. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that his trial counsel's performance was constitutionally deficient and, as a direct result of those deficiencies, he suffered prejudice. Among other things, defendant alleged that trial counsel erred when failed to call certain witnesses to testify. The appellate court found no error, determining that trial counsel made a strategic decision not to call the subject witnesses based on their assertion of the Fifth Amendment, his skepticism about whether they were telling the truth, and their serious criminal charges and convictions which would cast doubt about whether they were telling the truth. Defendant also argued that the trial court's sentence was unconstitutional in light of Apprendi, because the trial court found three aggravating factors that were not determined by the jury, specifically that the murder was especially heinous, that the crime involved substantial planning, and that the victim was "vulnerable" because of his age of 14 years old. The appellate court concluded that defendant was procedurally barred from raising that argument, and even if he were not, his argument was unpersuasive because Apprendi did not apply retroactively.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment of conviction and denial of defendant's motions for a new trial and for correction of sentence were affirmed.

Crossland v. United States


No. 10-CM-1234


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


32 A.3d 1005; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 686


November 10, 2011, Submitted 
December 15, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CMD7254-10). (Hon. Florence Pan, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which convicted him of two counts of assault on a police officer in violation of D.C. Code Ann. § 22-405(b) (2001) and denied his posttrial motion for a judgment of acquittal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of two counts of assault on a police officer and on appeal, he challenged the sufficiency of the evidence. He also asserted that the trial court erred in denying his posttrial motion for a judgment of acquittal, which he sought as a sanction against the government for the police officers' violation of his U.S. Const. amend. IV rights. The appellate court affirmed, stating that the trial court specifically credited the officer's testimony, which was to the effect that defendant delivered an elbow strike and attempted to hit the officer while in a fighting stance. The officer's testimony that he jumped on defendant's back and struck his knees only after defendant had attempted to hit the officer was also credited. Defendant failed to establish that the trial court's findings were plainly wrong or without evidence to support them.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

334 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Perry v. United States


Nos. 05-CF-662 & 05-CF-670


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


36 A.3d 799; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 687


May 13, 2008, Argued 
December 15, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended April 23, 2012.

As Corrected December 20, 2011.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (FEL7120-03 & FEL7119-03). (Hon. Lynn Leibovitz, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury trial in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, defendants were convicted of assault, aggravated assault while armed (AAWA) in violation of D.C. Code § 22-404.01, and assault with a dangerous weapon (ADW) in violation of D.C. Code § 22-402. They appealed.


OVERVIEW: The victim was assaulted by several men who kicked him repeatedly. Defendants argued that the instructions, given without objection, for aiding and abetting as to the AAWA and ADW charges were erroneous. The appellate court held there was no plain error in the aiding and abetting instruction given with respect to ADW, as they fairly apprised the jury that accomplice liability required a finding that defendants either knew or that it was reasonably foreseeable to them that the principal would use a shod foot to kick the victim. But an instruction that allowed the jury to convict them of AAWA as aiders and abettors if they participated in the crime, but stated that they did not need to have the intent to commit the particular crime committed by the principal offender, was erroneous. As the instruction omitted the mens rea element of the offenses charged, the error was of constitutional magnitude. As the law at the time of the appeal was settled and clearly contrary to the law at the time of trial, the error was clear. Finally, there was a reasonable probability that the jury would have been swayed by the erroneous instruction to convict defendants of AAWA as aiders and abettors.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed defendants' AAWA convictions and remanded the case for further proceedings. It affirmed the other convictions.

 

335 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Hatch v. United States


No. 08-CF-1362


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


35 A.3d 1115; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 682


February 24, 2011, Argued 
December 8, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F-26706-07). (Hon. Geoffrey M. Alprin, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury trial in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, defendant was convicted of first-degree sexual abuse (D.C. Code § 22-3002(a)(1)), possession of a firearm during a crime of violence, carrying a pistol without a license, and possession of an unregistered firearm. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: The complainant testified that while she was working as a prostitute, defendant solicited her services, and that after she demanded payment up front, he forced her to have sex with him at gunpoint. He testified that he had sex with her for money and denied using a weapon or force of any kind. The trial court, over defense objection, instructed the jury that it could consider evidence of consent in determining whether the sexual encounter was forcible, and that defendant had the burden of proving consent by a preponderance of the evidence. The appellate court noted that defendant claimed the complainant had sex with him without coercion, in exchange for cash, and accused him of sexual assault to exact revenge for his refusal to pay her for all the sexual services he enjoyed; he never claimed she was a willing participant despite his having held her at gunpoint. Thus, there was no factual basis for the instructions. Further, the trial court erred in giving the instructions because they were confusing and there was a reasonable likelihood that they led the jury to shift the burden of proof on the element of force from the government to defendant.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed defendant's convictions of first-degree sexual abuse and possession of a firearm during a crime of violence and remanded for a new trial on those counts. It affirmed his other convictions.

 

336 of 475 DOCUMENTS


In re Prosecution of Hall


No. 11-SP-591


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


31 A.3d 453; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 626


September 27, 2011, Argued 
November 17, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
On Certification from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CDC-18148-10). (Hon. Robert E. Morin, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Office of the Attorney General of the District of Columbia (OAG) charged defendant with possession of an unregistered firearm (UF) and unlawful possession of ammunition (UA). He objected to being prosecuted by the OAG. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia certified this question for summary appellate disposition: was the OAG or the Office of the United States Attorney for the District of Columbia (USAO) the proper prosecutor?


OVERVIEW: After the USAO declined to prosecute defendant for carrying a pistol without a license, the OAG filed the instant charges. defendant contended that UF and UA were "serious misdemeanors" and, therefore, were properly prosecuted by the USAO. The appellate court disagreed. The OAG was authorized by D.C. Code § 23-101(a) to prosecute violations of all "police or municipal ordinances or regulations." Because the District's statutes banning possession of an unregistered firearm (D.C. Code § 7-2502.01) and unlawful possession of ammunition (D.C. Code § 7-2506.01) were "police or municipal ordinances or regulations" within the meaning of § 23-101(a), prosecutorial authority lay with the OAG irrespective of the fact that violation of these provisions carried a maximum penalty of both a fine and imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The appellate court held that the OAG was the proper prosecutorial authority for violations of the UF and UA provisions and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

337 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Burton v. Office of Emple. Appeals


No. 09-CV-1493, No. 10-CV-963


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


30 A.3d 789; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 616; 32 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1881


September 29, 2011, Argued 
November 3, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAP6371-08 and CAP5322-08). (Hon. Judith E. Retchin, Trial Judge). (Hon. John Ramsey Johnson, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police commanders were demoted. They appealed to the Office of Employee Appeals (OEA), claiming that as Career Service employees they should not have been demoted without cause under the Comprehensive Merit Personnel Act, D.C. Code § 1-601.01 et seq. The OEA held that the chief had authority to reduce their rank without cause. The commanders appealed. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia affirmed. They sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The first commander, a Career Service employee, was progressively promoted to the positions of lieutenant, captain, inspector, and commander. The newly appointed chief of police informed the commander that she was returning him to the rank of captain and reassigning him to other duties. At no point did the chief suggest that the first commander's work was inadequate. The second commander, also a Career Service employee, was promoted to the positions of inspector and commander. The chief returned him to the rank of inspector. The plain meaning of the statutory language of D.C. Code § 1-608.01(d-1) was that Career Service employees of the Metropolitan Police Department were included within its reach. Additionally, § 1-608.01(d-1), applied alike to inspectors, commanders, and assistant chiefs of police. Accordingly, pursuant to § 1-608.01(d-1), the mayor, or the chief of police as the mayor's delegee, had explicit discretionary authority to return any officer above the rank of captain to a lower rank, without cause. Because § 1-608.01(d-1) provided the chief with discretionary authority to return any commander to a lower rank, the commanders' constitutional rights were not abridged.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments.

Gamble v. United States


No. 09-CF-184


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


30 A.3d 161; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 615


September 8, 2011, Argued 
October 27, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Gamble v. United States, 2014 U.S. LEXIS 8070 (U.S., Dec. 1, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Super``ior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-8905-08). (Hon. Hiram E. Puig-Lugo, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the denial of defendant's motion to dismiss the charges, defendant entered a conditional plea of guilty, in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, to carrying a pistol without a license (CPWL), reserving the right to appeal the denial of his motion to dismiss, and the Government dismissed the charges for possession of an unregistered firearm and unlawful possession of ammunition. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction for CPWL, arguing that the Second Amendment gave him the right to carry a concealed firearm outside his home or place of business. The appellate court agreed with the Government, that the District of Columbia Court of Appeals was under no obligation, as defendant asserted, to recognize that the Second Amendment right to possess and carry weapons in case of confrontation could not be limited to the confines of the home. Defendant also asserted that the District's statute prohibiting the carrying of a pistol without a license violated the Second Amendment because it amounted to an outright ban on the carrying of pistols. The appellate court disagreed, finding that the statute was not unconstitutional as applied to defendant's conduct and noting that defendant could not challenge the statute by arguing that it could not be constitutionally applied to other defendants differently situated.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Surur Fatumabahirtu v. United States


Nos. 08-CM-314 and 08-CM-781


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


26 A.3d 322; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 497


June 3, 2010,1 Argued


1 The appeals of Ms. Fatumabahirtu and Mr. Aslam were placed on the Summary Calendar. The court granted Ms. Fatumabahirtu's request for oral argument. Mr. Aslam did not request oral argument and his case was submitted without oral argument.

 
August 11, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Fatumabahirtu v. United States, 132 S. Ct. 1944, 182 L. Ed. 2d 799, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 2770 (U.S., 2012)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Aslam v. United States, 132 S. Ct. 2706, 183 L. Ed. 2d 62, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 3976 (U.S., 2012)
Post-conviction relief granted at Fatumabahirtu v. United States, 2016 D.C. App. LEXIS 410 (D.C., Nov. 3, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CMD17041-07 and CMD17029-07). (Hon. Wendell P. Gardner, Jr., Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Two defendants sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which convicted them of the attempted sale of drug paraphernalia in violation of D.C. Code §§ 48-1103(b) and 22-1803.


OVERVIEW: Defendants appealed after they were convicted of the attempted sale of drug paraphernalia. The appeal involved a matter of first impression requiring an interpretation of a subsection of the District of Columbia Drug Paraphernalia Act of 1982, D.C. Code § 48-1103 (b) (2001). The appellate court affirmed, stating that the government presented credible, strong and compelling indirect and circumstantial evidence beyond a reasonable doubt that at the time the first defendant sold the glass ink pen and the copper scouring pad to the officer, she and the second defendant knew or reasonably should have known that the buyer would use those items to inject, ingest, or inhale a controlled substance. The trial court credited the officer's account of the sale, that he asked for "an ink pen" and that the first defendant gave him a glass ink pen and a metal scouring pad, even though he did not request a metal scouring pad. The second defendant, the owner of the store, also ordered, stored, and specifically intended to sell items that obviously could be used with illegal drugs, including digital scales, empty ziplock bags, glass ink pens that had little ink, and copper scouring pads.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Paige v. United States


Nos. 04-CF-715 & 09-CO-1347


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


25 A.3d 74; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 438


February 1, 2011, Argued 
July 28, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Paige v. United States, 132 S. Ct. 1605, 182 L. Ed. 2d 212, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 1302 (U.S., 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (FEL5821-01). (Hon. Ann O'Regan Keary, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with first-degree murder, possession of a firearm during a crime of violence (PFCV), and carrying a pistol without a license (CPWL). The first trial resulted in a mistrial. Upon a second trial, defendant was convicted of the lesser-included offense of second-degree murder, PFCV, and CPWL. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia denied defendant's motions for acquittal and a new trial. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court's handling of the references to codefendant's guilty plea was reversible error because the references were inadmissible testimonial hearsay that violated his rights under the Confrontation Clause rights and the failure to give a cautionary instruction compounded the prejudice against him, requiring reversal. The appellate court concluded that if there was error on the part of the trial court it was not plain, and any prejudicial effect of the reference to codefendant's plea was minimal and far less than the effect such a reference might have in another circumstance. Defendant also argued that the convictions should be reversed because the court provided an erroneous instruction on aiding and abetting. The appellate court disagreed, noting that it had upheld virtually the same instruction on several other occasions. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying defendant's motion for a new trial, as the proffered newly discovered evidence was not new, but was known to defense counsel, who made strategic decisions on which evidence or testimony to use at trial.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

341 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Howard Univ. v. Wilkins


Nos. 09-CV-318, 09-CV-319 and 09-CV-544


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


22 A.3d 774; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 367; 112 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1420


September 9, 2010, Argued 
June 30, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB763-05). (Hon. Thomas J. Motley, Trial Judge). (Hon. Patricia A. Broderick, Motions Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee employee filed an action for retaliation and defamation against appellant employer. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia held that the employer was entitled to a qualified privilege with respect to the defamation claim. The jury found in favor of the employee on the retaliation claim, awarding her punitive damages under the District of Columbia Human Rights Act (DCHRA), D.C. Code § 2-1401.01 et seq. The parties cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee filed a lawsuit against the employer, alleging a DCHRA sexual harassment violation. While the action was pending, the employee fell and was placed on long-term disability. The employee was terminated during her disability absence period, allegedly due to theft of grant funds. However, the employee denied any wrongdoing, claiming that she had ordered certain paper supplies and billed them to her personal account and that the vendor had erred on the invoice. With regard to the retaliation claim, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying the employer's post-trial motion because the jury's punitive damages award was not constitutionally excessive. Although the compensatory damage award was nominal, the employer's behavior was reprehensible and the District had a strong interest in deterring DCHRA violations. With regard to the defamation claim, the record supported the summary judgment in favor of the employer based on a qualified privilege. Finally, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in not ordering reinstatement because it was doubtful that the parties would have been able to maintain a productive and amicable working relationship.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Smith v. United States


No. 09-CO-1412


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


20 A.3d 759; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 289


November 10, 2010, Argued 
May 26, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-19954-06). (Hon. John M. Mott, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pleaded guilty in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia to three charges: attempted carrying a pistol without a license, D.C. Code § 22-4504(a) (2001); possession of an unregistered firearm, D.C. Code § 7-2502.01(a) (2001); and unlawful possession of ammunition, D.C. Code § 7-2506.01(a)(3) (2001). He filed a petition for writ of error coram nobis, which was denied by the trial court. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: At the time he was stopped with the weapons, appellant was a 16-year veteran of the Metropolitan Police Department who had been wrongfully terminated and was working as a technician for an automated teller company until his reinstatement. He had a license to carry a firearm from the Commonwealth of Virginia. He argued that under the Armored Car Industry Reciprocity Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 5902 (2006), the District's gun control laws were preempted. The court held that appellant waived his preemption and Second Amendment claims because he knowingly entered an unconditional guilty plea and these claims were non-jurisdictional in nature. Although he had not waived his ineffectiveness claim that he would not have pleaded guilty if advised as to his statutory preemption claim, that claim failed. The facts did not establish that he was carrying this weapon while acting in the service of his employer as required for preemption under 15 U.S.C. § 5902(a)(2). Rather, he was carrying his loaded weapon through the District on his way to his wife's house at 1 a.m. to sleep prior to going to work the next day.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions.

 

343 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Dickens v. United States


No. 10-CM-467


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


19 A.3d 321; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 220


April 21, 2011, Submitted 
May 5, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CMD19869-09). (Hon. Truman A. Morrison, III, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a bench trial in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, defendant was convicted of assault on a police officer (APO) in violation of D.C. Code § 22-405(b) (2009 Supp.). He appealed.


OVERVIEW: When an officer tried to question defendant, who was walking a pit bull, about a suspicious bulge in his waistband, defendant ran off. Police pursued and caught him. As they were attempting to handcuff him, he yelled "get them" or "get him." The dog bit an officer, who pushed the dog off him and shot it as it charged him. The trial court found that while the dog attack itself did not constitute an APO by defendant, he had "intimidated" the officer, within the meaning of § 22-405(b). Defendant argued that his conviction based on words alone was contrary to § 22-405(b) and the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I. The appellate court held that the words he used to incite a dog attack were not mere words that exempted him from prosecution under § 22-405(b) or gave him a defense under the First Amendment. To convict him under § 22-405(b), the government had to prove only that his words could have a tendency to intimidate those who heard them, not that anyone was actually intimidated. "Intimidate" was not unconstitutionally vague and § 22-405(b) was not overbroad. As defendant's words were intended to injure one or more officers, they did not qualify as protected speech.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

344 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Blaine v. United States


No. 09-CF-557


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


18 A.3d 766; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 214


February 8, 2011, Argued 
April 28, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF1-9908-07). (Hon. Geoffrey M. Alprin, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which convicted him of second-degree murder while armed in violation of D.C. Code §§ 22-2103 and 22-4502 (2001), possessing a firearm during a crime of violence (PFCV) in violation of D.C. Code § 22-4504(b) (2001), and carrying a pistol without a license (CPWL) in violation of § 22-4504(a) (2001).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his convictions for second-degree murder while armed, PFCV, and CPWL, and the appellate court reversed and remanded for a new trial, stating that the impact of a re-instruction regarding reasonable doubt was improper. The impact was particularly serious because, however individual jurors would parse the re-instruction, there was a reasonable likelihood that, collectively, the jurors would have gained the overall, and correct, impression that the trial judge was restating the instruction in the government's favor, and thus that the concept of "reasonable doubt" was less stringent than they originally had thought. Once that impression was conveyed, it was virtually tantamount to an indication that the judge believed that defendant was guilty and that the government, therefore, had met its burden of proof. The two instructions in the current case conveyed different meanings when the trial judge, for purposes of re-instruction, not only reworked critical language but also told the jurors to see the instructions in the new light of "some change."


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed and remanded for a new trial.

 

345 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Bean v. United States


Nos. 10-CF-349 and 10-CO-439


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


17 A.3d 635; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 159


January 11, 2011, Submitted 
April 21, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-17141-07). Hon. John M. Mott, Motions Judge), (Hon. Anthony C. Epstein, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which convicted him of possessing cocaine and heroin with intent to distribute. He argued that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress because the police lacked probable cause to arrest him for possession of an open container of alcohol (POCA), D.C. Code § 25-1001(a) (2001), since the container in question was closed when an officer discovered it.


OVERVIEW: The officer pulled defendant over for operating a vehicle without headlights. He saw a bottle of alcohol in plain view resting on the floor of the vehicle. The officer placed defendant under arrest and found cocaine and heroin in his pockets. The court of appeals held that the trial court committed no error in denying defendant's motion to suppress. The officer had probable cause to believe that defendant had violated the POCA statute as amended by the new definitional section. Under D.C. Code § 25-101(35) (2001), "open container" meant a bottle, can, or other container that was open or from which the top, cap, cork, seal, or tab seal has at some time been removed. The evidence showed that the bottle of alcohol was unsealed and that its contents had been partially consumed when the officer found it in the vehicle in plain view, on the floor just inches from where defendant had been sitting. There was no due process violation in the government's failure to preserve or produce the actual alcohol bottle. Nothing in the record suggested that the police lost or destroyed the bottle so that defendant could not examine it or make evidentiary use of it.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the judgment.

 

346 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Richman Towers Tenants' Ass'n v. Richman Towers LLC


Nos. 08-CV-1027, 08-CV-1114, 08-CV-1340, 08-CV-1354, 08-CV-1438 & 09-CV-106


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


17 A.3d 590; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 157


January 14, 2010, Argued 
April 14, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB4510-07, CAB4511-07, CAB4512-07, CAB4516-07, CAB4513-07 & CAB4510-07). (Hon. Jennifer M. Anderson, Trial Judge), (Hon. Jeanette Clark, Trial Judge), (Hon. Judith E. Retchin, Trial Judge), (Hon. Natalia Combs Greene, Trial Judge), (Hon. Natalia Combs Greene, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tenant associations filed actions alleging that the sale of apartment complexes by appellee sellers to appellees, limited liability companies (LLC) and their owners, constituted a sale within the meaning of the Tenants' Opportunity to Purchase Act (TOPA), D.C. Code § 42-3404.02(a) et seq. (2001). They sought review of orders of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which granted the owners' motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found that the transfer of ownership did not constitute a sale within the meaning of TOPA and that the associations bringing lacked standing to bring the suits. The court of appeals held that summary judgment was appropriately granted for lack of standing against all of the associations except two. Those two associations had statutory standing to bring their respective actions. At the time of the alleged sale those associations qualified as "tenant organizations" within the meaning of the TOPA, D.C. Code § 42-3401.03(18) (2001). None of the associations had associational standing. The court of appeals concluded that the transfers constituted a sale subject to TOPA. The LLC I entities transferred absolute title in the apartment buildings to the LLC II entities as part of an agreement in which a third party, the owners, would thereby gain an interest in the property. After the closing, the sellers no longer owned the properties. TOPA, D.C. Code § 42-3404.02(a) (2001), applied to all sales and did not restrict the requirement of notice to tenants to situations where the sale was only for the purpose of demolition or discontinuance of housing use.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed in part the summary judgments entered in favor of all but two of the associations. It reversed the summary judgments entered against those two associations and the ruling that the transfers did not constitute a sale within the meaning of TOPA, and it remanded those judgments to the trial court further proceedings.

 

347 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Gaynor v. United States


No. 08-CF-206


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


16 A.3d 944; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 146


March 2, 2011, Argued 
March 31, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF1-11420-07). (Hon. Herbert B. Dixon, Jr., Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which convicted him of first degree sexual abuse with aggravating circumstances in violation of D.C. Code §§ 22-3002 and 22-3601. He argued that the trial court's jury instructions regarding the affirmative defense of consent, D.C. Code § 22-3007, unconstitutionally shifted the burden of proof to him to disprove the element of force.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals held that there was a reasonable likelihood that the jury applied the consent instructions in an unconstitutional manner. During deliberations the jury expressed confusion about the instructions, and the trial court's supplemental instructions failed to clear away that confusion. At a minimum, the jury's note to the trial court demonstrated the jury's confusion about the burdens of proof. It also tended to show that the jurors had reached the second step of the analysis, which inquired whether defendant had proven consent by a preponderance of the evidence, before deciding whether the government had proven all elements of the offense, including force, beyond a reasonable doubt. The trial court's supplemental instruction likely added to the confusion regarding the burdens of proof. Although, the trial court repeatedly and emphatically instructed the jury about the government's burden to prove the element of force beyond a reasonable doubt, that general instruction was not sufficient to overcome the confusion inherent in applying § 22-3007 to the circumstances and increased by the supplemental instructions.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals reversed defendant's convictions for first degree sexual abuse and remanded the case for a new trial.

Kinane v. United States


Nos. 08-CM-681, 08-CM-814, 08-CM-815, 08-CM-816, 08-CM-817, 08-CM-818, 08-CM-819, 08-CM-820, 08-CM-821, 08-CM-822, 08-CM-823, 08-CM-824, 08-CM-825, 08-CM-826, 08-CM-827, 08-CM-828, 08-CM-829, 08-CM-830, 08-CM-831, 08-CM-832, 08-CM-833, 08-CM-834, 08-CM-835, 08-CM-836, 08-CM-837, 08-CM-838, 08-CM-839, 08-CM-840, 08-CM-841, 08-CM-881, 08-CM-882, 08-CM-883, 08-CM-884, 08-CM-885


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


12 A.3d 23; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 16


November 19, 2010, Argued 
January 20, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Kinane v. United States, 181 L. Ed. 2d 18, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 6862 (U.S., 2011)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Kinane v. United States, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 8285 (U.S., Nov. 14, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia (CMD1213-08, et al.). (Hon. Wendell P. Gardner, Jr., Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a bench trial in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, defendants were convicted violating 40 U.S.C.S. § 6135 by unlawfully demonstrating on the U.S. Supreme Court plaza or in the Supreme Court building. They appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants gathered on the sidewalk front of the Supreme Court to demonstrate their opposition to the U.S. prison in Guantanamo Bay, Cuba. Some defendants left the sidewalk and protested on the Supreme Court plaza; others protested inside the first floor of the Supreme Court building. Both groups of defendants refused to leave the plaza or building despite being given warnings by police, and were arrested. The appellate court rejected their claim that the display clause of § 6135 violated their right to freedom of speech under the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I. Section 6135's restriction on expression was a reasonable measure to preserve the appearance of the Supreme Court as a body not swayed by external influence. Section 6135 was not unduly vague and provided adequate notice of unlawful activity; further, defendants were provided sufficient and clear warnings that they would be arrested if they continued their demonstrations on the plaza and in the building. Contrary to defendants' contention, a device did not have to be displayed both in the building and on the plaza in order violate § 6135.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

349 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Magnus v. United States


Nos. 09-CO-1312 & 10-CO-245


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


11 A.3d 237; 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 3


November 10, 2010, Argued 
January 6, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. Criminal Division (CMD-5941-96). (Hon. Ronna Lee Beck, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a former probationer who had pled guilty to carrying a pistol without a license, possession of an unregistered firearm, and unlawful possession of ammunition, sought relief from his convictions under D.C. Code § 23-110 (2001) or a writ of error coram nobis, and moved to withdraw his pleas under D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 32(e). The Superior Court of the District of Columbia, Criminal Division, denied relief; he appealed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued that in light of District of Columbia v. Heller, which held the District's general ban on possession of usable handguns in the home violated the Second Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. II, his guilty pleas were invalid because he was not informed that the government would have to prove the constitutionally of the charged crimes. The appellate court noted that while the cases holding that the statutes to which appellant pled guilty were unconstitutional were given retroactive effect, an unconditional guilty plea waived otherwise available constitutional defenses. However, though appellant did not file a direct appeal alleging his plea was not voluntarily or intelligently made, he might be able to overcome that procedural default by showing he was entitled to relief, via D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 32(e) or coram nobis, to correct a miscarriage of justice, i.e., his conviction for conduct that under the Second Amendment could not be criminalized. In holding that he could not show a miscarriage of justice because he was using the guns to protect illegal drugs, the trial court reached that conclusion prematurely because it did not hold an evidentiary hearing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded for an evidentiary hearing.

 

350 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Bailey v. United States


No. 06-CF-1422


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


10 A.3d 637; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 738


September 2, 2009, Argued 
December 30, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F-292-05). (Hon. Rhonda Reid Winston, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Superior Court of the District of Columbia denied his motion to sever, defendant was convicted attempted second-degree sexual abuse against victim one; two counts of first-degree sexual abuse, two counts of second-degree sexual abuse, two counts of third-degree sexual abuse, and two counts of fourth-degree sexual abuse against victim two; and, as to both victims, attempted tampering with evidence and two counts of forgery. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant obtained criminal history about two felons who were recently released on parole or probation so he could extort sexual favors from them. He showed victim one a forged letter expressing interest in hiring defendant to locate the victim for re-prosecution. Defendant told him he could get the charges dismissed if the victim had sex with him. Victim one rejected his offer. Defendant used the same ploy on victim two, who submitted to his sexual assaults. The appellate court held that defendant's severance motion was properly denied because evidence of his guilt would have been mutually admissible to prove each offense in separate trials. Evidence of his use of the same forged letter to extort the two victims was closely intertwined evidence and placed his crimes in an understandable context. As the government conceded it failed to prove all of the elements of forgery, those convictions could not stand. The two first-degree sexual abuse convictions, based on penetration with finger and penis, did not merge into one, as these assaults were different in character, but the three lesser-included sexual abuse convictions merged into the two first-degree sexual abuse charges.


OUTCOME: The appellate court remanded the case to the trial court with directions to vacate the forgery convictions, as well as the convictions for the two counts of second-degree, third-degree, and fourth-degree sexual abuse, and to re-sentence defendant accordingly. It affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to sever and his other convictions.

 

351 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Herrington v. United States


No. 07-CF-98


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


6 A.3d 1237; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 611; 64 A.L.R.6th 725


September 30, 2009, Argued 
November 4, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, Criminal Division. (FEL 6549-05). (Hon. Lynn Leibovitz, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury trial in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, Criminal Division, defendant was convicted of unlawful possession of ammunition (UA), in violation of former D.C. Code § 7-2506.01 (2001) (current version at D.C. Code § 7-2506.01(a) (Supp. 2010)). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction was based solely on evidence that he possessed handgun ammunition in his home. Subsequently, in District of Columbia v. Heller, the U.S. Supreme Court held that the District's general ban on possession of usable handguns in the home violated the Second Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. II. As a corollary to Heller, defendant argued, his conviction also violated the Second Amendment. The appellate court agreed. The right to keep and bear arms extended to the possession of handgun ammunition in the home. As it was not proved at trial that defendant was disqualified from exercising his U.S. Const. amend. II rights by, for example, possessing ammunition for an illegal purpose, or by failing to comply with applicable registration requirements, § 7-2506.01 was unconstitutional as applied. The unconstitutionality of his conviction was plain, as he was convicted solely on proof of constitutionally protected conduct. And as convicting him in violation of the Second Amendment unquestionably affected his substantial rights, and seriously impugned the fairness, integrity, and public reputation of the judicial proceedings, all four prongs of the plain error test were met.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed defendant's conviction.

Stuart v. Walker


No. 09-CV-900


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


6 A.3d 1215; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 606


January 13, 2010, Argued 
October 28, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument granted by, Vacated by Stuart v. Walker, 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 697 (D.C., July 8, 2011)
Decision reached on appeal by, En banc Stuart v. Walker, 2012 D.C. App. LEXIS 737 (D.C., Feb. 16, 2012)
Decision reached on appeal by Stuart v. Walker, 2016 D.C. App. LEXIS 261 (D.C., July 28, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB7923-08). (Hon. Judith N. Macaluso, Trial Judge).
Walker v. Pharm. Research & Mfrs. of Am., 439 F. Supp. 2d 103, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 47982 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant atty sued appellee former client to recover attorney's fees. The client moved to compel arbitration pursuant to D.C. Bar R. XIII; the Superior Court of the District of Columbia granted the motion. The atty appealed; intervenor District of Columbia was made a party.


OVERVIEW: The attorney argued that Rule XIII was unconstitutional and beyond the appellate court's authority to promulgate. The court of appeals noted that under D.C. Code § 11-721(a)(1) (2001), its jurisdiction was limited to review of final orders of the superior court. As an order to compel arbitration was not a final appealable order because it did not dispose of the entire case on the merits, and as the superior court stayed the underlying lawsuit rather than dismissing it with prejudice, the appellate court lacked jurisdiction to hear the appeal. D.C. Code § 16-4427(a), an amendment to the District of Columbia Uniform Arbitration Act, D.C. Code § 16-4401 et seq. L. No. 93-198, 87 Stat. 774 (1973), by unlawfully expanding the court of appeals' jurisdiction to hear appeals over non-final orders of the superior court.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals dismissed the appeal.

Quincy Park Condo. Unit Owners' Ass'n v. D.C. Bd. of Zoning Adjustment


No. 09-AA-70


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


4 A.3d 1283; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 554


March 2, 2010, Argued 
September 30, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Petition for Review of an Order of the District of Columbia. Board of Zoning Adjustment. (BZA No.17729A).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District of Columbia Board of Zoning Adjustment (BZA) approved intervenor developers' proposal to expand a hotel. Petitioner condominium unit owners' association sought judicial review.


OVERVIEW: The documents intervenors provided the advisory neighborhood commission (ANC) did not indicate how close the hotel's new annex would come to the condominium. Without appreciating that fact, the ANC recommended that the application be granted. After the BZA granted intervenors setback variances and other zoning relief, the ANC, claiming it had been misled, withdrew its approval. The association claimed that by denying the ANC's request for a rehearing, the BZA breached its obligation to give "great weight" to the ANC's concerns. The appellate court disagreed. D.C. Code § 1-309.10(d)(3)(A) (2006) contemplated that the ANC's position would be considered as part of the BZA's deliberative process, not after that process was completed. D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 11, § 3113.13(b) (2000), which allowed notice of the hearing to be mailed to the association instead of each unit owner where the condominium had 25 or more units, did not deprive the owners of due process or equal protection. Notice to the association was sufficient as its officers, the owners' fiduciaries, were obliged to inform them of the hearing, and the classification was rationally related to a legitimate government interest.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition for review and affirmed the BZA's order.

Lowery v. United States


No. 06-CM-1195


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


3 A.3d 1169; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 511


October 27, 2009, Argued 
September 9, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Lowery v. United States, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 708 (U.S., Jan. 17, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CMD12018-06).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the denial of his motion to suppress, the Superior Court of the District of Columbia convicted defendant of one count of attempted possession of an unregistered firearm, in violation of D.C. Code §§ 7-2502.01(a) and 22-1803 (2001). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, for the first time on appeal, that application of the pre-Heller firearms law was unconstitutional as applied to him. The appellate court concluded that defendant failed to show that the statute was unconstitutionally applied to him, because defendant failed to show that he lawfully could have registered a firearm. The government conceded that defendant was 25 years old and had no prior convictions. But the record on appeal did not establish that defendant could have met the other requirements for registering a firearm, such as those pertaining to mental health history, prior adjudication for firearm negligence, and vision. The appellate court further concluded that a remand was not appropriate, because defendant did not preserve the Second Amendment issue for appeal. Defendant also argued that a statement he made to a deputy marshal should have been suppressed, because the marshals had no right to detain him while conducing an eviction. The appellate court found no error, noting that although defendant was detained in his underwear on a sofa in his home, the officers's weapons were holstered and he was not handcuffed or restrained and thus, not in custody.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Daniels v. United States


No. 06-CF-265


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


2 A.3d 250; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 494


October 20, 2009, Argued 
August 19, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (FEL4633-02). (Hon. John H. Bayly Jr., Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, District of Columbia, which convicted him of first-degree murder while armed, possession of a firearm during a crime of violence, and carrying a pistol without a license.


OVERVIEW: Appellant contended that the trial court committed reversible error by (1) admitting hearsay statements during the testimony of two government witnesses; (2) permitting improper prosecutorial comments during the government's rebuttal argument; and (3) convicting him of conduct protected under the Second Amendment to the Constitution. Regarding one witness, the instant court found that although the evidence established only a reasonable probability, and not a certainty, that appellant possessed the murder weapon, the connection between the witness's comment and the murder was not conjectural and remote, and so the lack of certainty went to the weight of the evidence, not its admissibility. Next, the instant court found that the prosecutor's comments relating to facts not in evidence did not rise to the level of substantial prejudice to appellant. Additionally, the trial court three times instructed the jury that the attorneys' arguments were not evidence.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

356 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Am. Bus Ass'n v. District of Columbia


No. 08-CV-808


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


2 A.3d 203; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 493


January 12, 2010, Argued 
August 19, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB6647-07). (Hon. Jeanette Jackson Clark, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant trade associations sued appellee District of Columbia, challenging the validity of D.C. Code § 50-1501.02(j) (2009), which required charter buses traveling in the District of Columbia to obtain registration on an apportioned basis, reflecting mileage driven in the District, or to obtain a trip permit. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia entered summary judgment in favor of the District. Appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: Appellants' members included non-District-based charter bus operators whose buses brought passengers into the District and thus were subject to the requirements of § 50-1501.02(j). Appellants argued that § 50-1501.02(j)'s registration scheme discriminated against and unduly burdened interstate commerce and therefore violated the dormant Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The appellate court disagreed. Section 50-1501.02(j) did not on its face, or because of its intent or its practical effect, discriminate against or burden interstate commerce. Its focus was the impact on the infrastructure and environment of buses that used the District's roadways, not on the buses' crossing jurisdictional lines into the city, and there was no evidence that the fees it imposed, which were not excessive, deterred interstate activities. There was no conflict between § 50-1501.02(j) and the International Registration Plan (IRP), as the IRP did not, as appellants claimed, prohibit an IRP member jurisdiction like the District from requiring vehicles that were not "apportionable" from choosing between apportioned registration and some other forms of registration and fee payment.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Jackson v. D.C. Bd. of Elections & Ethics


No. 10-CV-20


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


999 A.2d 89; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 400


May 4, 2010, Argued 
July 15, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Jackson v. Dc Bd. of Elections, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 730 (U.S., Jan. 18, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB-8613-09). (Hon. Judith N. Macaluso, Trial Judge).
Jackson v. D.C. Bd. of Elections & Ethics, 2009 D.C. Super. LEXIS 9 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants proposed an initiative to repeal the District of Columbia Jury and Marriage Amendment Act of 2009 (JAMA), D.C. Code § 46-405.01 (2009 Supp.) and which provided that only heterosexual marriage was valid. Appellee District of Columbia Board of Elections and Ethics rejected the measure. Appellants sought a writ of mandamus. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia granted the Board summary judgment. Appellants sought further review.


OVERVIEW: JAMA provided that the District would recognize valid same-sex marriages entered into in other jurisdictions. The Board rejected the initiative on the ground that it would authorize or have the effect of authorizing discrimination barred by the D.C. Human Rights Act (HRA). The appellate court held that the HRA safeguard set out in D.C. Code § 1-1001.16(b)(1)(C) (2006) was not inconsistent with the District of Columbia Council's intent as conveyed by the language of the D.C. Charter Amendments Act (CAA), D.C. Code §§ 1-204.101 - 1-204.115. Substantial deference was owed to the Council's interpretation that the HRA safeguard carried out the intent of the CAA. The Council could not have intended to authorize, as a proper subject of initiative, any initiative that would have the effect of authorizing discrimination prohibited by the HRA. The D.C. Home Rule Act, Pub. L. No. 93-198, 87 Stat. 777, gave the Council the authority to direct the Board, through legislation implementing the CAA, to refuse to accept an initiative that would authorize prohibited discrimination. The Board correctly determined that the proposed initiative would have the effect of authorizing such discrimination.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court upholding the Board's determination was affirmed.

Padou v. District of Columbia


No. 09-CV-390


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


998 A.2d 286; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 334


May 18, 2010, Argued 
June 17, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by, in part, Remanded by, in part Padou v. District of Columbia, 2011 D.C. App. LEXIS 607 (D.C., Oct. 20, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB8274-08). (Hon. Judith E. Retchin, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens filed a pro se amended complaint alleging that appellee District of Columbia (District) violated their U.S. Const. amend. I, constitutional rights by selectively removing posters announcing a rally to protest the District's failure to bury utility lines as proposed in a Department of Transportation study. The citizens appealed the Superior Court of the District of Columbia's grant of summary judgment in favor of the District.


OVERVIEW: The citizen's posters were removed by a District employee allegedly because they violated D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 24 §§ 108.7, 108.10, and 108.11. However, the citizens' complaint alleged that the District's removal of the posters was selective and based solely on the content of the posters and that commercial posters that did meet statutory requirements were not removed. The trial court improperly interpreted the citizens' First Amendment selective enforcement claim as an equal protection claim, rather than as a First Amendment contention that, as applied to them, § 108 was unconstitutional. The trial court erred in not advising the citizens of the pleading requirements for a summary judgment motion and in not affording sufficient time for discovery to properly defend the motion. The trial court also erred in considering the First Amendment overbreadth doctrine before considering the citizens' as applied issue, which was sufficiently raised in the citizens' filings.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of the District and remanded the action with instructions to afford the parties an opportunity to conduct discovery followed by further proceedings.

In re T.L.


No. 06-FS-798


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


996 A.2d 805; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 279


April 9, 2009, Argued 
June 3, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia Family Court. (No. DEL-2728-05). (Hon. Juliet J. McKenna, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia Family Court, which adjudicated him a juvenile delinquent for disorderly conduct under D.C. Code § 22-1231 (2001) and for possessing cocaine with the intent to distribute.


OVERVIEW: The sixteen-year-old juvenile was arrested for loudly yelling in protest and calling for his mother, on the street in a residential neighborhood in the middle of the night, after a police officer confiscated his money without permission or right. The trial court adjudicated him a juvenile delinquent for disorderly conduct and for possessing cocaine with the intent to distribute. On appeal, the court reversed. Under D.C. Code § 22-1321 (2001), the juvenile's loud words, though they may have annoyed or disturbed nearby residents, did not threaten a breach of the peace or manifest an intent on his part to provoke one. The police therefore lacked probable cause to arrest him for disorderly conduct, and the trial court should have granted his motion to suppress the drugs discovered on his person in a search incident to his arrest. Because the evidence at trial did not suffice to prove disorderly conduct, the trial court should have acquitted him of that charge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the juvenile delinquency adjudication.

Modern Mgmt. Co. v. Wilson


Nos. 08-CV-18, 08-CV-85 & 08-CV-187


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


997 A.2d 37; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 283


September 24, 2009, Argued 
June 3, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Abell v. Wilson, 132 S. Ct. 111, 181 L. Ed. 2d 36, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 5544 (U.S., 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
On Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (04-CA-7270B). (Hon. Mary A. Gooden-Terrell, Trial Judge).
Hughes v. Abell, 634 F. Supp. 2d 110, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 62495 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action by appellee homeowner alleging common law fraud and violations of the D.C. Consumer Protection Procedures Act (CPPA), D.C. Code § 28-3905, appellants, alleged foreclosure specialists, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which awarded compensatory and punitive damages to the homeowner.


OVERVIEW: Days prior to her foreclosure, the foreclosure specialists sought out the homeowner and persuaded her to enter into an agreement whereby she was still liable for the mortgage payments but became a tenant of her house. When they evicted her, she filed an action against them, and the jury found that they were all liable for common law fraud and for violating the CPPA. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment because the punitive damages were not grossly excessive or violative of due process; the punitive awards did not reflect the jury's attempt to punish them for their previous transactions with non-parties in other foreclosures. The award of treble and punitive damages was proper because their actions were particularly malicious; the homeowner was tricked into selling the home that she owned for over twenty-two years in exchange for a mere $ 5,000, even though expert trial testimony established that the fair market value of the house was $ 300,000 at the time of the transaction The court remanded the case to the trial court to apply a setoff to the treble award in the amount of a settlement the homeowner had reached with another party.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment but remanded and directed the trial court to apply a setoff to the treble award.

Riddick v. United States


No. 07-CF-875


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


995 A.2d 212; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 264


October 22, 2009, Argued 
May 13, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F-1314-05). (Hon. Wendell P. Gardner, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, entered on a jury's verdict, convicting him of second-degree murder, possession of a firearm during a crime of violence, carrying a pistol without a license (CPWL), and CPWL outside the home, a violation of D.C. Code § 22-4504(a) (2001).


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the shooting of the victim, his girlfriend, was accidental. In support of his claim, he offered a handwritten note found in the victim's bedroom. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in excluding the note from evidence because there was no indication of when the note was written. However, whether or not the trial court erred in excluding the note, any error in its exclusion was harmless. The government's case was strong and undermined defendant's account of what occurred when the victim was shot. Even if the jury had seen the note, it was questionable whether they would have been willing to draw the inference that the victim confronted defendant with a loaded gun when he attempted to leave. The note was not evidence that in substantial measure would have corroborated defendant's account. Next, defendant sought reversal of his CPWL convictions on U.S. Const. amend. II grounds. Plain error review was applied because defendant did not raise the claim in the trial court. The CPWL statute, § 22-4504(a), was constitutional both on its face and as applied to defendant's case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions.

 

362 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Debruhl


No. 09-CO-1208


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


993 A.2d 571; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 208


February 4, 2010, Argued 
April 22, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Different results reached on rehearing at, Remanded by United States v. Debruhl, 2012 D.C. App. LEXIS 68 (D.C., Feb. 23, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-982-09). (Hon. Gerald I. Fisher, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with possession of a controlled substance with intent to distribute, in violation of D.C. Code § 48-904.01(a)(1) (2001), and possession of drug paraphernalia, in violation of D.C. Code § 48-1103(a) (2001). Defendant filed a motion to suppress the evidence. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia granted defendant's motion, holding that the search violated U.S. Const. amend. IV. The government appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for a traffic violation. After he was removed from his car and handcuffed, the arresting officers conducted a warrantless search of the passenger compartment of his vehicle. Before defendant's trial, the U.S. Supreme Court decided Arizona v Grant, 129 S. Ct. 1710 (2009), which precluded warrantless searches of an automobile after the occupants had been removed and secured with handcuffs, and thus no longer remained a threat to police safety or to preservation of evidence. Grant's revised interpretation of the Fourth Amendment applied retroactively. The good faith exception to the exclusionary rule did not apply. Prior to Grant, the law was not settled regarding warrantless searches incident to a lawful arrest where the occupants had been removed and secured. Even if prior case law may have been widely understood to allow warrantless searches of vehicle passenger compartments without limitation incident to an arrest, momentum against such a sweeping interpretation on facts such as those in the instant case had been accelerating in the courts for years before defendant's arrest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Little v. United States


No. 06-CF-140


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


989 A.2d 1096; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 82


February 18, 2009, Argued 
February 25, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
On Appeal from the Criminal Division of the Superior Court. (F-1292-04). (Hon. Erik P. Christian, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions of armed robbery (AR), assault with a dangerous weapon (ADW), aggravated assault while armed (AAWA), possession of a firearm during a crime of violence (PFCV), carrying a pistol without a license (CPWL), possession of an unregistered firearm (UF), and unlawful possession of ammunition (UA) entered by the Criminal Division of the Superior Court (D.C.).


OVERVIEW: Defendant and three other men robbed and assaulted a drug dealer and his friend in the drug dealer's home. The drug dealer was shot in the head and the friend had his throat cut with a knife. The appellate court found that even if there were error in extending the CPWL, UF, or UA prohibitions to defendant's conduct, he could not show that such error was "plain." The evidence effectively established not only that defendant aided and abetted knowingly and intentionally in the possession of a firearm during a crime of violence (armed robbery), carrying a pistol without a license, possession of an unregistered firearm, and unlawful possession of ammunition, at a minimum, but that he was actually the principal. The evidence also showed that defendant took several deliberate actions to aid his confederates who did possess firearms. Even if defendant was able to establish that admission of the certificates of no record of firearms registration (CNRs) was plain error that affected his substantial rights, reversal would not be required because, he could not establish that the error seriously affects the fairness, integrity or public reputation of judicial proceedings.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed.
