DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


987 A.2d 1134; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 22


December 2, 2009, Submitted 
January 28, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (04-CA-6667B). (Hon. Melvin R. Wright, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sued appellee department of motor vehicles (DMV), alleging wrongful termination. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia dismissed the case. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee was a hearing examiner for the DMV. He was terminated after he consistently dismissed tickets generated by the Automated Traffic Enforcement system. The employee appealed to the Office of Employee Appeals (OEA), which sustained the termination. The employee filed suit in the superior court, which bifurcated the case and affirmed the OEA decision. The employee appealed that decision, but his appeal was dismissed. The employee then filed a second amended complaint, which alleged that he was "wrongfully terminated." The trial court held that the ATE statute was constitutional and that the employee lacked standing to challenge it, and dismissed the case. The appellate court found that, once he abandoned judicial review of the OEA decision, the employee was precluded from filing a separate action challenging his termination. The complaint was properly dismissed. Under the Comprehensive Merit Personnel Act, D.C. Code § 1-601.01 et seq. (2001), common law wrongful termination claims were preempted and the sole recourse to challenge a termination was by appeal to the OEA. The trial court had no jurisdiction to address the counts of the complaint related to the termination.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court dismissing the complaint was affirmed.

 

365 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Bergman v. District of Columbia


No. 08-CV-859


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


986 A.2d 1208; 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 3


October 1, 2009, Argued 
January 14, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bergman v. District of Columbia, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 5915 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CAB7992-06). (Hon. Judith Bartnoff, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District of Columbia Superior Court granted appellees, the District of Columbia and its council members (District), summary judgment in a challenge to the White Collar Insurance Fraud Prosecution Enhancement Amendment Act of 2006 (Act), D.C. Code § 22-3225.14 (Supp. 2009), on First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, District of Columbia Home Rule Act (HRA), D.C. Code § 1-201.01 et seq. (Supp. 2007), and separation of powers grounds.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorney appealed. The district's highest court held that the Act did not violate the First Amendment. It addressed and materially advanced the District's substantial interest in the protection of consumers from unsolicited and often distressing intrusions upon their privacy, effected to secure their business in the aftermath of an automobile accident. The Act was narrowly drawn as it targeted the problems described in the legislative record--intrusive personal solicitation by practitioners, or their agents or runners, in the immediate aftermath of an accident. The Act constituted a reasonable means to achieve the legitimate objectives of the council. The Act was not aimed at the content of a practitioner's speech, but at the offensive behavior that often accompanied its delivery. The Act was not designed to suppress a viewpoint that the attorney espoused. The Act was an exercise of the council's police power to enact legislation for the protection of residents of the District of Columbia from intrusive and exploitative practices. It did not violate the HRA, interfere with the district's highest court's authority over attorneys, or violate separation of powers.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

366 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Plummer v. United States


No. 04-CF-857


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


983 A.2d 323; 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 572


March 11, 2008; January 15, 2009, Argued 
November 12, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reprinted as amended at Plummer v. United States, 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 753 (D.C., Nov. 12, 2009)
Amended by, Modified by, Rehearing denied by, Rehearing, en banc, denied by Plummer v. United States, 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 785 (D.C., May 20, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F6520-03). (Hon. Judith Retchin, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Superior Court of the District of Columbia denied defendant's motions to suppress and to dismiss the indictment on U.S. Const. II grounds, he was convicted of carrying a pistol without a license (former D.C. Code § 22-4504) and possession of an unregistered firearm (former D.C. Code § 7-502.01). He appealed.


OVERVIEW: A 911 caller reported a man carrying a handgun. Guns drawn, police approached defendant, who matched the description given by the caller, and told him to turn around and put his hands up. He seemed to try to pull something out of his pants; after the officers repeated the order several times, he put his hands up. Officers frisked him, found a gun, and arrested him. The appellate court held that as defendant failed to comply with orders to put his hands up, he did not submit to authority and thus was not "seized" at that time; and as police had reasonable, articulable suspicion to stop defendant, his motion to suppress was properly denied. Defendant had standing to raise the Second Amendment, U.S. Const. II, as a defense by moving to dismiss the indictment, even though he had not tried to obtain a registration certificate and license for his handgun before his arrest. Former §§ 7-2502.01(a) and 22-4504(a) were not facially invalid under the Second Amendment. However, because the trial court resolved the Second Amendment issue under pre-Heller law, it did not reach the issue of whether defendant would have been disqualified from registering under D.C. Code § 7-2502.03.


OUTCOME: The appellate court remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to hold a hearing to determine whether defendant would have satisfied the statutory requirements in § 7-2502.03.

Plummer v. United States


No. 04-CF-857


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 753


March 11, 2008, Argued; January 15, 2009, Argued 
November 12, 2009, Decided


NOTICE:

THIS OPINION IS SUBJECT TO FORMAL REVISION BEFORE PUBLICATION IN THE ATLANTIC AND MARYLAND REPORTERS. USERS ARE REQUESTED TO NOTIFY THE CLERK OF THE COURT OF ANY FORMAL ERRORS SO THAT CORRECTIONS MAY BE MADE BEFORE THE BOUND VOLUMES GO TO PRESS.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] 
Amended May 20, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F6520-03). (Hon. Judith Retchin, Trial Judge).
Plummer v. United States, 983 A.2d 323, 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 572 (D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which denied his motion to suppress and convicted him of carrying a pistol without a license (CPWL), and the offense of possession of an unregistered firearm (UF), in violation of D.C. Code § 7-502.01.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his convictions for CPWL and UF, asserting that the policy seized him under U.S. Const. amend. IV when they drew their weapons and ordered him to turn around and put his hands up. The appellate court concluded that he was not seized when the police approached him with their guns drawn and ordered him to put up his hands because he did not comply with that show of authority. Furthermore, at the time he was seized after complying with the order to put up his hands, the police had suitable corroboration showing reliability that he was the person identified in an anonymous 911 call as carrying a gun, and that because of his repeated movements to his waist where guns were commonly concealed, the police had suitable corroboration demonstrating reliability that he was engaged in the criminal act of carrying a gun. Consequently, the police had reasonable, articulable suspicion to stop him, and the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress. D.C. Code §§ 7-2502.01 (a) and 22-4504 (a) were not facially invalid.


OUTCOME: The appellate court was constrained to remand the case to the trial court with instructions to hold a hearing to determine whether, prior to the imposition of charges, defendant would have been able to satisfy the then existing and applicable statutory and regulatory requirements for obtaining a registration certificate and license for his handgun.

 

368 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Gaffney v. United States


No. 04-CF-1103


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


980 A.2d 1190; 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 464


September 19, 2007, Argued 
September 17, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (No. F-470-04). (Hon. Thomas J. Motley, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Superior Court of the District of Columbia for perjury. Defendant challenged the sufficiency of the evidence to support his conviction for perjury before the grand jury.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted on two counts of perjury for statements he made under oath before a grand jury investigating a murder. The target of the investigation was suspected of having ordered the murder. Defendant made three negative responses during his grand jury testimony to questions posed by the prosecutor. Under oath, he testified (1) that he had not spoken with anyone other than his girlfriend about the shooting; (2) that he had not told anyone he had information regarding who killed the victim; and (3) that no one ever had spoken to him about the suspect. The appellate court held the first two statements were contradicted by testimony that defendant told the witness that the suspect had arranged for the victim to be murdered. However, that testimony was not corroborated by any independent evidence. The third statement was not contradicted by any testimony as neither witness was asked whether he or she had spoken to defendant about the suspect, and neither claimed to have done so. If either witness in fact had said anything to defendant about the suspect, it was incumbent on the government to elicit that fact in order to prove that defendant lied when he denied it.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed. The matter was remanded for entry of a judgment of acquittal.

 

369 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Hunter v. United States


No. 06-CF-539


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


980 A.2d 1158; 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 459


January 9, 2008, Argued 
September 17, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F 6533-04). (Hon. Harold L. Cushenberry, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by the Superior Court of the District of Columbia of possession of an unregistered firearm under D.C. Code § 7-2502.01, unlawful possession of ammunition under D.C. Code § 7-2506.01, assault, and two counts of threatening to injure a person in violation of D.C. Code § 22-1810 (2001). Defendant appealed his convictions, except the assault conviction.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that defendant uttered one threat to his former girlfriend's mother when he told her he would blow her head off, and that he uttered a new threat when he directed his attention to his former girlfriend, striking her and uttering a second threat, directed to both victims collectively. Since the threats took place sequentially, the convictions did not merge under the Double Jeopardy Clause, U.S. Const. amend. V. Evidence of the TP's prior convictions for drug trafficking while armed and possession of a pistol did not indicate that someone else committed the charged offenses. The prior convictions were not Winfield or reverse-Drew evidence. However, the prior convictions were relevant. The prior convictions had a tendency to prove that defendant did not know about the gun in his current girlfriend's closet, or have ready access to it. The error was harmless as defendant's threat to the mother was strong evidence that he had access to a gun. Further, the TP's pistol had been recovered, and the trial court was justified in trying to prevent jury confusion by keeping out evidence of limited probative value. Ownership of the gun was not dispositive.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed.

 

370 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Hinton v. United States


No. 01-CF-1145


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


979 A.2d 663; 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 386


April 29, 2009, Argued En Banc 
September 3, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (No. F-459-01). (Hon. Zoe Bush, Trial Judge).
Hinton v. United States, 951 A.2d 773, 2008 D.C. App. LEXIS 279 (D.C., 2008)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 24(c), the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, over defendant's objection, removed an empaneled juror and replaced him with an alternate. Defendant was convicted of one count of possessing a controlled substance with the intent to distribute in a drug-free zone. He appealed; a three-judge panel of the appellate court affirmed. Defendant's request for en banc review was granted.


OVERVIEW: The questions Juror 8 submitted to be put to the witnesses revealed his skepticism about the State's evidence. The trial court, finding that his questions indicated that he would have difficulty deliberating, replaced him with an alternate. The panel held that while the trial court violated the standards of Rule 24(c), the error was harmless because defendant did not show that as a result of Juror 8's removal, an impaneled juror failed to conscientiously apply the law and find the facts. The appellate court held that defendant had standing to object to the removal of the juror in contravention of Rule 24(c), as this implicated his right to trial by an impartial jury. A showing that defendant had a biased jury was not a prerequisite to a finding that the trial court violated Rule 24(c); (Nathaniel) Thomas v. United States, 824 A.2d 26 (D.C. 2003), which held otherwise, was overruled. The record was inadequate to justify the trial court's implicit finding that Juror 8 was "unable" to perform his juror duties; his questions had a reasonable basis in the evidence and were not objectionable. The error was not harmless, as Juror 8 might well have hung the jury by voting to acquit.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial.

Brown v. United States


No. 02-CF-1313


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


979 A.2d 630; 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 370


January 28, 2009, Argued 
August 27, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Brown v. United States, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 9811 (U.S., Dec. 13, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (FEL-1212-99). (Hon. Lee F. Satterfield, Motions Judge). (Hon. Robert I. Richter, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of the District of Columbia convicted defendant of first-degree murder while armed under D.C. Code §§ 22-2101, -4502(a) (1999 Supp.), possession of a firearm during a crime of violence under D.C. Code § 22-4504(b) (1999 Supp.), and carrying a pistol without a license (CPWL) under D.C. Code § 22-4504(a)(1) (1999 Supp.). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the trial court erred in failing to suppress a videotaped statement he made to police, because it was obtained following an unreasonable delay in presentment, in violation of D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 5(a). The appellate court found no error, noting that defendant's waiver of his Miranda rights also waived his right to presentment without unnecessary delay. Defendant also argued that the evidence was insufficient to support is CPWL conviction, as the evidence was insufficient to show he carried or was armed with a pistol where no evidence was presented about the length of the barrel of the gun. The court found that rational jurors could not have failed to find that the barrel of the gun was less than 12 inches, noting, inter alia, defendant's reference to the firearm as a pistol and his testimony that he and the victim each carried their gun in their waistband. The appellate court also rejected defendant's claim that the CPWL statute was invalid, on its face and as applied, noting, inter alia, that prohibiting an adjudicated delinquent still under sentence from obtaining a license to carry a pistol served an important governmental objective.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Tetaz v. District of Columbia


Nos. 07-CT-140, 07-CT-141, 07-CT-262, 07-CT-271, 07-CT-272, 07-CT-273, 07-CT-284, 07-CT-285, 07-CT-410, 07-CT-434


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


976 A.2d 907; 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 333


April 2, 2009, Argued 
July 30, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CDC-21526-06, et al). (Hon. Rufus G. King, III, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District of Columbia Superior Court entered final judgments of conviction after if found defendants guilty of unlawful assembly or unlawfully crossing police lines during demonstrations involved in protests of the Iraq war. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants engaged in protests against the Iraq war at buildings that were on or near the grounds of the Unites States Capitol. As a result of that activity that occurred on successive days, they were convicted of unlawfully assembly in violation of D.C. Code § 22-1307 (2001) or unlawfully crossing police lines in violation of D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 24, § 2100.3 (1996). Regarding convictions as to a demonstration at one building, defendants on appeal did not challenge them regarding unlawful assembly and the appellate court thus conclude any such challenge was waived. As to the remaining convictions, the appellate court found that the Government did not have to prove an actual or imminent breach of the peace so long as their intent to impede entry was shown, which it concluded was shown. It further found that defendants did not show that their First Amendment rights were violated regarding the convictions for unlawfully crossing police lines, as police pursuant to D.C. Code § 10-503.16(b)(7) (2001) could bar demonstrations within any of the Capitol Buildings and the police were not shown to have set up the police lines to thwart the expression of any particular message.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgments of conviction.

Jones v. United States


No. 06-CF-1000


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


972 A.2d 821; 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 183


September 10, 2008, Submitted 
June 4, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F-1520-06). (Hon. Craig S. Iscoe, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the denial of his motion to suppress, a jury in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia convicted defendant of carrying a pistol without a license, possession of an unregistered firearm, possession of unregistered ammunition, and possession of marijuana. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence, because the traffic checkpoint was an unconstitutional roadblock. The appellate court concluded that the checkpoint was not an impermissible roadblock and that the issue was whether the officers had reasonable articulable suspicion to believe defendant was violating a motor vehicle law and whether he was seized while approaching the checkpoint before such suspicion materialized. The appellate court held that defendant was lawfully arrested for driving without a license and that his van and his person were lawfully searched incident to that arrest. Defendant was not seized when he was slowly merging into one lane to pass through the checkpoint. The checkpoint was not a roadblock, as the police stopped only those cars presenting noticeable traffic violations, such as defendant's lack of a rear-view mirror. Defendant also contended that the evidence was insufficient to support his convictions, as there was no evidence he intended to possess the loaded gun. The appellate court disagreed, as the jury could have inferred possession from the fact that the gun was just below where defendant sat.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Perez v. United States


Nos. 99-CF-107, 02-CO-1242 & 06-CO-341, Nos. 99-CF-207 & 02-CO-1205, Nos. 99-CF-212, 02-CO-1385 & 06-CO-1164, Nos. 99-CF-226, 02-CO-1253 & 06-CO-1563, Nos. 99-CF-257, 01-CO-1019, 02-CO-1231 & 06-CO-1170

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected April 6, 2009.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Villatoro v. United States, 130 S. Ct. 474, 175 L. Ed. 2d 317, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 7527 (U.S., 2009)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bonilla v. United States, 130 S. Ct. 473, 175 L. Ed. 2d 317, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 7505 (U.S., 2009)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Bonilla v. Wainwright, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 79767 (D.D.C., July 22, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (Hon. Mary Ellen Abrecht, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal arising out of a murder and various assaults, five defendants challenged their convictions in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. Among other crimes, four defendants were convicted of conspiracy to assault and to commit murder, in violation of D.C. Code §§ 22-105(a), -2401, -3202 (1996 Supp.), and first-degree premeditated murder while armed, in violation of D.C. Code §§ 22-2401, -3202 (1996 Supp.).


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals found that although the prosecutor's use of subpoenas and payment of fees intended for grand jury witnesses was improper, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in rejecting defendants' new trial motions (D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 33) because the abuse did not lead to subornation of perjured testimony that was presented at trial by the prosecutor. In participating in an assault on a victim with the knowledge that one of his cohorts carried a knife, a defendant acted with a wanton and willful disregard of an unreasonable risk to human life. Therefore, he had the requisite intent for second-degree murder. The evidence from which the jury could have inferred another defendant had the specific intent to kill a victim and did so with premeditation or deliberation was circumstantial and its source was impeached. The case against another defendant for assault was based on an eyewitness's testimony that he saw defendant knock a homeless man to the ground, and neither of three other uncalled witnesses had anything to say in this regard. The trial court did not err in denying, without a hearing, his motion for a new trial based on ineffectiveness of counsel.


OUTCOME: The first-degree murder while armed convictions of three defendants were reversed and their cases remanded with instructions to enter, instead, convictions for murder in the second degree while armed and resentence them. On remand, the trial court could consider whether another defendant's conviction of first-degree murder while armed should stand, and appoint new counsel if appropriate. In all other respects, the convictions were affirmed.

Howard v. United States


No. 08-CM-317


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


966 A.2d 854; 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 42


February 18, 2009, Argued 
March 5, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CMD28367-07). (Hon. Bruce D. Beaudin, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged her conviction in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia of assaulting, resisting, or interfering with a police officer (APO), in violation of D.C. Code § 22-405(b) (2001).


OVERVIEW: The trial judge found defendant guilty based solely on evidence that she failed to take her hands out of her pockets despite a lawful order to do so. The appeals court found that the trial court based its finding of guilt on an erroneous interpretation of the statute. Defendant did not actively or physically oppose or interfere with the officers simply by failing to remove her hands from her pockets. In light of the holdings in C.L.D., E.D.P., and Dolson, this aspect of defendant's conduct did not cross the line into active confrontation, obstruction, or other action directed against an officer's performance in the line of duty. In contrast, the evidence that defendant resisted officers' attempts to arrest her by swinging her arms and elbows and striking them, if credited by the finder of fact, would be amply sufficient to sustain a conviction under the APO statute, but the testimony from an officer and defendant was conflicting, and the trial court did not make any credibility determinations or factual findings to resolve those conflicts.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Superior Court was vacated, and the case was remanded so that the trial court could determine whether to convict based on a more comprehensive view of the evidence.

Howerton v. United States


Nos. 04-CF-1058 & 06-CO-599


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


964 A.2d 1282; 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 32


March 11, 2008, Argued 
February 19, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (FEL-631-04). (Hon. Rafael Diaz, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia of assault with a dangerous weapon (D.C. Code § 22-402 (2001)), threatening to injure a person (D.C. Code § 22-1810 (2001)), carrying a pistol without a license (D.C. Code § 22-4504(a) (2001)), possessing an unregistered firearm (D.C. Code § 7-2502.01 (2001)), possessing ammunition (D.C. Code § 7-2506.01(3) (2001)), and drug offenses. He appealed his weapons convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant said his weapons convictions violated the Second Amendment. The appellate court held the Second Amendment barred absolute bans on handguns held for self-defense in the home, where he had the gun in question, but nothing showed he had it for self-defense. The statutes he was convicted under were not facially unconstitutional or invalidated by a finding that, collectively, the District of Columbia's gun-control laws violated the Second Amendment. He did not show his convictions were plain error, as he used a gun to threaten another instead of for a lawful purpose. Sufficient evidence showed he carried a pistol without a license because no proof of carrying it outside his home was required, it was "convenient of access" to him when found, and he used it two days earlier. Not moving to sever drug-related charges from the assault and threats charges was not ineffective because the motion would have been denied as the charges arose from sufficiently related transactions, under D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 8(a), and no prejudice requiring severance was shown as the crimes were not similar, the evidence as to each was distinct, and the jury was told to consider them separately.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed.

Sims v. United States


No. 06-CF-137


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


963 A.2d 147; 2008 D.C. App. LEXIS 493


May 15, 2008, Submitted 
December 31, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F-2664-05). (Hon. Erik P. Christian, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which convicted him of carrying a pistol without a license (CPWL), possession of an unregistered firearm (UF), and unlawful possession of ammunition (UA).


OVERVIEW: A witness testified that defendant had confronted him on the street with the gun after the two had a brief dispute over a debt in defendant's home. A police officer saw defendant throw a pistol into the rear yard of a dwelling, which was not defendant's residence. On appeal, the court affirmed. While defendant claimed that U.S. Const. amend. II barred his prosecution for the weapons offenses as the CPWL, UF, and UA statutes were constitutionally invalid on their face in light of District of Columbia v. Heller, 128 S. Ct. 2783 (2008), the claim was not preserved as it was not raised in the trial court. The court noted that the U.S. Const. amend. II challenge was not jurisdictional and thus could be waived. Applying plain error review to defendant's claim, the argument failed at the threshold as Heller dealt with possession of a firearm in one's residence. Additionally, the evidence did not link defendant's possession of the gun to any arguable motive of self defense that would cause him to remove the gun from his home.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Gause v. United States


Nos. 06-CF-20 & 06-CF-47


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


959 A.2d 671; 2008 D.C. App. LEXIS 432


June 17, 2008, Argued 
October 30, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, granted by, Vacated by Gause v. United States, 968 A.2d 1032, 2009 D.C. App. LEXIS 58 (D.C., 2009)
Reaffirmed, in part, Adopted by, in part, Rejected by, in part, On reconsideration by, En banc Gause v. United States, 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 613 (D.C., Nov. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F-3701-05 & F-3694-05). (Hon. Hiram Puig-Lugo, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia convicted defendants of armed robbery of a senior citizen and of weapons charges. Defendants challenged the denial of a D.C. Code § 11-1910 motion for discovery of jury-selection records and requests for other jury information, claiming violations of the Fifth and Sixth Amendments and the District of Columbia Jury System Act (DCJSA), D.C. Code § 11-1901 et seq. (2001).


OVERVIEW: The question presented was whether the trial judge correctly denied the § 11-1910 motion and request for discovery for failure to present a prima facie case of a violation of the Constitution or of the DCJSA in the motion. Defendant claimed that the jury panels were disproportionately Caucasian because professionals, who tended to be Caucasian, asked to be deferred, and were given Mondays to come back to court. The court reviewed the comparable federal statute, 28 U.S.C.S. § 1867(f), and found that the DCJSA omitted language that created the unqualified right to inspect jury records prior to the filing of a motion that alleged constitutional or statutory deficiencies in the juror selection process. A criminal defendant in the District did not have the same unqualified right as his federal counterpart. In the District of Columbia, if there was in fact reason to believe that constitutional or statutory violations occurred, it was not unduly difficult for a competent attorney to satisfy the relatively modest standard that access to the records was available to a defendant only if there was reason to believe that there had been a constitutional or statutory violation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision on the jury motion and remanded the case for a determination of whether defendants demonstrated a reason to believe that the jury selection violated the Constitution or the DCJSA. If such reason to believe existed, then the trial court was to grant discovery, set a hearing, and rule on the motion's merits. If there was no reason to believe that a violation occurred, the request for discovery was to be denied.

 

379 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Banks v. United States


No. 07-CF-127


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


955 A.2d 709; 2008 D.C. App. LEXIS 354


March 27, 2008, Submitted 
July 31, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CF2-20318-06). (Hon. Jeanette Jackson Clark, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court of the District of Columbia for a violation of 40 U.S.C.S. § 6134, which prohibited, inter alia, the discharge of a firearm, firework, or explosive in the United States Supreme Court Building or on the grounds.


OVERVIEW: A police officer observed defendant on the front plaza of the Supreme Court; when the officer approached defendant to tell him he was not permitted to demonstrate on the grounds, defendant lifted an object over his head and fired some sort of projectile. The officer determined that the object was a flare gun after defendant threw it down. Defendant claimed that shooting a flare gun was not within any of the categories of activities that § 6134 made unlawful and that § 6134 was unconstitutionally vague. On appeal, the court affirmed. Although "firework" was not defined in § 6134, the trial court properly used the dictionary definition to determine that the flare gun was covered under the statute; defendant acknowledged that the flare gun was a signaling device, and a firearms instructor testified that the projectile from a flare gun caused a flame when it was ejected. Because defendant failed to make his constitutional argument below, the plain error standard was applied. Section 6134 was not unconstitutionally vague because the criminal offense was defined with sufficient definiteness and defendant could reasonably understand that his conduct was proscribed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Watergate E. Comm. Against Hotel Conversion to Co-Op Apts. v. D.C. Zoning Comm'n


No. 04-AA-1056


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


953 A.2d 1036; 2008 D.C. App. LEXIS 333


February 22, 2006, Argued 
July 24, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
(ZC03-16).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a committee, filed a petition to review the order of respondent, the District of Columbia Zoning Commission, which approved a modification to the Planned Unit Development (PUD) of intervenor developer. The controversy was sparked by the applicant's plan to convert a hotel into a cooperatively-owned apartment building. The committed filed several objections to the Commission's findings and challenged certain of its procedures.


OVERVIEW: At issue was a historic hotel, which the developer sought to modify to covert it into 133 cooperative apartments. The application also proposed increasing the parking available, making minor changes to the exterior of the building and roof area, and modifying, but not eliminating, the health club and restaurant facilities. The committee asserted various procedural and substantive challenges to the Commission's approval of the modified PUD. The court addressed each challenge and found no merit to any of the contentions. The court disagreed with the committee's contention that the Commission adopted the applicant's findings virtually verbatim. The court found that the Commission added sentences and phrases, changed sentence structure, referenced the applicable regulations, changed the grammar, and, in some places, added entirely new paragraphs. The court found that the approved modifications did not interfere with any vested interest as there was no vested right in zoning classifications for anyone to assert. Ultimately, the court found that the Commission's order was supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

Fields v. United States


No. 06-CF-894


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


952 A.2d 859; 2008 D.C. App. LEXIS 128


September 28, 2007, Argued 
April 10, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F-832-05). (Hon. Harold L. Cushenberry, Jr., Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia of possession of marijuana in violation of D.C. Code § 48-904.01(d) (2001). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The parties agreed that a report prepared by the Drug Enforcement Administration, which determined that the green weed substance found on and near defendant was marijuana, was erroneously admitted into evidence in violation of defendant's constitutional right to confrontation under the Sixth Amendment. The parties disagreed as to the appropriate remedy. The government argued that the case was to be remanded with instructions to the trial court to vacate the conviction of possession and enter a conviction for the lesser-included offense of attempted possession of marijuana. Defendant countered that he was entitled to reversal and a new trial because the admission of the report was not harmless error even as to the lesser-included offense. On appeal, the court found that the erroneous admission of the report without an opportunity for defendant to cross-examine the chemist who prepared it was not harmless beyond a reasonable doubt as to the offense of attempted possession of marijuana because it could not be said that the error did not contribute to the verdict, and the government did not otherwise present overwhelming evidence that defendant intended to possess marijuana.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for a new trial.

 

382 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Embassy Real Estate Holdings, LLC v. D.C. Mayor's Agent For Historic Preservation


No. 06-AA-1083


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


944 A.2d 1036; 2008 D.C. App. LEXIS 107


March 27, 2007, Argued 
March 20, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Petition for Review of a Decision of the Mayor's Agent for Historic Preservation. (No. 06-171).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Historic Preservation Review Board (HPRB) held that the issuance of construction permits to petitioner property owner was inconsistent with a former embassy's designation as a historical landmark under the Historic Landmark and Historic District Protection Act, D.C. Code § 6-1101 et seq. (2001), respondent Mayor's Agent for Historic Preservation affirmed the HPRB's decision. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The building was designated a landmark after the owner had applied for permits. The court first held that the HPRB and the Mayor's Agent had jurisdiction to review the permits under the Historical Preservation Act. D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 10A, § 209.5 contemplated that historical landmark applications might be filed after permit applications were filed. Next, the Mayor's Agent properly held that the owner had not shown that its project had "special merit" under D.C. Code § 6-1102(11), as there was evidence that the project was not of the exceptional quality as the original landmark. Futhermore, there was substantial evidence that the project would not bring a significant social benefit. Finally, denial of the permits did not result in an unreasonable economic hardship under § 6-1102(14), which incorporated the Fifth Amendment's protection against the taking of property without just compensation. The property could be used in its original form, or the owner could propose a different design. The owner's investment-backed expectations were not reasonable, as it had taken a calculated risk in the face of concerns that the property might be designated as a historic landmark.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition to reverse the decision.

 

383 of 475 DOCUMENTS


District of Columbia v. Beretta U.S.A. Corp.


Nos. 06-CV-721, 06-CV-757


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


940 A.2d 163; 2008 D.C. App. LEXIS 4; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P17,899


November 20, 2007, Argued 
January 10, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Lawson v. Beretta U.S.A. Corp., 2009 U.S. LEXIS 1922 (U.S., Mar. 9, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CA-428-00). (Hon. Brook Hedge, Trial Judge).
District of Columbia v. Beretta U.S.A. Corp., 2006 D.C. Super. LEXIS 8 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, various individuals and the District of Columbia, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which dismissed their cause of action alleging negligence, creation of a public nuisance, and liability under the District of Columbia's Assault Weapons Manufacturing Strict Liability Act of 1990, D.C. Code § 7-2551.01 et seq., against defendants, firearm manufacturers, importers, and distributors.


OVERVIEW: The suit was dismissed pursuant to the terms of the Protection of Lawful Commerce in Arms Act (PLCAA), 15 U.S.C.S. § 7901 et seq., which required dismissal of pending claims against defendants when the claimed harm alleged was solely caused by the criminal or unlawful misuse of firearm products by others when the product functioned as designed and intended. The court upheld the dismissal of plaintiffs' cause of action because plaintiffs did not allege liability on the theory that defendants knowingly violated any proscriptions or requirements of local or federal law governing the sale or possession of firearms. Instead, plaintiffs argument was that the SLA, which by its terms would make defendants strictly liable in tort for death or injuries resulting from the discharge of an assault weapon or machine gun manufactured or sold, embodied a legal duty owed to the residents of the District and that its requirement to compensate for injuries thus presupposed a violation of a statutory duty. The court refused to allow an impermissible interpretation of the PLCAA that was not intended. Additionally, the court found no merit to plaintiffs' constitutional challenges to the PLCAA.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

384 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Dean v. United States


Nos. 98-CF-851 & 04-CO-186


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


938 A.2d 751; 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 701


June 14, 2005, Argued 
December 28, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected February 26, 2008.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Dean v. United States, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 4875 (U.S., June 29, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F-1104-97). (Hon. Frederick H. Weisberg, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia of murdering a member of the Metropolitan Police Department and other crimes. The court sentenced defendant to a mandatory term of life without the possibility of parole for the murder of the law enforcement officer. The court denied defendant's motion for a new trial under D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 33. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was removed from a nightclub when he became disorderly. The jury found that he then shot and killed a Metropolitan Police Department officer, who was on routine patrol in full uniform and in a marked police cruiser, while his patrol car was stopped at an intersection. On appeal the court found that (1) D.C. Code § 22-2406 (1995 Supp.) (recodified as D.C. Code § 22-2106 (2001)), the statute providing for mandatory life imprisonment without the possibility of parole for the murder of a law enforcement officer, did not unconstitutionally violate the guarantee of equal protection under the laws pursuant to the Fifth Amendment, nor was the punishment so disproportionate to the offense as to have violated the Eighth Amendment; (2) the trial judge, despite defendant's allegations of intoxication, did not err by finding that defendant's waiver of rights and confession were voluntary and in admitting defendant's confession at trial; and (3) the trial court did not impermissibly coerce the jury to render a verdict. Additionally, the trial court properly determined that it lacked jurisdiction over defendant's untimely motion for a new trial under D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 33.


OUTCOME: The convictions and the denial of the motion for a new trial were affirmed.

Parnigoni v. District of Columbia


No. 04-CT-1378


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


933 A.2d 823; 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 588


June 20, 2006, Argued 
October 11, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Parnigoni v. District of Columbia, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 8054 (U.S., Nov. 3, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (D-2773-03). (Hon. Erik P. Christian, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia found defendant guilty of two counts of indecent exposure in violation of D.C. Code § 22-1312 (2001). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was playing ping-pong with an 11-year-old child and suggested that whoever lost a game would have to play the next game naked. Defendant lost and took off all his clothes. The child's father walked in during the second game and saw defendant naked. On appeal, defendant argued that his convictions should be vacated because D.C. Code § 22-1312 (2001) was unconstitutionally vague. The appellate court disagreed, finding such an argument refuted by a longstanding line of cases. The appellate court also noted that a consent defense was barred by the statute because the victim was under the age of 16 when the events took place. Defendant also argued that there was insufficient evidence to support his conviction involving the father because there was no evidence he intended to expose himself to the father or that he actually exposed his genitalia to the father. The appellate court disagreed, noting that the specific intent to expose oneself to a particular person was not required, there was no question defendant intended to expose himself, and the father's testimony that defendant was facing him "completely naked" supported a finding that the father saw defendant's genitalia.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

September 6, 2007, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F1040-02). (Hon. Robert I. Richter, Trial Judge).

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia of first-degree child sexual abuse, in violation of D.C. Code § 22-3008 (2001). The court sentenced defendant to a term of incarceration, to be followed by a period of supervised release with the conditions that he register as a lifetime sex offender and undergo sex offender treatment. Defendant appealed.

OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Cook v. United States


No. 05-CO-1399


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


932 A.2d 506; 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 486


July 12, 2007, Submitted 
August 16, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F-2839-02). (Hon. Erik P. Christian, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted appellant of unlawful possession of heroin with intent to distribute. Appellant was sentenced to 144 months in prison followed by five years of supervised release. After the conviction was affirmed on direct appeal, appellant filed a motion to reduce sentence under D.C. Super. Ct. R. Crim. P. 35. The Superior Court of the District of Columbia denied the motion. Appellant challenged the decision.


OVERVIEW: The court stated that appellant's sentence fell well within the 30-year statutory maximum under D.C. Code § 48-904.01(a)(2)(A) (2001). Although appellant argued that had he received a "guideline sentence" under the current sentencing guidelines, he would have received a considerably lesser sentence, the voluntary sentencing guidelines had not been in effect when appellant was sentenced. Furthermore, as the guidelines were voluntary, they would not have compelled a lesser sentence. Accordingly, given the limited standard of review, the court could not say that the trial court abused its discretion in denying the motion. As for appellant's Eighth Amendment argument, he had not raised that argument on direct appeal and had not shown cause for his failure to do so. Furthermore, even if appellant's claim were considered on the merits, his sentence was not so grossly disproportionate to the crime as to violate the Eighth Amendment. It was well within the statutory maximum, and there were a number of aggravating circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant club member sued appellees, the club, club and club board members, and the club manager, in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, for violations of the District of Columbia Human Rights Act of 1977, D.C. Code § 2-1401.01 et seq. (2001 & 2006 Supp.); breach of the club's by-laws; failure to provide due process; breach of contract; and tortious acts. OVERVIEW: The club's board members concluded that the club member had used the club's facilities to conduct business with persons who publicly expounded racist and anti-Semitic views. During the investigation, the board members gave the club member written notice of the charges and gave him a copy of an investigative report, which included a summary of interviews that were conducted and served as the basis for the charges against him. District of Columbia v. Craig


No. 06-TX-177, No. 06-TX-178


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


930 A.2d 946; 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 458


January 12, 2007, Argued 
July 19, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected July 20, 2007.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CVT8112-02 & CVT8141-02). (Hon. Eugene N. Hamilton, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District of Columbia Superior Court Tax Division, in two certified class actions, granted summary judgment against appellant government representatives in cases where appellee taxpayers challenged property tax assessments regarding properties located in the District of Columbia. The government representatives appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court certified two class actions arising out of real estate tax assessments made against two groups of taxpayers. It also found the assessment method was not a "rule" that could be made subject to rule-making procedures.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgments were reversed and the cases were remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Franco v. Nat'l Capital Revitalization Corp.


No. 06-CV-645


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


930 A.2d 160; 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 398


April 12, 2007, Argued 
July 12, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Franco v. District of Columbia, 3 A.3d 300, 2010 D.C. App. LEXIS 500 (D.C., 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CA5335-05). (Hon. Geoffrey M. Alprin, Trial Judge).
Rumber v. Dist. of Columbia, 487 F.3d 941, 376 U.S. App. D.C. 255, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 12200 (2007)
Franco v. District of Columbia, 422 F. Supp. 2d 216, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 11552 (D.D.C., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee, the National Capital Revitalization Corporation (NCRC), initiated condemnation proceedings in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, against appellant owner's property pursuant to D.C. Code § 2-1219.19 (2001). The trial court granted the NCRC's motion to strike the owner's defenses under D.C. Super. Ct. R. Civ. P. 12(f), and granted the NCRC's motion for immediate possession of the property. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The NCRC was an independent instrumentality of the District of Columbia charged with several public purposes under D.C. Code § 2-1219.02(b) (2001). Pursuant to D.C. Super. Ct. R. Civ. P. 71A(e), the owner as an affirmative defense asserted primarily that the taking of his property violated the Takings Clause of the Fifth Amendment of the United States Constitution because the taking was for a private use and that the declared public purpose for the condemnation was a pretext. On appeal, the court found that it was appropriate for the court to exercise pendent appellate jurisdiction because the order granting immediate possession was properly before the court for review under D.C. Code § 11-721(a)(2)(C) (2001), and it was inextricably intertwined with the court's decision to strike the owner's defenses. The court concluded that the owner sufficiently pleaded a defense of pretext under D.C. Super. Ct. R. Civ. P. 8. Moreover, the pretext defense raised an issue requiring proof. That defense therefore had to be resolved on its merits, and could not be stricken for insufficient pleading.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for further consideration of the owner's pretext defense. The order granting the NCRC's motion for immediate possession was vacated, subject to a stay.

Landise v. Mauro


No. 04-CV-499


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


927 A.2d 1026; 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 321


January 16, 2007, Argued 
May 31, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by, Remanded by Landise v. Mauro, 2016 D.C. App. LEXIS 208 (D.C., June 30, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (No. CA2456-92). (Hon. Michael L. Rankin, Trial Judge).
Landise v. Mauro, 725 A.2d 445, 1998 D.C. App. LEXIS 217 (D.C., 1998)



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney sued appellee partner in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, for breach of a partnership agreement. After the trial court instructed the parties to select an auditor to conduct an accounting of partnership assets, the court ordered the attorney to make an additional deposit into the court registry pursuant to D.C. Code § 15-703(b) (2001). The attorney appealed. The partner filed a motion to dismiss the appeal.


OVERVIEW: The parties were colleagues in a law firm. Given the attorney's nonresident status, the trial court required the attorney to post security for costs pursuant to D.C. Code § 15-703(a) (2001). After the jury found liability in favor of the attorney, the trial court ordered an accounting of partnership assets. However, a dispute arose between the parties regarding the costs of the accounting. As a result of this dispute, the trial court required the attorney to pay an additional amount into the court registry pursuant to D.C. Code § 15-703(b) (2001). On appeal, the court found that the collateral order doctrine did not apply to the type of order at issue, which was, in effect, a discretionary decision by the trial court as opposed to a disputed question of law. Therefore, the trial court's ruling was the type of discretionary ruling that did not warrant immediate review. Further, the question whether the trial court abused its discretion was an issue that could await a determination with respect to damages, at which point, the issue may have become moot. Finally, security orders pursuant to D.C. Code § 15-703(b) were not subject to interlocutory appeal as injunctions.


OUTCOME: The appeal was dismissed.

In re Krouner


No. 04-BG-431


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


920 A.2d 1039; 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 159


January 25, 2007, Submitted  
April 12, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] (BDN 04-D141).
In re Krouner, 305 A.D.2d 932, 759 N.Y.S.2d 402, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6085 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, May 23, 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an attorney disciplinary action, the District of Columbia Bar Counsel issued a Report and Recommendation recommending that respondent attorney be disbarred under D.C. Code § 11-2503(a) (2001) based on his conviction of crimes of moral turpitude per se in New York, and that disbarment be deemed to commence for purposes of reinstatement nunc pro tunc to May 23, 2003. The attorney filed an exception to the recommendations.


OVERVIEW: In New York State, the attorney pled guilty to insurance fraud, grand larceny, and workers' compensation fraud, stemming from his falsely reporting his level of professional functioning in order to obtain increased disability benefits from his private insurance coverage. The appellate court first noted that D.C. Code § 11-2503(a) (2001) was constitutional and that the Americans with Disabilities Act did not shield the attorney from disbarment arising from convictions for crimes of moral turpitude. Next, the appellate court noted that because District of Columbia case law put the attorney on clear notice that conviction for crimes of moral turpitude per se would result in automatic disbarment, the attorney could not claim ignorance of the potential consequences of his conviction. Finally, the appellate court concluded that the attorney's convictions involved moral turpitude, as all required the element of intentional theft or a specific intent to defraud.


OUTCOME: The attorney was disbarred from the practice of law in the District of Columbia as of May 23, 2003, the date of the attorney's good-faith attempt to comply with the bar rules.

Ottenberg's Bakers, Inc. v. D.C. Comm'n on Human Rights


No. 01-AA-669


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


917 A.2d 1094; 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 98; 100 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 411


March 5, 2002, Argued  
March 8, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Review of a Decision of the District of Columbia Commission on Human Rights. (P-326-95).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer petitioned for review of a decision of the District of Columbia Human Rights Commission awarding intervenor former employee damages and other relief found to have been caused by the wrongful termination of his employment.


OVERVIEW: The employer argued that the Commission's finding of discrimination was not supported by substantial evidence or in accordance with applicable law. As the findings upon which the Commission relied were supported by substantial evidence in the record, the appeals court had to accept its decision that the reasons given by the employer for its action were a pretext, even though a different one might have been reached on the same record. There was a rational basis for the Commission to find that the employer's explanation was not worthy of credence. When considered with the employee's prima facie case and other evidence cited by the Commission supporting its finding of liability, the appeals court could not conclude that the Commission erred in finding the employer liable for unlawful discrimination. Under the totality of the circumstances, a finding that the employee's rejection of a position was objectively reasonable was not supported by the evidence. Therefore, the employee's rejection of the employer's unconditional offer of the position of Regional Manager eliminated any claim for back-pay or front-pay on or after March 22, 1995, the date he rejected the employer's offer.


OUTCOME: The Commission's finding of discrimination was sustained, its award of damages was reversed, and the case was remanded to the Commission for further proceedings consistent with the opinion.

Agomo v. Fenty


No. 03-CV-813


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


916 A.2d 181; 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 11; 26 A.L.R.6th 767


May 12, 2005, Argued  
February 1, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CA-6520-02). (Hon. Melvin R. Wright, Trial Judge).
Agomo v. Williams, 2003 D.C. Super. LEXIS 31 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the vehicle owner and the leasing corporation, sued appellee District of Columbia in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, alleging that the Automated Traffic Enforcement System (ATE System) established by D.C. Code § 50-2209.01 (2001) et seq. violated the Fifth Amendment guarantees of due process. The trial court granted summary judgment to the District. The owner and the corporation appealed.


OVERVIEW: The ATE System, developed pursuant to D.C. Code § 50-2209.01, detected moving violations under the District's traffic laws through the use of photographs taken by automated cameras installed at various locations throughout the District. The owner and the corporation argued that the method of assessing liability preliminarily to the registered owner of a car conflicted with the statutory framework in D.C. Code § 50-2302.06(a), which required that the District prove a moving violation by clear and convincing evidence, and that the presumption of liability on the registered owner of a car under D.C. Code § 50-2209.02 violated their due process rights. They also argued that the compensation arrangement with a private corporation for administrative aspects of the ATE System under D.C. Code § 50-2209.03 violated their due process rights by creating an adjudicatory tribunal that was tainted by financial considerations. On appeal, the court found that neither the statutory mechanism for imposition of liability nor the compensation arrangement of the ATE System violated due process. Therefore, the grant of summary judgment for the District was proper.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

395 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Potts v. United States


NOS. 05-CM-781, 05-CM-867 & 05-CM-928


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


919 A.2d 1127; 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 153


January 23, 2007, Argued  
January 30, 2007, * Decided


* The decision in these consolidated appeals originally was released as an unpublished Memorandum Opinion and Judgment. On motion of appellee filed February 28, 2007, we now reissue the opinion for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (M-1206-05, M-1207-05, & M-1208-05). (Hon. Patricia A. Wynn, Trial Judge).
Potts, 916 A.2d 198, 2007 D.C. App. LEXIS 31 (D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted after a bench trial in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia of violating 40 U.S.C.S. § 6135 by displaying, on the United States Supreme Court grounds, a banner or device designed or adapted to bring into public notice a party, organization, or movement. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were at the United States Supreme Court as part of a small group protesting the mistreatment of prisoners at the Abu Ghraib and Guantanamo Bay prisons and the appointment of a United States Attorney General. To dramatize their cause, one defendant wore a black hood; a second defendant wore an orange jumpsuit and black hood; and the third defendant held a sign over his head that read "no taxes for war or torture." Defendants ascended several steps from the sidewalk to the plaza in front of the United States Supreme Court building. On appeal, the court found that (1) jurisdiction in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia was proper because 40 U.S.C.S. § 6137(b) provided that violations of 40 U.S.C.S. § 6135 could be prosecuted in the Superior Court as well as in United States District Court; (2) 40 U.S.C.S. § 6135 did not violate the First Amendment, either on its face or as applied to defendants; (3) the display clause of 40 U.S.C.S. § 6135 did not suffer from overbreadth or vagueness; and (4) the trial court did not misconstrue the statute or clearly err in finding that defendants' costumes were devices designed or adapted to bring into public notice a movement.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed.

Thomas v. United States


No. 03-CF-1125


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


914 A.2d 1; 2006 D.C. App. LEXIS 655


February 1, 2005, Argued  
December 28, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected January 5, 2007.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Thomas v. United States, 552 U.S. 895, 128 S. Ct. 241, 169 L. Ed. 2d 160, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 9904 (2007)
Post-conviction proceeding at United States v. Inyangette, 446 Fed. Appx. 634, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 19100 (4th Cir. Va., 2011)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Inyangette v. Drew, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 138404 (D.S.C., Feb. 16, 2012)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Iyangette v. Bragg, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 183181 (D.S.C., May 7, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F-5011-02). (Hon. Rafael Diaz, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, which convicted appellant for distributing cocaine.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's conviction arose from an arranged buy and bust operation. At appellant's trial, the prosecution introduced a Drug Enforcement Administration chemist's written expert testimony against appellant without calling the chemist to appear and testify in person. Appellant asserted on appeal that his Sixth Amendment right to confrontation was violated by the admission without calling the chemist to appear and testify in person. The chemist's written statement was offered in evidence, pursuant to D.C. Code § 48-905.06 (2001), and its admission conformed to the then-prevailing understanding of the Confrontation Clause and the statute. However, the court agreed that the Confrontation Clause barred the prosecution from introducing the report without calling the chemist to testify in person, unless the chemist was unavailable and appellant had a prior opportunity to cross-examine the chemist, which neither of the two latter prerequisites were met. The court agreed that plain error occurred but that it did not seriously affect the fairness, integrity, or public reputation of the judicial proceeding to merit reversal of appellant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction.

 

397 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Boyd v. United States


Nos. 98-CF-862 and 00-CO-558


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


908 A.2d 39; 2006 D.C. App. LEXIS 535


May 10, 2005, Argued; February 24, 2006, Reargued  
September 28, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F2266-97). (Hon. Mary Ellen Abrecht, Trial Judge).
Watson v. United States, 881 A.2d 1129, 2005 D.C. App. LEXIS 480 (D.C., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his convictions by a jury in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia of first-degree murder, felony murder, kidnapping, attempted kidnapping, conspiracy, and related firearms offenses.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that his prosecution on the basis of a factual theory allegedly different from, and irreconcilable with, the factual theory relied on by the government to secure the conviction of defendant's former codefendant deprived defendant of his liberty without due process of law, in violation of the Fifth Amendment, and that the government violated its obligations pursuant to Brady by failing to provide him with material exculpatory statements by prospective witnesses. The appeals court found that although the prosecutor should have revealed to the jury in defendant's case that the government's factual theory had changed on a material issue from the position the government took at the codefendant's trial, defendant was not entitled to reversal on that ground, for the inconsistency in the government's theory did not go to the core of its case. Further, the potentially exculpatory statement of at least one prospective witness who had been interviewed by the police should have been provided to the defense, and the trial judge abused her discretion in declining to review, in camera, two other allegedly exculpatory statements.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the opinion. If the trial judge concluded, after the parties had an opportunity to be heard, that the withholding of the statements of five witnesses, individually or collectively, violated defendant's rights, the judge was to order a new trial. If the judge concluded the statements were not material, then the convictions would stand, subject to the right to appeal.

 

398 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Threatt v. Winston


No. 04-CV-191, No. 04-CV-545


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


907 A.2d 780; 2006 D.C. App. LEXIS 511


March 29, 2006, Argued  
September 21, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (CA7462-01). (Hon. Mary A. Gooden Terrell, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former tenant sued appellee landlord in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, for wrongful eviction, breach of the covenant of quiet enjoyment, and conversion of personal property. The trial court granted the landlord's motion to dismiss. The tenant appealed.


OVERVIEW: After a dispute arose about the tenant's alleged failure to pay rent to the landlord, the landlord filed a complaint for possession. The Landlord and Tenant Branch of the Superior Court of the District of Columbia entered a default judgment granting possession of the apartment to the landlord after the tenant failed to appear or defend. The tenant did not seek to vacate the judgment against him, nor did he appeal the judgment. Instead, more than a year later, he filed his civil complaint against the landlord. The tenant argued that he should not have been precluded from bringing his wrongful eviction action because the previous judgment was void for lack of personal jurisdiction. On appeal, the court found that, having allowed the time for appeal to expire, the tenant could attack the default judgment, if he wanted to avoid its preclusive effect, only by filing a motion to vacate under D.C. Super. Ct. R. Civ. P. 60(b)(4), or by filing the type of independent action to vacate the judgment authorized by D.C. Super. Ct. R. Civ. P. 60(b). Thus, the trial court did not err in ruling that the doctrine of res judicata barred the tenant's complaint for wrongful eviction.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

399 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Hairston v. United States


Nos. 00-CF-1045 and 03-CO-417


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


905 A.2d 765; 2006 D.C. App. LEXIS 484


November 3, 2004, Argued  
August 17, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hairston v. United States, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 11896 (U.S., Oct. 29, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeals from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (F7749-97). (Hon. Ann O'Regan Keary, Trial Judge).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted after a jury trial in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia of, among other crimes, conspiracy to assault and murder members of a rival neighborhood faction, D.C. Code §§ 22-105a, -501, -2401 (1996) (recodified at D.C. Code § 22-1805a, -401, -2101 (2000)) and first-degree murder while armed, D.C. Code § 22-2401 (recodified at D.C. Code § 22-2101). He filed a timely direct appeal, and a timely collateral appeal.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals found that the trial court did not violate the Fifth Amendment by denying defendant's motion to suppress his confession, where his interrogation was in two phases, before and after Miranda warnings. A detective elicited none of the details from defendant in phase one that emerged in phase two, and it was clear that the Miranda warnings as administered would meaningfully apprise a reasonable suspect of the right or choice to remain silent, and thus were effective. The court did not err in refusing to hold a hearing on his D.C. Code § 23-110 motion, as defendant presented no affidavit in support of his allegations, and the trial court found his assertions regarding trial counsel and defendant's statement to the police "palpably false." Moreover, defendant raised some new allegations on appeal which had not been preserved, and hence, would not be considered on appeal. A rational trier of fact could have found the elements of conspiracy beyond a reasonable doubt, as the evidence revealed that defendant and two others discussed and agreed to exact revenge against persons in another neighborhood block because of actions and words by two members of that faction.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

400 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Shepherd v. United States


No. 04-CM-51


DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA COURT OF APPEALS


905 A.2d 260; 2006 D.C. App. LEXIS 477


November 8, 2005, Submitted  
August 10, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Shepherd v. United States, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 3787 (U.S., Apr. 2, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the District of Columbia. (M-2987-03). (Hon. Ronald P. Wertheim, Trial Judge).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a bench trial in the Superior Court of the District of Columbia, defendant was convicted of attempted cruelty to animals, D.C. Code §§ 22-1001, 22-1803 (2001) and of two counts of assault, D.C. Code § 22-404 (2001). He had been charged under the Bias-Related Crimes Statute, D.C. Code §§ 22-3701, 22-3703 (2001). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: When passing by some women walking their dogs, defendant hit two of the women, called them "dykes," and yelled obscenities at them. He allegedly tried to kick one of the dogs. At trial, defendant admitted yelling anti-lesbian insults at the women but denied kicking the dog. The court first held that the evidence sustained the conviction of attempted cruelty to animals. The trial court had found no reason to doubt the credibility of the Government's witnesses, and it had doubted defendant's testimony that he was merely trying to push the small dog away. Next, defendant argued that the bias statute was unconstitutional. Reviewing the argument for plain error because it had not been raised before the trial court, the court stated that it was defendant's assaultive conduct motivated by bias, not his homophobia as such, that had been subjected to criminal sanction. There was no vagueness in the statute as it applied to defendant, given his evident animus against the women's sexual orientation. Moreover, application of the bias statute had not violated defendant's substantial rights because his sentence had not been enhanced beyond that available for simple assault.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction.
